## Introduction[*] 

## Teresa Fanego 

University of Santiago de Compostela 

The Eleventh International Conference on English Historical Linguistics (11 ICEHL) was held at the University of Santiago de Compostela between 7th and 11th September 2000. The number of participants exceeded 250, while the papers delivered within the conference’s main programme came to 120. The distinguished panel of plenary speakers featured Douglas Biber, Laurel J. Brinton, Santiago González Fernández-Corugedo, Raymond Hickey, Chris B. McCully, Frans Plank, Irma Taavitsainen, Ingrid Tieken-Boon van Ostade and Anthony Warner. There were also several events running concurrently with the main programme, notably a workshop on historical word-formation, a parasession on electronic corpora and a poster session. 

This volume is a companion to another one also containing papers from the same conference: _English Historical Syntax and Morphology. Selected Papers from 11 ICEHL, Santiago de Compostela, 7–11 September 2000_ , edited by Teresa Fanego, María José López-Couso and Javier Pérez-Guerra (CILT 223). The two volumes together offer a representative sample of the contributions presented at the conference, including some of those delivered during the workshop on historical word-formation. The papers that have survived the successive selection procedures for presentation and publication[1] quite accurately reflect the various concerns of English historical linguistics at the turn of the millennium and the different methodologies applied to address them. Largely for this reason, we have made no attempt to organize the papers thematically and have simply presented them in alphabetical order. However, so as to give the reader some preliminary idea of what this volume has to offer, we will give a brief summary of the main issues in each individual paper. 

Although most papers in one way or another address theoretical and methodological issues, in some papers theoretical aspects are placed in the foreground more than in others. A case in point is Hickey’s “Ebb and Flow: A Cautionary Tale 

2 Teresa Fanego 

of Language Change”. The article’s title alludes to the fact that the trajectory along which linguistic change proceeds within a community is not always unilinear: a change can move in one direction and then reverse its course, as happened with the vowel in the trap lexical set in British English. Early twentieth-century descriptions of British English pronunciation record a raised realization of the vowel, but by the middleof the twentieth century this trendwas reversed and there is currently a noticeable tendency for the lowering of the vowel. Such shifts of transmission across generations, which Hickey refers to collectively as ‘ebb and flow’, have important implicationsfor thespecial statusaccorded insociolinguistic studiesto so-called ‘remnant communities’ (Wolfram & Schilling-Estes 2002), that is, communitiesthatforreasonsofgeographicalisolationareusuallyassumedtostill embody a stage of a language which has long been superseded in other more central, urban areas of the language in question. As Hickey notes, one cannot automatically assume that feature values in the remnant community which differ from feature values in more central areas have been transmitted unchanged throughout history: a contemporary realization of a feature may be similar to a first input, but not necessarily a direct reflex of this if ebb and flow has taken place. 

Another paper with a sociolinguistic orientation is Bax’s “Linguistic Accommodation: The Correspondence between Samuel Johnson and Hester Lynch Thrale”. This is a pilot study testing the applicability of Communication Accommodation Theory (CAT; cf. Giles et al. 1987) to historical sociolinguistics. CAT was originally developed in the context of present-day sociopragmatics to analyse face-to-face conversations and the ways in which speakers adapt their language and communication towards others. Bax stretches the concepts of speaker/listener and applies the model to the analysis of historical correspondence, as illustrated by the correspondence between Samuel Johnson (1709–1784) and his close friend Hester Lynch Thrale (1741–1821). He discusses three types of accommodation: accommodation through content, lexical convergence and syntactic convergence. Lexical convergence is examined in terms of the ratio of Latinate polysyllables employed by each of the two correspondents, while the criterion used to test syntactic convergence is the ratio of paratactic vs. hypotactic constructions. Bax shows that Johnson converged to Thrale’s colloquial style by using simple, paratactic structures and by refraining from his heavy Ramblerian, Latinate diction. Thrale, for her part, converged to Johnson’s writing chiefly through the content of her letters, but she did not converge lexically, nor did she adopt a syntactically more complex style. These differences in accommodative behaviour are accounted for by Bax with reference to some of the principles of Accommodation Theory. 

Introduction 

3 

The papers by Durkin, Kay & Wotherspoon, Lutz, Vennemann, and Culpeper & Kytö (for this last paper see further below), testify to the continued interest in lexis and semantics among English historical linguists. In “Changing Documentation in the Third Edition of the _Oxford English Dictionary_ : Sixteenth-century Vocabulary as a Test Case” Durkin examines the implications that the complete revision of the _OED_ now in progress has for studies attempting to assess the nature and extent of the accessions to the English language in a given period. Taking as a sample items with a sixteenth-century first date in _OED2_ or _OED3_ , he shows some of the possible pitfalls in making uncritical use of dictionary data, especially where such data is to be used for statistical purposes. Durkin also discusses changes in the etymological component of the _OED_ , which is also the central topic of Vennemann’s “ _Key_ Issues in English Etymology”. He is concerned with unetymologized English words, i.e. words whose etymologies in the _OED_ end with statements such as “of obscure origin” or “derivation uncertain”. In all, there are some 4,696 entries characterized in this way, and Vennemann argues that comparing such words to Basque and Semitic can lead to fruitful new etymologies and can also throw light on the stratal, cultural relationships between English or Germanic, on the one hand, and Basque and Semitic on the other. The word _key_ in the title of the paper, whose etymology the _OED_ qualifies as “unknown” but which Vennemann traces back to Vasconic, is a case in point. 

Kay & Wotherspoon (“Wreak, Wrack, Rack and (W)ruin: The History of Some Confused Spellings”) examine changes between ·r-Ò and ·wr-Ò spellings, as in the unetymological _wrack_ for _rack_ ‘instrument of torture’. This occurs considerably later than the actual simplification of the cluster /wr-/ in the history of English and thus involves the addition of a silent letter, despite the trend towards spelling pronunciations evident in English since the sixteenth century (Scragg 1974:55). Since a high proportion of the English words beginning with ·wr-Ò have meanings with components of torment, distortion or discomfort (witness _wreak_ , _wreck_ , _wrench_ , _wring_ , _writhe_ or _wrought_ , among others), Kay & Wotherspoon suggest that their semantic and visual associations may have encouraged the movement from _rack_ to _wrack_ , or that from _rapt_ to _wrapt_ . On the model of _phonaesthesia_ and _phonaestheme_ (cf. Samuels 1972:46) they propose the terms _graphaesthesia_ and _graphaestheme_ respectively to designate the phenomenon underlying such semantically motivated spellings with ·wr-Ò and the group ·wr-Ò itself. 

In another paper on lexis (“When Did English Begin?”), Lutz addresses the vexed question of the periodization of English and argues that the conventional 

4 Teresa Fanego 

tripartite division into Old, Middle and Modern English, which is based on the degree of morphological synthesis, cannot be applied to the lexical development of the language. By carefully considering a selection of texts (such as LaŠamon’s _Brut_ or _The Owl and the Nightingale_ ) that are otherwise considered to exemplify the so-called Early Middle English period, she shows that, whether one makes 1100 the dividing line between Old English and (Early) Middle English (see eg. Hogg 1992:9), or brings it forward to around 1200, as proposed by Henry Sweet (1892:211)[2] and, more recently, Kitson (1997:250), both from a lexical and a cultural point of view, ‘Saxon’ English ended long after Middle English as the period of levelled inflexions began. For the lexicon, she therefore proposes a separate, bipartite periodization distinguishing Anglo-Saxon (a period of essentially Germanic vocabulary comprising Old and Early Middle English) from English (a period of heavily gallicized vocabulary comprising all later stages). 

Several of the issues discussed by Lutz are taken up by Scahill in “Dan Michel: Fossil or Innovator?”. His analysis of the language of the _Ayenbite of Inwyt_ (1340) reveals a text showing the integration of native and foreign components at the level of orthography, but otherwise offering a striking mixture of lexical innovation (with a substantial number of exotic borrowings from French) and grammatical conservatism (with very few signs of morphological transition). Thus, though we tend to think of the transition from Old English to Early Middle English as characterized by morphological collapse, in the case of the _Ayenbite_ the transition affects the morphology last, such that it belongs in the later of the two periods distinguished by Lutz (see above) lexically, but early in the Middle English period morphologically. This confirms that different modules of language — including orthography, as Scahill argues — can change at different rates cross-dialectally; in the particular case of Middle English, “externally-driven lexical change and internally-driven morphological change proceeded rather independently” (p.197). 

Another area of research that found favour at the 11th ICEHL was text types and genres. Four of the papers in this line — by Taavitsainen, Görlach, Claridge & Wilson, and Culpeper & Kytö — appear in this volume. Taavitsainen (“Historical Discourse Analysis: Scientific Language and Changing ThoughtStyles”) looks at the evolution of medical discourse from Late Middle English to the Royal Society period. Starting from the hypothesis that the different styles of thinking represented by scholasticism and empiricism will be reflected in the linguistic repertoire of medical texts, she examines evidential features, especifically the use of speech act verbs of reporting ( _say_ , _tell_ ) as opposed to ‘private’ verbs (cf. Quirk et al. 1985:§4.29) of processes that can only be 

Introduction 

5 

subjectively verified ( _know_ , _think_ ). The former are found to be an important feature of scholastic writings, which are logocentric and rely on axioms (“X says/claims that Y”). By contrast, empirical science relies on sensory evidence, hence verbs of observation and cognitive processes become prominent, and there is also an overall shift to a more subjective, involved way of writing marked by the use of first person pronouns and past tense verbs. Görlach (“A Linguistic History of Advertising, 1700–1890”), in turn, is concerned with the development of the modern commercial advertisement as a text type. He considers in detail the linguistic components (vocabulary, formulaic expressions, syntax, etc.) of early advertisements, and also extralinguistic parameters such as the topics advertised, the audience addressed or the role of non-verbal constituents, especially illustrations. 

Like Görlach, Claridge & Wilson focus on the modern period. In “Style Evolution in the English Sermon” they look at how sermons as a text type have changed linguistically from the seventeenth century to the present day, using Biber’s factor analysis (1988) and concentrating on three of the factors or dimensions proposed by Biber, namely 1 (Involved vs. Informational Production), 4 (Overt Expression of Persuasion) and 6 (On-Line Informational Elaboration). Sermon style appears to have remained constant over time with respect to both factors 4 and 6, while there is a general tendency for sermons to exhibit greater involvement. Overall, however, the stylistic evolution in sermons seems to be more closely tied to linguistic distinctions which, for the most part, do not figure on Biber’s factor scales, such as the formality of vocabulary (Anglo-Saxon vs. Latinate), sentence length, or the use of rhetorical figures. Claridge & Wilson thus suggest that a full account of the evolution of sermon style would involve making use of more features than are available on Biber’s dimensions.[3] 

Finally, in “Lexical Bundles in Early Modern English Dialogues: A Window into the Speech-related Language of the Past”, Culpeper & Kytö examine lexical bundles[4] — recurrent word-combinations that commonly go together in natural discourse — such as _in order to_ , _you know what_ or _the fact that._ Occurrences of these and similar combinations in the Corpus of English Dialogues (1560–1760) are organized by Culpeper & Kytö into broad functional categories modelled on the three functional dimensions — ideational, interpersonal and textual — recognized by systemic-functional grammar (Halliday 1994). This enables them to show the grammatical properties of the different classes of lexical bundles, their relation to particular pragmatic and discoursal functions, and how they distribute across the texts examined, as well as how they compare 

6 Teresa Fanego 

with those word-combinations identified in studies of present-day spoken and written English. 

This brings us to the last two papers in the volume: Williamson’s “The Dialectology of ‘English’ North of the Humber, c. 1380–1500” and McCully’s “What’s Afoot with Word-final C? Metrical Coherence and the History of English”. Williamson’s paper applies the methods of historical dialectology to the analysis of Older Scots and Northern Middle English during the late fourteenth and fifteenth centuries. Up to the fifteenth century these two language labels are used to distinguish from a geopolitical viewpoint what is perceived as a common speech area, with actual linguistic divergence between Lowland Scotland and Northern England starting roughly from the fifteenth century. Williamson uses data drawn from _A Linguistic Atlas of Late Mediaeval English_ and the Edinburgh Corpus of Older Scots to reveal patterns of phonological and morphological variation in Scots and Northern English texts. He shows that, in addition to literary texts, local documents, such as record books and charters, constitute important sources of evidence for dialectology studies because they are usually datable and can be localized. 

McCully’s contribution, like Hickey’s discussed at the beginning of these pages, is primarily theoretical. He examines recent work in generative phonology that accounts for the distribution and development of stress in Old English, Old High German and some of the Nordic languages by appealing to the socalled Germanic foot (Dresher & Lahiri 1991; Lahiri, Riad & Jacobs 1999) and argues that evidence for the postulation of the Germanic foot is problematic. First, iterative assignment of such feet over-generates and leads to merely mechanical destressing, thus complicating the grammar. Secondly, the Germanic foot would be a specific addition to a universal foot inventory and thus suspicious. Moreover, the Germanic foot crucially relies on interactions with extrametricality in order to play an interesting role in phonological processes (which interactions are largely stipulative). McCully attempts to show how the Germanic foot might be replaced by the moraic trochee, and sketches the consequences of such a move for the organization of the stress phonology of Old English (in particular), modelling those consequences in optimalitytheoretic terms. More generally, McCully’s discussion of right-edge effects traditionally handled under the rubric of extraprosodicity contributes to the ongoing debate concerning the explanatory power of rule-based and constraintbased phonological frameworks. 

We would like to close this brief introduction by thanking the many people and institutions that helped to make the 11th ICEHL a success. Among the 

Introduction 

7 

former, we are grateful to all those who delivered papers, as well as to the several academics who helped us in the difficult task of selecting from the large number of abstracts submitted the contributions that were accepted for presentation at the conference. Our thanks also to the students who collaborated with the Organizing Committee both before and during the conference. Sponsorship was gratefully received from the Xunta de Galicia (Secretaría Xeral de Investigación e Desenvolvemento and Dirección Xeral de Turismo), the Spanish Ministry of Education and Culture, the University of Santiago de Compostela, the Department of English, the City of Santiago de Compostela, the British Council, the Spanish Association for Canadian Studies (AEEC), and the Fundación Pedro Barrié de la Maza.

## Linguistic accommodation 

The correspondence between Samuel Johnson and Hester Lynch Thrale[*] 

Randy C. Bax University of Leiden 

## 1. Introduction 

One course of action open to the historical linguist has been to resort to models that are used in modern linguistics in an effort to determine their applicability to historical data, a procedure which may lead to insights that, in turn, have implications for modern linguistics. For example, linguists attending the Tenth International Conference on English Historical Linguistics (Manchester, August 1998) demonstrated both the value and limitations of Milroy & Milroy’s social network approach (L. Milroy 1980) for the investigation of earlier stages of the English language (see e.g. Bax 2000; Tieken-Boon van Ostade 2000). The present paper serves as a starting-point from which to explore the potential of the Communication Accommodation Theory, or CAT (Giles et al. 1987; Coupland & Giles 1988), from the viewpoint of socio-historical linguistics. In the light of the prediction that CAT may be applicable across a very wide range of media such as electronic mail (Giles et al. 1991:4), it will try to show that some of its components are indeed valuable to the analysis of reciprocal correspondence. It will concentrate on one specific type of historical data, eighteenth-century correspondence, and on the letters and the relationship of two correspondents in particular, one famous, the other at best well-known: Samuel Johnson (1709–1784) and his close friend Hester Lynch Thrale (later Piozzi) (1741–1821). The types of accommodation that will be considered are accommodation through content, lexical convergence and syntactic convergence. 

As the author of _A Dictionary of the English Language_ (1755), Johnson needs no introduction; he was, Thrale wrote in her diary, one of the “best known 

10 Randy C. Bax 

Characters in London — perhaps in Europe” (Balderston 1951:I, 495). Johnson met Thrale and her husband in January 1765 (McAdam et al. 1958:84). Almost instantly attracted to each other, the Thrales and their guest quickly arranged that he would dine with them every Thursday. Mrs. Thrale soon must have seen in Johnson a substitute for her old mentor, Dr. Arthur Collier (1707–1777), who had been her classics tutor until 1763 (Clifford 1968:56). Showing a similar appreciation for her literary interests, Johnson set her to work writing and, as Brownley (1985:625) points out, “the enormous intellectual stimulation Johnson gave to her reinforced her tendencies towards literary pursuits”. After suffering from a nervous breakdown in 1766, Johnson took the opportunity to recover at Streatham Park, the Thrales’ country estate in Surrey. The Thrales gave him his own room and he soon became an adopted family member (Clifford 1968:64–65). 

Most of Johnson’s letters to Thrale have survived; according to Tom Keymer (1997:224), 373 in total. Most of Thrale’s letters have, unfortunately, perished. About a hundred have survived, and Chapman suggests that perhaps as many are lost (1952:III, 297–298). I have tried to include as many messageand-response strings as possible and to interpret the findings within the accommodation framework. The electronic corpus compiled for the purpose of this paper consequently consists of 47 letters written by Thrale to Johnson (12,458 words) and 54 letters that Johnson wrote to her (15,103 words). For Thrale’s letters I have resorted to Chapman’s edition. Johnson’s letters were, for practical reasons, taken from both the editions of Redford (1992) and Chapman (1952). It should be noted that although the relationship between the two correspondents started in 1765, lasting until Johnson’s death in 1784, apparently no letters have survived that Thrale wrote to Johnson before 1770. In his last days, Johnson destroyed almost all of his private papers, and we know that he burned an unknown number of letters that Thrale had sent him (Riely 1984:80–81). 

## 2. CAT and the analysis of historical reciprocal correspondence 

The Accommodation Theory was originally developed to analyse face-to-face conversations. Named Speech Accommodation Theory (SAT), it deals with motivations underlying and consequences that are the result of ways in which speakers adapt their language and communication towards others (Giles 1973), that is, “linguistic variation that is occasioned by who a particular speaker is addressing”(Coupland&Jaworski1997:229).Thebroaderlabel,Communication 

Linguistic accommodation 11 

Accommodation Theory (CAT), emerged much later in 1987 (Giles et al. 1987), and covers aspects of communication other than those of speech, such as constancy of gaze and frequency of gestures (cf. Street 1991:137). Accommodation towards the language use of one’s addressee is known as _convergence_ while accommodation away from it is called _divergence_ . As increasing behavioural similarity is likely to increase, among other things, a person’s attractiveness and interpersonal involvement in the eyes of the recipient, one of the model’s central predictions is that convergence reflects the need for social approval: “the greater the speakers’ need to gain another’s social approval, the greater the degree of convergence there will be” (Giles et al. 1991:18–19). Speakers have been found to converge and diverge with respect to various aspects of language, e.g. phonological variants, utterance length, speech rate, word frequency, information density, self-disclosure and conversational topics, i.e. content convergence (Giles et al. 1991). (See Giles et al. 1991 for an elaborate discussion of accommodation theory.) 

The concepts of speaker and listener in face-to-face conversations were themselves stretched by Bell, who applied CAT to the speaker-audience relationship in mass communication, the latter being structurally different from face-to-face interaction, because it involves “a disjunction of place, and often also of time, between communicator and audience [and] most media content is also not ad lib speech, but scripted in whole or in part” (Bell 1991:70,72). The socio-historical linguist needs little imagination to see how these two factors can be related to eighteenth-century correspondence: writers are usually separated by place and time, and ‘scripted’ is easily associated with the standard recommendation found in early modern and eighteenth-century manuals for letterwriters, namely that letters should be “especially spontaneous” and “comparable to conversation” (Biester 1988:151–152). What makes accommodation theory furthermore interesting to the study of historical correspondences is that the latter are, so to speak, completed interactions, with a beginning and an end, and often stretching out over a period of several years. Ferrara (1991:217) has suggested that the true extent of accommodation “may only emerge with studies of extended contacts such as is possible in longitudinal studies”. In the case of the Johnson-Thrale correspondence one is dealing with nearly two decades. 

When attempting to interpret written communicative behaviour, one should not only take account of the differences existing between written and spoken communication (see Nystrand 1986) but also be familiar with the range of social norms, or “socially shared ideas about appropriate and inappropriate behavior” (Gallois & Callan 1991:249), that were operative at the time of 

12 Randy C. Bax 

writing. Gallois & Callan argue that “norms put constraints of varying degree and strength on the accommodative moves that are perceived as desirable in an interaction” and that “it is important to identify those situations whose norms are likely to constrain strongly the accommodative moves that are available” (1991:253). Because eighteenth-century polite correspondence was subject to particular normative constraints, any accommodation-based analysis would have to take these into account. For example, one needs to distinguish the public from the private mode, as letters were commonly read by, and read to, other people than the recipient (Anderson & Ehrenpreis 1966:274). These alternating modes may have caused shifts in linguistic accommodation in letters addressed to the same correspondent, because a letter-writer would presumably have been less accommodative, or accommodative in different ways, in public as opposed to private letters. Interestingly, as Redford points out, “it is the Johnson of the letters to Hester Thrale who exemplifies (…) the widest possible divorce between ex cathedra and sub rosa selves” (i.e. between public and private selves) and, except for a few letters that were written to her in the public mode of address, Johnson does not retreat behind “the mask of forum” but, on the contrary, incurs “the risk of continuous self-revelation” (1986:206–208). 

Unfamiliarity with contemporary norms may lure the investigator into taking a correspondent’s exploitation of norms at face value. If my interpretation is correct, Johnson exploits the conventional salutation (see Baker 1980:45–51 on the anatomy of the eighteenth-century letter) in order to maintain rapport when he ends a letter to Thrale, stating “I am, Dearest of all dear Ladies, Your servant and slave, and admirer, and honourer, Samuel Johnson” (Redford 1992:II, 195). Taking Johnson’s words at face value, one would draw incorrect conclusions about the nature of his accommodative behaviour toward Thrale, namely that he considered himself to be inferior to her and that mentioning his inferior status was simply the proper thing for him to do; one would perhaps be inclined to interpret these words as a confirmation of Johnson’s alleged masochistic behaviour (Balderston 1949). Furthermore, letter-writers typically did not simply write down whatever sprang to mind, but they worked hard to achieve this effect. As Redford (1986:206) explains, “men and women of letters perfected a public voice (…) Almost without exception the mask of the forum prepares us to some degree for the face of the closet”. Johnson mocks this convention when he writes in a letter to Thrale: “Is not my soul laid open in these veracious pages? (…) This is the pleasure of corresponding with a friend, where doubt and distrust have no place, and everything is said as it is thought” (Redford 1992:III, 89–90). 

Linguistic accommodation 13 

## 2.1 Accommodation through content: Allusions 

Redford (1986) points out that, as the Thrale-Johnson correspondence matured, Johnson began to apply the device of literary allusion to convey messages he knew Thrale would immediately understand. He created webs of allusion with quotations and references that gave expression to his emotions and attitudes. It is not easy to identify every single case, but one can see that in the course of time Thrale adopted Johnson’s technique in her letters to him, which can be interpreted as an example of accommodation through content (Ferrara 1991:216); by adopting Johnson’s style of writing, she made her usage more similar to his. It should be noted that she did this in spite of the many domestic worries — deaths, illnesses, debts — that often preoccupied her, giving her little opportunity to compose. This is the reason why her letters would never contain the great number of allusions that she could find in his. For example, in Johnson’s letters we come across allusions to Swift with which he articulated “lines of connection” (Redford 1986:237–238), as in (1) and (2): 

- (1) You did not tell me in your last letter how Mr. Thrale goes on. If he will be _ruled for aught appears he may live on these hundred years_ (Redford 1992: III, 246; allusion to Swift, pointed out in fn.9.) 

- (2) Now I know you want to be forgetting me, but I do not want to be forgotten, and would rather send you letters like _Presto’s_ , than suffer myself to slip out of your memory (Redford 1992:I, 322; allusion to Swift, pointed out in fn.1.) 

Thrale responded to Johnson’s allusions with her own, as in example (3): 

- (3) (…) unless little Queeney sends her _Country Post_ as usual to give Information of a new _Sail of Ducks_ or some such Important Intelligence (Chapman 1952:II, 66; allusion to Swift, cf. _OED_ s.v. _sail_ n.[2] 2.b: 1727 Swift _Country Post_ Wks. 1755 III. i. 175 “Yesterday a large sail of ducks passed by here”.) 

Johnson’s letters did not limit themselves to allusions to Swift. He knew that Mrs. Thrale, being well-read, would be able to recognize references to many authors, including Richardson, Shakespeare and Rochefoucauld, as in examples (4–6) respectively: 

14 Randy C. Bax 

- (4) At last finding the Dr somewhat moody I bid him not to be disturbed, for he could not be injured till the death of Mrs. Rudd, and her life was better than his. So I _comforted and advised him_ (Chapman 1952:II, 130; allusion to Richardson’s _Clarissa_ , pointed out in fn.2.) 

- (5) I have written to Manucci to find his own way, for the _Law’s delay_ makes it difficult for me to guess when I shall be (…) (Chapman 1952:II, 132; allusion to _Hamlet_ , pointed out in fn.8.) 

- (6) the husband will feel, as Rochefoucault says, _something that does not displease him_ (Chapman 1952:II, 230; allusion to _Réflexions_ , pointed out in fn.6.) 

Thrale’s letters to Johnson, in turn, also contain a variety of allusions, some of them concerning the same authors as the ones Johnson refers to in his letters, such as Richardson and Rochefoucauld in (7) and (8). To name a few, other references in her letters to Johnson relate to Rousseau, Milton, Sheridan, Goldsmith and Armstrong, as in (9–13) respectively: 

- (7) You will serve me (bad as you are) as you did D[r] Delap, I suppose, & _give me a Glass of Wine_ when you _do_ come (Chapman 1952:II, 141; allusion to Richardson’s _Pamela_ , cf. _OED_ s.v _. insolence_ n. 1.d: 1741 Richardson _Pamela_ (1824) I. 180 “Insolence! continued she, this moment, when I bid you, know your duty, and give me a glass of wine”.) 

- (8) La Conversation des Amis ne tarit pas says even Rochefoulcoult (Chapman 1952:II, 44.) 

- (9) Suivez, as Rousseau says, la Chaine de tout cela (Chapman 1952:I, 295.) 

- (10) I am really hurried like the fallen Spirits in Milton from Frost to Fire, and from Fire to Frost (Chapman 1952:I, 327.) 

- (11) How can you say what you do about the _great Affair_ (Chapman 1952:II, 141; allusion to Sheridan, cf. _OED_ s.v _. petition_ n. 3: 1736 Sheridan in _Swift’s Lett._ (1768) IV. 161 “Thus this great affair has ended”.) 

- (12) I mend gradually and get better on the whole but am as weak as a _Cat_ now, not as strong as a _Lyon_ (Chapman 1952:II, 141; allusion to Goldsmith, cf. _OED_ s.v _. cowardice_ n.: 1774 Goldsmith _Nat. Hist._ (1776) III. 267 “It is like all of the cat kind except the lion, remarkable for its cowardice”.) 

- (13) I said I was very sorry any Trees had been felled (…) — then I am sure they are safe enough, for Mr Thrale is a very unlikely man to touch the _sacred Groves_ (Chapman 1952:II, 197; allusion to Armstrong, cf. _OED_ 

Linguistic accommodation 15 

s.v. _word_ n. IV.29: 1753 Armstrong _Taste_ 131 “Those sacred groves where raptur’d spirits in word-hunting waste the live-long day”.) 

That Mrs. Thrale adopted Johnson’s use of allusions is perhaps illustrated best by passages (14) and (15). As Chapman (1952:II, 258 fn.5) points out, Johnson’s reference to Dodd is echoed by Thrale’s in (15): 

- (14) I never said with Dr Dodd that _I love to prattle upon paper_ (Chapman 1952:II, 258; allusion to Dodd, pointed out in fn.5.) 

- (15) I am called to Breakfast & must _prattle upon paper_ no longer (Chapman 1952:II, 266.) 

Mrs. Thrale, then, accommodated her style of writing to Johnson’s. She did so for a number of reasons that will be discussed in Section 3 below. 

## 2.2 Lexical convergence 

Johnson, famous for his heavy Ramblerian[1] prose style (Redford 1986:207), “remains associated with Latinate lexis and syntax” (Percy 2000). If he adapted his style of writing to that of his correspondents, as Chapman (1952:I, xix) claims but does not show, one expects to find a moderate use of multi-syllabic words in his letters to Thrale. The reason for this is that she was known for a colloquial style of writing (Brownley 1985:634–635; Percy 2000), in which there was little room for words historically derived from the classical languages, which tend to be “polysyllabic and learned in register” (Wright & Hope 1996:213). Chapman’s claim is supported by the figures in Table 1 below, which shows the distribution of polysyllables in the letters of the two correspondents. Johnson’s averages are, indeed, not much higher than those of Thrale: while 21.9 per cent of his letters to Thrale consists of words with two syllables or more, and 6.8 per cent of words with three syllables or more, the corresponding figures for Thrale’s letters to Johnson are 19.5 and 5.0 per cent respectively.[2] 

16 Randy C. Bax 

Table 1. Distribution of polysyllables in the Thrale-Johnson correspondence 

|||Thrale|Johnson|
|---|---|---|---|
|||(%)|(%)|
|≥2|syllables|19.5 (n=2,429)|21.9 (n=3,307)|
|≥3|syllables|5.0 (n=623)|6.8 (n=1,027)|



The individual letters on which these averages are based (that is, all those in the corpus) are presented in Figure 1, which shows the distribution of monosyllables. The earliest data are from 1771. As said, no letters have survived that Thrale wrote to Johnson prior to 1770 and, to my knowledge, no letter-andreply strings written before 1771. There are two further gaps in the data, one in 1774 and one in 1782. Nevertheless, what has survived is relatively much and also spread fairly evenly across the entire period. 

With caution, the following observations can be made. Except for a few sharp rises and falls, of which at least one can be accounted for, the correspondents seem to have followed a more or less similar pattern in their production of monosyllables. On the basis of the material that has survived, it is possible to point out what clearly appear to be periods (A-C) in which Johnson not only adapted his style to Thrale’s in general by refraining from his Ramblerian, Latinate voice (thereby diminishing the difference between Thrale’s and his own average use of monosyllables), but ones in which an increase or decrease in the use of monosyllables in the letters of one correspondent is also found in the letters of the other correspondent, or vice versa. In period A, for example, one sees that both Thrale’s and Johnson’s letters show a rise in the distribution of monosyllables, which is followed by a fall, a rise, and yet another fall. It should be noted that very few letters cover the years 1771 and 1772, making it hard to say anything about this period. 

If the observed pattern can be attributed to accommodation, it will become necessary to be able to account for any deviations from it. This is, admittedly, not easy. It should be acknowledged that the data in Figure 1 present an isolated part of the correspondents’ lives, which does not take account of influences on their language use other than Johnson’s (on Thrale) and Thrale’s (on Johnson). The correspondents’ mood at the moment of writing may, for instance, have played a role. The sharp decrease in monosyllables to 69 per cent in one of Thrale’s letters from 1784 (Chapman 1952:III, 155–156) is uncharacteristic of her conversational style, but it can be explained. By that time, her relationship

with Johnson had become unstable. A widow with plans to get remarried soon, she had been detaching herself emotionally from Johnson since June 1783, which in retrospect is clearly reflected in the letter concerned. Devoid of the many indications of involvement that are so characteristic of her earlier letters, such as words of deep affection and constant references to their special relationship, it shows a Thrale who is almost feeling sorry for the old man; she is a bit distant and evasive, something which she was fully aware of and in the near future would attempt to justify (Riely 1984:78). 

## 2.3 Syntactic convergence 

Redford notes that Johnson adapted his language to Thrale’s conversational style, relying heavily on simple and compounded structures, and exhibiting “a decided preference for paratactic rather than hypotactic constructions” (1986:208). Parataxis, which is typically found in spoken language, involves the coordination of main clauses, each being of equal value. For example, “Here has been Dr. Laurence with me, and I showed him your letter, and you may easily believe we had some talk about my Master” (Redford 1992:III, 261). Hypotaxis, on the other hand, involves a sequence of clauses where the links are subordinating 

18 Randy C. Bax 

conjunctions, and it is typically found in written as opposed to spoken language (Coates 1993:26). For example, “If I interpret your letter right, you are ignominiously married” (Chapman 1952:III, 174). 

Redford’s intuitions are supported by the figures in Table 2 below: 78.2 per cent of Thrale’s letters to Johnson consisted of simple and paratactic structures; the corresponding figure for Johnson’s letters to Thrale is 73.6. 

Table 2. Syntactic structures in the Thrale-Johnson correspondence 

||Thrale|Johnson|
|---|---|---|
||(%)|(%)|
|Simple/paratactic structures|78.2 (n=772)|73.6 (n=1,033)|
|Hypotactic structures|21.8 (n=215)|26.4 (n=371)|



The following passages in (16) and (17) will illustrate Johnson’s and Thrale’s colloquial style respectively. 

- (16) Susy sends her duty and love with great propriety. Sophy sends her duty to You, and her love to Queeny and papa. Mr. Evans came in after me. You may set your heart quite at rest, no babies can be better than they appear to be. Dr Taylor went with me, and we staid a good while. He likes them very much. Susy said her Creed in French. (Redford 1991:II, 326.) 

- (17) Mr. Thrale heard me read the Passage in your Letter about Wetherell and I suppose will do right, he will say nothing. Poor Mrs Williams’s Illness makes me quite sorry, I send her a separate Card. I looked for a Girl at our School today but there was none fit for me it seems, but a blind one, and I don’t want a blind Maid of all Things. (Chapman 1952:II, 21.) 

3. Motivations underlying the correspondents’ accommodative patterns 

There are several reasons which help explain the correspondents’ accommodative behaviour. Some are, in fact, supplied by Johnson himself; other reasons become apparent when one examines their individual histories and the dynamics of their relationship. 

Linguistic accommodation 19 

Johnson was to some extent conscious of his own accommodative behaviour. This is shown by his rejection of the standard view that letters “demand plainness of diction and moderation in the use of figures” and that authors were assumed to be not only sincere but also spontaneous in their letters (Biester 1988:155). He maintained that plainness, ease and simplicity force the writer to ignore decorum, insisting that the variety among one’s correspondents demands flexibility in style (Biester 1988:155). In doing so, Johnson echoed a basic concept of CAT, namely that “the addressee is a full participant in the formulation of the message (…) and, indeed, may be regarded in a very real sense as the cause of the message” (Kraus 1987:96, as quoted in Giles et al. 1991:6). He furthermore argued that letters were not necessarily spontaneous nor did they necessarily lack invention, for the reason that one strives to appear in a good light: “a letter is addressed to a single mind, of which the prejudices and partialities are known; and must therefore please, if not by favouring them, by forbearing to oppose them” (Hill 1905:III, 207, as quoted in Biester 1988:158). 

CAT predicts that “sometimes there are beneficial outcomes to one or the other participant (…) or both” underlying a deliberate or subconscious “reduction of linguistic dissimilarities” (Giles et al. 1991:3,18). Indeed, there were clear beneficial outcomes to both Johnson and Thrale which appear to be plausible explanations for their accommodative behaviour towards each other. Johnson was in a way financially dependent on his wealthy friends, the Thrales. An adopted family member, he was allowed to profit from their wealth and enjoy a luxurious life-style previously unknown to him. Until his late thirties, Johnson had not lived very far above poverty, earning a “frugal living by supplying the Gentleman’s Magazine with poems, brief biographies (…) reviews, and essays” (Fussel 1972:18,24). In his view, poverty was “a great enemy to human happiness, it certainly destroys liberty, and it makes some virtues impracticable, and others extremely difficult” (quoted in Middendorff 1965:53). This was something Hester Thrale knew when she wrote in her diary that “Mr Johnson has more Tenderness for Poverty than any other Man I ever knew; and less for other Calamities” (Balderston 1951:I, 184). Now, Johnson enjoyed lavish meals, and the Thrales took him with them on journeys and trips. “I lived much with Mrs. Thrale”, he writes, “and had all the care from her that she could take” (Chapman 1952:II, 476). This life was the exact opposite of his years of poverty — a traumatic experience — and it was in striking contrast to his dark and cramped house in London “where he dined on roast from a local cook house (…) [and] slept in dirty linen” (Redford 1986:211). 

It probably was not difficult for Johnson to get used to these physical 

20 Randy C. Bax 

comforts, as we may gather from one of his letters to Mrs. Thrale in which he happily comments on her husband enlarging the Thrale residence in Southwark: “So _we_ shall have a fine house in the winter”, he writes, “as _we_ already have in the summer [i.e. Streatham Park]. I am not sorry for the appearance of a little superfluous expense” (Redford 1992:II, 226, my italics). It seems only understandable that, although the Thrales were his friends, it was Johnson’s wish that they would continue to be so. For although he considered Streatham Park to be home, and even called it so (Redford 1992:I, 284), Johnson remained but a virtual member of the Thrale household, as Hester Thrale perhaps subconsciously made clear to him when she wrote “that next to my own very Household, Your friendship is the dearest Thing to your faithful & Obliged Servant H: L: Thrale” (Chapman 1952:II, 140). 

But while he clearly enjoyed these physical comforts, Johnson longed primarily for Mrs. Thrale’s company and conversation (Redford 1986:212). Abhorring solitude, he craved for attention. As the mistress of Streatham Park and the family life it represented, Mrs. Thrale was able to give him just that. She became the one person who took care of him when he was ill, listened to whatever he had to say; she would stand the strain of “the late vigils which her guest’s insomnia and constant tea drinking rendered necessary” (Clifford 1968:149), and as his close friend and confidant understood the many allusions in his letters and listened to his confessions. In an excellent discussion of the psychological complexities which characterized their relationship, Brownley goes even further, arguing that Johnson found in Thrale “a woman who combined many of the best traits in his two former maternal figures”, his mother and his wife, who had both died in the 1750s: she mothered him, both physically and intellectually (1984:68–69). 

Thrale converged to Johnson’s writing by developing a style in which allusions play an important role. This can partly be explained by her need for approval, which is one of the reasons why people converge to each other linguistically (Giles et al. 1991:38). Thrale had had literary ambitions ever since she was a child; she had always had the need to show off her talents as a writer, and was still looking for approval. She found it in Johnson, who was interested in her writing (Brownley 1985:624–625). Moreover, the fact that Johnson wrote to her in the style she preferred must have been interpreted by her as some kind of approval. In addition to receiving approval, she wanted to nurture their relationship, because her connection with Johnson made her a literary figure “by association” (Brownley 1985:627). Accommodating her style of writing to his, which was something that pleased him, was one way of doing this. 

Linguistic accommodation 21 

The data presented indicates that Thrale did not, however, converge lexically, at least not in terms of a consistent increase in her use of polysyllables, nor did she adopt a syntactically more complex style. Accommodation theory offers an explanation: convergence may bring rewards as well as costs; potential costs include possible loss of personal and social identity (Giles et al. 1991:21). Indeed, Thrale’s “choice of a colloquial, easy, and familiar style”, Brownley (1985:635) argues, “was a creative alternative to fulfil what she saw as her own stylistic needs and those of the age”.

Inflectional suffixes were excluded from the counting (e.g. _swimming_ = monosyllabic) as were plural _-s/-es_ and _-en_ (e.g. _horses_ = monosyllabic).

## Style evolution in the English sermon[*] 

Claudia Claridge and Andrew Wilson University of Greifswald / Lancaster University 

## 1. Sermons as a genre or text type 

Sermons represent an interesting text type for two main reasons.[1] First, they are at the crossroads between orality and literacy. Although Ellison (1998:14) called the sermon a genre of “oral literature”, sermons can actually be oral, _or_ written, _or_ a mixture of both, making a clear characterization of the genre as a whole very problematic (cf. Bach 1997:76): for instance, to take just two extreme cases, J.H. Newman simply _read out_ his detailed manuscripts verbatim, whilst C.H. Spurgeon preached completely extempore and had transcriptions made only for publication (Ellison 1998:16). Second, sermons belong to the special register of religion, which Crystal (1995:371) maintains “is probably the most distinctive of all occupational varieties”. It has traditionally been a more formal, more conservative register than others, one slower to change and thus exhibiting more archaic features: it is at a greater distance from ordinary, everyday language than other registers. 

Up to the seventeenth century, homiletic theory was based on classical oratory. However, the seventeenth century witnessed a gradual and mounting counterreaction to (heavily) ornamented styles and a development towards an ideal of a ‘plain’ style, which was to last for the future (cf. Howell 1956, Lessenich 1972, Ellison 1998). This ideal was propagated by sermon manuals such as Wilkins’ _Ecclesiastes_ (1646), which advocated plain, wholesome, and affectionate expository writing and was so popular that it continued to be reprinted until 1843 (Howell 1971:451–457). Properties of the plain style are said to include few modifiers, short sentences, little use of imagery, simple or asymmetrical paratactic syntax, Anglo-Saxon vocabulary, and also what are, strictly speaking, non-linguistic features such as ease of comprehension, fluency of expression, and exact and honest representation of content (Adolph 1981:440).[2] 

26 Claudia Claridge and Andrew Wilson 

Despite the interesting aspects of the sermon and the continuity of the genre, making it an ideal object for historical stylistics, sermons have so far received little linguistic attention. This paper therefore intends to look at how sermons as a text type have changed linguistically from the seventeenth century to the present day, using Biber’s (1988) factor analysis and concentrating on the three factors or dimensions which seem particularly relevant for sermons, namely 1 (Involved vs. Informational Production), 4 (Overt Expression of Persuasion) and 6 (On-Line Informational Elaboration). These are connected with aspects important for preaching, namely the greater or lesser degree of orality, the quantity and packaging of information contained in the sermon, the amount of emotional and attitudinal involvement and the author’s orientation towards the addressees. 

## 2. The data 

The data for this study consists of a small corpus of 87,427 words, comprising 24 sermons from three periods. As the corpus is small, care was taken to make it homogeneous by keeping extralinguistic factors as stable as possible. The criteria for the selection of sermons were therefore: (i) Anglican sermons only (as different denominations also have different preaching styles and traditions); (ii) English (not pan-British or American), as this country has the longest documented tradition; (iii) expository sermons (if possible), as different textual approaches will influence the structure and possibly the linguistic features of the sermon; and (iv) complete texts, as features can cluster or be absent in smaller parts of the texts, which might bias the analysis. 

The three periods and the texts chosen are as follows: 

_late 17th/early 18th century_ : Richard Sherlock (4,395 words), Thomas Pittis (7,176), John Waller (6,323), Joseph Trapp (12,915) (30,809 words; 4 sermons/authors)[3] _19th century (1800–1850)_ : Richard Hurrell Froude (3,429), Frederick Denison Maurice (4,932), Henry Melvill (7,428; 8,056), John Henry Newman (3,150), Robert Wilberforce (4,825) (31,820 words, 6 sermons/5 authors)[4] 

_late 20th century (1990s/2000)_ : George Carey (1,744), Peter Kashouris (1,591), Brian Mountford (1,395; 1,406), Martyn Percy (1,999), Janice Scott (1,302; 1,160), Brian Michael Smith (1,171), Owen Spencer-Thomas (1,580), David Stone (1,335; 2,094), Melvin Tinker (2,873; 2,808), Thomas Butler (2,340) (24,798 words, 14 sermons/10 authors)[5] 

Style evolution in the English sermon 27 

The different number of authors and sermons for each period reflects a change in sermon length, with sermons becoming shorter and shorter the nearer one gets to the present. 

## 3. Biber’s factors and their adaptation 

As this is not the place for a full discussion of Biber’s model (cf. Biber 1988: Chapters 4–5), a brief summary must suffice. Factors are essentially sets of cooccurring features, and these features may have either positive or negative weights on the factor. The communicative function served by such a set of features leads to the postulation of a functional dimension, such as ‘involved vs. informational’, along which texts can vary. For the factor score analysis each feature is identified in the individual texts, normalized to a text length of 1,000 words, standardized to a mean of 0.0 and a standard deviation of 1.0, and then added up to yield the factor score for the text (features with negative weights are subtracted).[6] Period factor scores are determined by adding individual text scores and then dividing by number of texts. 

The following are the features contained in Factors 1, 4 and 6 (Biber 1988:89f.):[7] 

_factor 1:_ (a) _features with positive weights (‘involved’)_ : private verbs, _that_ deletion, contractions, present tense verbs, second person pronouns, _do_ as proverb, analytic negation, demonstrative pronouns, general emphatics, first person pronouns, pronoun _it_ , _be_ as main verb, causative subordination, discourse particles, indefinite pronouns, general hedges, amplifiers, sentence relatives, _wh-_ questions, possibility modals, non-phrasal coordination, _wh-_ clauses, final prepositions; (b) _features with negative weights (‘informational’)_ : nouns, word length, prepositions, type/token ratio, attributive adjectives 

_factor 4: positive features (‘persuasive’)_ : infinitives, prediction modals, suasive verbs, conditional subordination, necessity modals, split auxiliaries — _no negative features_ 

_factor 6: positive features (‘on-line elaboration’)_ : _that-_ clauses as verb complements, demonstratives, _that-_ relative clauses on object positions, _that-_ clauses as adjective complements — _no negative features with sufficiently high weights_ 

ThepresentstudydifferedinsomewaysfromBiber’sapproach.Foronething,the procedure adopted was less automated and involved more manual interventions, 

28 Claudia Claridge and Andrew Wilson 

partly because the tagging program used was not quite adequate for the task in hand.[8] Another, more important reason was that it is necessary to take into account the specific linguistic characteristics of older texts and the fact of language change.[9] In particular, Biber’s lists of lexical features have been expanded by including terms likely to have been used in earlier times. The list of private verbs, for example, has been increased by _apprehend_ , _conceive_ , _comprehend_ , _esteem_ , _foreknow_ , _ponder_ , _regard_ . Additions were also made to the lists of hedges ( _perhaps_ , _may be_ ), general emphatics ( _indeed_ , _right_ , _surely_ , _truly_ , _verily_ ), amplifiers ( _exceedingly_ , _passing_ , _full well_ ), and discourse particles ( _why_ , _whatever_ ).[10] In the case of emphatic _do_ , which belongs to the category of general emphatics, all instances in which the use of _do_ is a consequence of inversion (rather common in the nineteenth century) have been excluded. _Thou_ , _thy_ , _thee_ , _thine_ were included in the second person pronoun search. In the case of indefinite pronouns, manual checks were also made for the two-word spelling variants and relevant instances included in the search results. 

As regards the syntactic features, particular care was taken with those where Biber included punctuation in the search algorithms (i.e. sentence relatives, _wh_ -questions, non-phrasal coordination, final prepositions and _that_ -complementation). Punctuation in older texts is notoriously difficult and cannot be relied upon when looking for other features. In these cases, the words or word classes in question were first located and then checked through manually, hence the present results are based on a slightly wider interpretation than Biber’s. With prepositions, for example, stranding cases which occur in mid-sentence have also been included; with split auxiliaries, both intervening adverbs and longer adverbials have been permitted. 

However, no completely new features have been added at this point, since this would damage the integrity of the present factor structure. The aim was not to redo the whole factorial extraction process with a new set of original features, but to see how sermons fared on those dimensions that have been relatively well attested so far. 

## 4. The development of sermons on the three dimensions 

Figure 1 shows the development of sermons along the informational-involved dimension from the seventeenth century to the present day. 

Clearly the development is from more informational textual characteristics to more involved ones. This concurs with the general drift of various genres 

Style evolution in the English sermon 29 

**==> picture [327 x 146] intentionally omitted <==**

**----- Start of picture text -----**<br>
Involved<br>5<br>20 [th ] century (3.8)<br>0<br>19 [th ] century (–0.9)<br>-5<br>-10<br>17 [th] /18 [th] century (–11.7)<br>-15<br>Informational<br>**----- End of picture text -----**<br>


Figure 1. The development of sermons on Dimension 1 (Involved vs. Informational Production) 

towards more involved linguistic characterizations the nearer one gets to the present, which was found by Biber & Finegan (1989, 1992) in their historical studies. However, on the whole, even the mean for present-day sermons does not come out as very involved.[11] This could be due to the restraining influence of the religious register as a whole (cf. Section 1 above), especially within a mainstream Anglican context. It is quite possible that certain more charismatic styles of preaching will show a greater degree of involvement. Also, the amount of content sermons have to carry will draw them more onto the informational side. Indeed, if one looks at individual features, it is apparent that sermons of all three periods are relatively high on the ‘negative’, content-carrying features of this dimension (nouns, prepositional phrases, attributive adjectives), with the twentieth-century ones actually being the most substantival. The present result would also point to the hypothesis that most sermons are in fact prepared speeches, and thus reflect a more careful and literate approach to textual composition. One also has to take into account that not all priests are naturally good speakers, so that carefully preparing their sermons is the obvious thing for them to do. 

It is also interesting to take a closer look at the individual sermons in their respective periods, which is done in Figure 2. The spread of individual sermons in each period is rather wide, something that also goes for Biber’s (1988) and Biber & Finegan’s (1989, 1992) data. However, this, together with the apparent overlap between periods (the nineteenth and twentieth centuries have an almost identical range), might raise some doubts about the value of the period means discussed above. While sermons clearly have a similar social context and goal — 

30 Claudia Claridge and Andrew Wilson

Figure 2. Individual sermons on Dimension 1 

and are thus a genre by extralinguistic standards — this need not imply that they all display similar stylistic features. Personal and doctrinal characteristics of the preachers will certainly play an important role, as may the existence of an influential teaching or manual tradition — which latter might have been more dominant in former times.[12] 

As to the four seventeenth- and eighteenth-century sermons, they present a unitary picture, at least in so far as they are all in the negative range and thus clearly at the informational end of the spectrum. Also, they interestingly exhibit a clear chronological sequence from the earliest and least involved (Sherlock 1669) to the latest and most involved (Trapp 1721). The end of the seventeenth century was exactly the time when the so-called ‘plain’ style was said to become more influential and more widely used, so a somewhat more balanced mix of informational andinvolvedcharacteristics oftextscould correspondtotherise ofthisstyle. However, much more data would be necessary to follow up this hypothesis. 

The nineteenth-century sermons show a rather wide spread. This, however, is partly due to Maurice’s sermon, which comes out as very involved. The question of whether Maurice is just the ‘odd one out’ or whether the huge gap 

Style evolution in the English sermon 31 

between him and the other five sermons would be filled by using more data (thus confirming the wide spread) cannot be answered at the moment.[13] It needs to be noted that Maurice has, of course, heavily influenced the period mean. The majority (four sermons) are, as in the preceding period, in the negative range, with the fifth just barely in the positive, but they do not extend as far down as before. An interesting aspect is that Newman, who is known for his literate approach (cf. Section 1 above), is next to Maurice the most involved of the nineteenth-century preachers we looked at. 

The spread in the twentieth century is not only wide but also fairly even, which points towards sermons as a rather disparate text type nowadays. An interesting point to be seen both in the nineteenth- and the twentieth-century data is the fact that sermons by the same author can come out differently with respect to dimension scores (nineteenth century: Melvill 1+2; twentieth century: Stone 1+2, Tinker 1+2, Scott 1+2, and most strikingly Mountford 1+2, with scores of 8.5 and −7.9 respectively). This highlights that the personal style of the preacher alone cannot explain the spread, but other factors can also exert considerable influence. In two of these cases, the reason for variation is apparently the topic/content of the sermon. As the topic in hand can influence or correlate with communicative purpose, a resulting shift on the dimension is not altogether surprising. Mountford 2 and Scott 1, which are the more informational members of the pairs, both deal with the difficult doctrinal topic of the Trinity, whereas Scott 2 treats Jesus’ resurrection in a down-to-earth way and Mountford 1 talks about salvation by works or faith (in itself actually also a difficult topic, but one that can be treated argumentatively and on the level of the individual Christian). Mountford 2 is distinguished (in the informational direction) from Mountford 1 in so far as it has (far) fewer private verbs (8.5 per thousand), present tense verbs (59.0), second person pronouns (4.9), hedges (0), and amplifiers (0.7), giving it less (inter)personal and emotional flavour. The negative feature figures, however, for both sermons are almost identical. On the other hand, Mountford 2 uses twice as many contractions (10.7), as if it wanted to make up for a heavier content by employing superficial orality markers. The following two quotations contrast typical passages of the two sermons: 

- (1) We might start by asking what faith is. The idea of faith so easily gets confused with the idea of belief — belief in the body of doctrines, the official teachings; what is sometimes called simply “the faith”. If you believe what the Church teaches, then you will be saved. Well, that presents all sorts of problems for people who entertain secret doubts, or who intellectually cannot accept the whole package — whatever the whole package is. In this 

32 Claudia Claridge and Andrew Wilson 

sense, faith is much harder than works, and makes salvation even more inaccessible. At least a good work is a concrete thing that you can set about doing, and know when you’ve done it. (Mountford 1) 

- (2) In the fourth century there was hot debate with a _guy_ called Arius who said that the Son of God was not eternal but created by God. He was not therefore God by nature. The Council of Nicaea (325), where _our Nicene Creed_ originates from, takes great pains to say that Jesus Christ “was begotten not made, of one being with the Father”. Then there was much debate about whether Christ could be both human and divine and whether he had one nature or two natures. The Council of Chalcedon in 451 tried to _sort this one out_ , but didn’t entirely succeed. In other words, from time to time, Christians have found it important to try to standardise belief or at least to set boundaries to speculation. One of the key questions _for us in the late nineties_ is whether there can be such a thing as orthodoxy — i.e. a right and a wrong way of believing — particularly as Christianity has mushroomed into so many denominations. (Mountford 2) 

It needs to be mentioned, however, that in Mountford 2 there are also passages similar to quote (1). Most noticeable in (2) is the largely impersonal, contentcentred approach. However, it is also apparent that the author tries to include the personal aspect wherever possible ( _our Nicene Creed_ , _for us in the late nineties_ ) and tries to lighten the seriousness by bringing in more colloquial expressions, such as _guy, sort this one out_ (things which the factor scale analysis does not capture), or light-hearted comparisons, such as _some sort of Rubic Cube of Father, Son, and Holy Spirit_ in an earlier section of the sermon. 

In contrast to Dimension 1, Dimensions 4 and 6 do not yield a clear distinction of period scores. The scores on Dimension 4 are −0.00016 (nineteenth century), −0.0001 (twentieth century) and 0.0005 (seventeenth/eighteenth centuries), which means that the overall persuasiveness of sermons, contrary to expectations, is not very pronounced in the first place and, furthermore, has remained constant over time. It might be that preachers take it for granted that their congregations share their views anyway, or that they use less overt, more subtle means of persuasion not covered in this factor. Biber (1988:151) suggested that persuasiveness may still be a characteristic of individual texts within genres otherwise undistinguished in that respect. Indeed, there is again a wide spread, with some sermons clearly exceeding the period mean, but the majority nevertheless comes out as not very persuasive (cf. Figure 3). 

ThespreadonDimension6with−0.0002(twentiethcentury),0(seventeenth/ eighteenth centuries), and 0.37 (nineteenth century) is also rather unremarkable.

Figure 3. Sermons on Dimension 4 (Overt Expression of Persuasion) 

Again there is no historical development. Moreover, sermons seem not to be distinguished on this dimension, which is interesting in view of the fact that in Biber’s (1988:155ff.) investigation both prepared and spontaneous speeches have high scores. This dimension links informational elaboration under realtime production constraints with the expression of speakers’ opinions and attitudes. The degree of preparedness of sermons may play a role here: sermons which are based on a careful written draft (and thus potentially influenced by the norms of written language) and simply read out in church are not at all bound by real-time constraints. As with the results on Dimension 1 above, this points to a rather literate approach to sermon writing. In all three periods, however, there are individual sermons which have comparable scores to Biber’s speeches on this dimension (cf. Figure 4).
