## 5. Some examples 

In this section, some more detailed examples are presented, concentrating on the extreme cases in each of the periods (and mostly on Dimension 1, as it yields clear contrasts). 

Extract (3) from Sherlock represents the most informational sermon from the seventeenth century: 

- (3) Against all such sinful Compliance, Take heed to your selves, my Brethren take heed to your selves, and to your vows and promises, Sacerdotal and Ecclesiastical, confirmed by your respective Subscriptions to the Articles and Canons of the Church, to the Injunctions of your Metropolitan and Dioecesan, to the Approbation and constant use of the Liturgy of the Church, and of that onely in publick: In which last particular, wherein the 

Style evolution in the English sermon 35 

greatest part of the Ministerial Function consists, I cannot but testifie in this Assembly, so much concern’d therein: That there is utterly a fault amongst us; In that those Divine and excellent Prayers, and heavenly inspired Prayers of God, and that ancient and Constant way of Gods worship in Publick, is by many too much slighted and neglected, by many irreverently and indevoutly celebrated, by many mangled and maimed, curtail’d, abbreviated, and by the Additions of others implicitly vilified: And generally almost by all secretly undermin’d; enervated and subverted by each mans private prayer in publick: (Sherlock) 

It features the highest proportion of nouns in this period and is rather verbose, whereas some of the involved features are much reduced, e.g. private verbs (3.9), demonstrative pronouns (2.3), first person pronouns (13.4), pronoun _it_ (4.3), possibility modals (2.9) — i.e. those that increase personal involvement and vagueness. The type/token ratio, however, is one of the lowest in the whole data sample, which reduces informational complexity or density and may point to a rather ornamental use of some of the informational features, such as those similar to Ong’s (1982:38ff.) formulaic epithets (e.g. _Divine and excellent Prayers_ ). Another noticeable feature of this sermon, which would disconnect it from the plain style and link it instead rather with the ornamented style, is its copiousness as evidenced, for example, in the common phrasal coordination (cf. Biber 1988:75), such as _your vows and promises, Sacerdotal and Ecclesiastical, irreverently and indevoutly_ or _mangled and maimed_ . Sherlock incidentally also comes out lowest on Dimensions 4 and 6, which points to the fact that they highlight similar traits (in particular orality, emotionality). 

In contrast, Trapp, illustrated in (4), is at the least informational end of the scale for the seventeenth/eighteenth century, and is also above average on Dimension 6, visibly especially in the use of demonstratives (12.4) and _that_ -complementation following verbs (8.1). 

- (4) I. First then I observe, That certain sacred Orders of Men are useful, and even necessary; being instituted by God himself; that great is the Benefit and Dignity of their Office; and that they ought to be loved, and honoured accordingly. I say, I observe This: For I pretend not here to prove it at large: Nor would there be any Occasion of proving it at all; were every one, who calls himself a Christian, really such. Those among our Adversaries, who believe any thing of the Bible, must, and I think, do acknowledge, that the Jewish Prophets and Priests were by Divine Institution; because That People was under the immediate, and even visible, Inspection of God himself. And the same they are pleased to grant of the Apostles, and Others 

36 Claudia Claridge and Andrew Wilson 

who had the Gift of working Miracles; under the Gospel Dispensation. But as We have no such direct and particular Revelations, nor any such extraordinary Powers; they imagine that they have room to object against the Commission of our Ministry, said to be derived through so many Ages by a continued and regular Succession. They may indeed object against This; and we cannot help it. (…) Whoever, I say, shall consider all This, and yet deny, or so much as question, the Divine Commission of Us, who at This Day call ourselves the Ministers of Christ, must either be a Hypocrite while he pretends to believe any thing of the Gospel; or one of Those perverse Opiniatres, who dispute about every thing, and therefore are not fit to be disputed with about any thing. (Trapp) 

This passage illustrates a more personal style, immediately visible in the use of pronouns, especially first person pronouns (27.9). On the whole there are more first person plural pronouns than singular ones, which is true also for most of the other sermons. _We_ , _us_ , _our_ can be, and often are, employed in their inclusive sense to create a community feeling between preacher and audience, which, in this case, consists of the sons of the clergy. However, not all parts of the sermons are as personalized as the one quoted, which highlights again that it is necessary to examine complete texts for a comprehensive analysis. 

Of the nineteenth-century sermons, Wilberforce’s is the most informational one. It is in the lead for all ‘negative’ features with the exception of type/token ratio. Wilberforce also uses nouns where a verbal expression would have been more natural and perfectly possible, e.g. _are (…) a witness to, holding converse_ . Notice also the type of words he uses, which are rather complex and mostly of a more learned or formal stylistic level, for instance, _infrequent_ , _counteracting system_ , _mode of intercourse_ , _external frame_ , _delusive pageant_ . The different stylistic implications of individual words is something not captured by factor scale analysis, but it plays a role in the characterization of plain style and may indeed be an important point of a more detailed stylistic investigation. 

- (5) For whatever may be the language of our Public Offices, yet silent Churches, deserted altars, infrequent Eucharists, are but too plain a witness to the national unbelief. The counteracting system must appeal surely to some principle of our nature, and be built upon some law, which we are compelled to admit. And so it doubtless is: for if the principle of mediation be our revealed mode of intercourse with God, yet is there another channel, which the very constitution of our being forbids us to neglect. This compound being, compacted of soul and body, has in it two distinct principles, and while the one supplies our means of holding converse with our 

Style evolution in the English sermon 37 

brethren, our relation to God depends upon the other. For the external frame, which enables us to play our part in the delusive pageant of life, is not really ourselves. And the hidden principle, which witnesses by intuitive consciousness in each man’s bosom, claims natural kindred with the Most High. (Wilberforce) 

Maurice, on the other hand, represents the extreme (and perhaps unusual) involved approach to preaching in the nineteenth century: 

- (6) Yes! brethren, I believe that you must give heed to these words if you would know what Christmas day is, or what any day of your lives is, or what you yourselves are, or why you have come into this world, and what you have to do in it. But I believe, also, that they are not hard words; not words which poor and ignorant people need turn away from. I am sure they are meant especially for those who find that the things which are told them in books puzzle them very much, and that they cannot make out the sense of what is told them from pulpits; for men who have a livelihood to get by the sweat of their brows; for men whose bodies and minds are wasted by disease. I say this confidently, and I think when you have considered what it is that perplexes you in books and in sermons, you will agree with me. Is it not the words you read and hear in them? They float about you; they tell you of something that you are sure you want to know, but you cannot see them or handle them, and the things you can see and handle, do not tell you what they signify. (Maurice) 

This passage is typical of the whole sermon, which is composed in a very personalized manner. Apart from being decidedly low on all the negative features, it has rather (or very) high instances of private verbs (18.4), present tense verbs (72.2), first and second person pronouns (40.1 & 17.6), _be_ (36.1), and stranded prepositions (2.4). Also note the sentence structure: whilst these are not, strictly speaking, simple sentences, they do contain a large quantity of clausal coordination and parataxis, which make the passage easier to follow than is the case when greater quantities of subordination and embedding are present. Maurice also has the highest score of all nineteenth-century sermons on Dimension 6, which together with the result for Dimension 1 points to a more spontaneous and emotional approach to preaching. 

As mentioned above, it is remarkable that Newman with his literate approach to sermon writing (captured also in his low score on Dimension 6) does not come out further down the informational scale. On the one hand, Newman employs relatively large amounts of positive features such as private 

38 Claudia Claridge and Andrew Wilson 

verbs (17.1), pronouns (14.3 & 21.3), emphatics (10.5), and amplifiers (5.1), but, on the other hand, he is remarkably low on the negative features. If one looks at (7), one is struck by the pervasive presence of a feature (one not captured by factor analysis) that is listed as typical of a plain style: he shows a clear preference for short, simple sentences and a paratactic, sometimes abruptsounding, structure of sentence combination. Perhaps his careful production technique leads to his finding exactly the right balance between more involved, and also more ‘natural’, linguistic features and more informational ones. 

- (7) How piously they sometimes talk! they seem to own the shortness and uncertainty of life, they seem to own the importance of religion — they speak even of the mercies of Christ, His death and miraculous resurrection — yet this is all — with this they are satisfied — they seem to reverence the Saviour — but in works deny Him; they think they believe all the miracles He wrought and so they do; but in the same manner that they believe that there was once such a person as Pontius Pilate — (…) Oh (worldly and careless livers), think of your perilous state, — our words indeed are weak, but we speak as though God did beseech you by us (2 Cor 5) — Dare not to profess without practising — you are standing on the brink of destruction — the dying fall about you like the leaves in autumn. (Newman) 

In the twentieth century it is Butler who is the most informational, actually reaching nearly the same score as Wilberforce in the nineteenth century. Butler is relatively high on nouns (211.5), and the highest of all on prepositional phrases (117.9) and attributive adjectives (69.6). In contrast, there are very few private verbs (5.1) and second person pronouns (2.6). The whole sermon makes a very formal impression and it is obvious that a considerable amount of work went into its rhetorical composition. This might be due to the extralinguistic fact that this is Butler’s enthronement sermon as the new Bishop of Southwark and our example was thus one of his first sermons there. 

- (8) We witness to our faith in a world where most Anglicans live overseas; where the centre of gravity of our Communion is now in Africa, whilst in that continent and in Asia many of our fellow Christians are living as minority groups in places where the practice of the faith is difficult or dangerous. Our words and our ways in Southwark can have a profound effect in such places. We are not a parochial church, we are a church which is world wide and history long and we are called to work out our faith locally within that global context. (Butler) 

Style evolution in the English sermon 39 

Another sermon that is rhetorically rather carefully constructed is Tinker 1 (9), which, however, has a much higher dimension score. This, like the Newman example above, shows that careful preparation need not lead to a more elaborate, informational outcome. 

- (9) So why the lure? What is the attraction of idolatry? Well, the first and most obvious reason is that we think it means we are not accountable to God because we have made the idol and so we feel we can control it. Notice the emphasis on who does the making in vv 12–15 — it is man who does the shaping or fashioning, a phrase which is repeated three times. They are safe, predictable, controllable, there to serve us rather than we serve them — why in v 17 the plea to the idol is “save us.” In short the idols reflect the self which is on the throne of our hearts. We may want power and status — why? It makes us feel important — it’s our idol. We may move from one sexual conquest to the next. Why? Because it makes me feel good — it’s our idol. All the time it is self which is central. (Tinker 1) 

The last example here illustrates the most involved sermon of our twentiethcentury group: 

- (10) And so what seemed at the time to be completely inexplicable to Peter and the others turns out to be the central plank of God’s plan of rescue for humanity. Now, of course, the bad things that happen to us aren’t of anything like the same significance! But the principle Peter lays down holds good. At the time we do not always know what God is up to and why it is that he allows the most awful things to happen. But the thing is that God has a proven track record — he can be relied on absolutely. We’re not always invited to understand God but we are always invited to trust him. (Stone 1) 

Contractions (16.5), stranded prepositions (7.5), present tense verbs (92.1) and _wh_ -questions (9.7) are among the features of this sermon with high frequencies. The rarity of attributive adjectives (29.2) is very noticeable, and the sermon also has the lowest word-length average (4.08) of all sermons looked at. Word length, as a rule, proved not to be a substantial discriminator between the individual sermons, but a lower figure in this respect might point to less use of Latinate (and thus less informationally dense) vocabulary. A check on this sermon’s lexical usage found that only 25% of its lexical types are non-Germanic (among them also many simple items such as _basic, copy, plan_ ), which is low compared to Hughes’ (2000:393) calculations of a Norman-French/Latin proportion of 55% of the core vocabulary of English. 

40 Claudia Claridge and Andrew Wilson 

The two most persuasive sermons in the corpus are Melvill 2 (12) in the nineteenth century and Tinker 2 (11) in the twentieth century. Melvill and Tinker are especially high on infinitives (21.8 & 24.9) and prediction modals (9.5 & 6.1), Melvill additionally on split auxiliaries (9.2) and Tinker on conditionals (6.1). Looking at the two extracts one notices a difference: in Tinker’s sermon the persuasive features are closely linked with the interpersonal features found on Dimension 1, which heightens their effectiveness, while Melvill uses them in a more detached, third-person context, thus actually reducing the overall persuasiveness. 

- (11) But it should be inconceivable that a Christian should live like that. No, he or she has someone else at the centre of their lives now, a person who shapes and motivates all that they do — the Lord. If Christ is Lord then nothing is outside his dominion. How? V9 “For this very reason, Christ died and returned to life so that he might be …” what? How would we complete that verse if we were writing it? We would probably put “Saviour”, wouldn’t we? (Tinker 2) 

- (12) Now it is very interesting to remark how the accomplishment of ancient prophecy seems often to have hung upon a thread, so that the least thing, a thought or a word, might have sufficed to prevent its occurrence. There are many predictions in reference to Christ, which could only be fulfilled by his enemies, and of which we might have expected that these enemies, anxious to disprove his claims, would have been too shrewd to help the accomplishment. The marvel is, that these enemies were not more on the alert; that they should have done, or allowed things which, on a moment’s consideration, they might have seen to be evidences that Jesus was Messiah. One would have expected that, with prophecies in their hands which they themselves applied to the Christ, they would have taken pains to prevent, so far as possible, their apparent fulfilment in Jesus of Nazareth. (Melvill 2) 

## 6. Conclusion 

Of the three dimensions studied here, ‘Involved vs. Informational Production’ (1), ‘Overt Expression of Persuasion’ (4) and ‘On-Line Informational Elaboration’ (6), only the first one showed a historical stylistic development for sermons. Overall, sermons appear to have a relatively low degree of involvement, which is not surprising given the amount of information that they need to carry; but nevertheless, as time goes on, there is a general tendency for 

Style evolution in the English sermon 41 

sermons to demonstrate what Biber identifies as a more involved style. However, although this _general_ tendency exists, greater involvement is by no means a universal stylistic feature, and there is, especially at later dates, substantial within-period and within-author variation. Thus, rather than there being an across-the-board drift from an informational to a more involved style, there has been instead a drift towards greater diversity and the _admittance_ of more involved styles. 

However, although some kind of stylistic evolution towards intuitively simpler language has taken place within the sermon genre (as is clear from the qualitative stylistic analysis in Section 5), this change does not seem to be so closely linked with the informational vs. involved dimension posited by Biber. Biber’s dimension appears to have more to do with a change in ‘mind style’ as opposed to ‘prose style’ _per se_ ; but, in the present case, the change seems to be more closely tied to issues such as the formality of vocabulary (e.g. Anglo-Saxon vs. Latinate) and the complexity of grammatical structure (e.g. sentence length, proportion of clausal coordination vs. subordination, parataxis, etc.), i.e. features that are connected to the ‘plain style’. The degree to which traditional rhetorical figures are employed also appears relevant. These are contrasts that mostly do not feature on Biber’s factor scales. 

Clearly, then, a full account of the evolution of sermon style will involve examining more distinctions than are available on the dimensions investigated here. A potentially fruitful development of this research would thus be to carry out an _ab initio_ factor analysis on similar lines to Biber’s original study, but making use of additional relevant features, such as those mentioned above. Biber’s five-factor framework has been shown to be a powerful and strongly replicable model for analysing style variation in English, but, as he points out himself (Biber 1995:346–347), it is both possible and useful to add additional features that seem of interest.

## Lexical bundles in Early Modern English dialogues 

A window into the speech-related language of the past[*] 

Jonathan Culpeper and Merja Kytö Lancaster University / Uppsala University 

## 1. Introduction 

The purpose of our study is to investigate the role played by recurrent wordcombinations in speech-related language of the Early Modern English period. Our interest in words that frequently co-occur arises from the fact that studies on Present-day English (see Section 2) have shown that: (a) word-combinations (e.g. ‘I don’t know’) play a special role with regard to the on-line planning pressures of speech, and are thus a characteristic of the spoken medium; (b) word-combinations characterize particular text types (e.g. ‘in order to’ is particularlycommoninacademicwriting);and(c)word-combinationscharacterize particular speech acts (e.g. ‘thanks very much’ is characteristic of thanking) (see Altenberg & Eeg-Olofsson 1990:1–6 for further references relating to all the above points). We will focus on trial proceedings and comedy drama, as drawn from the Corpus of English Dialogues 1560–1760, and on word-combinations which consist of three words (as in the examples given above). In future research we will also consider two-word and four-word combinations. We start with three-word combinations because they offer a good compromise between the great number of different two-word combinations and the small number of different four-word combinations. We are also mindful of the fact that threeword first person parenthetical verb constructions of the type ‘I + verb + you’ are a focus of current research interest (e.g. Busse 1998; Traugott 2000). 

Our research questions can be summed up as follows: (1) what were the word-combinations characteristic of authentic and constructed dialogue of the 

46 Jonathan Culpeper and Merja Kytö 

period; (2) how do these word-combinations relate to particular pragmatic and discoursal functions; (3) what are the grammatical characteristics of these wordcombinations; (4) how do these word-combinations vary across our text-types and our period 1560–1760; (5) how do these word-combinations compare with those revealed in present-day studies for spoken and written English? We will first briefly consider studies that have had a particular impact on our own work. We will then describe our data and methodology, which, as far as we are aware, has never previously been applied to historical data. Finally, we will introduce and discuss the results of our analysis. 

## 2. Studies on lexical bundles and related phenomena in Present-day English 

A welter of overlapping terms appears in the literature on the area under discussion: recurrent word-combinations have been referred to as lexical bundles, multi-word units, clusters, repetitive phrases, conversational routines, fixed expressions, idioms, clichés — to name but a few. Some of these terms can be distinguished according to semantic and grammatical criteria, as well as factors such as discoursal/pragmatic function and the communicative situation. For example, ‘so that’ is a multi-word grammatical item, ‘kick the bucket’ is semantically opaque or metaphorical, ‘you know’ is used as a discourse marker.[1] Other terms — lexical bundles, multi-word units, and clusters — are usually used for computationally derived groups of words, where each word is found to have a particular statistical relationship (often frequency of co-occurrence) with other words in the group. Frequency of co-occurrence is the principle that underlies the word-combinations we examine in this paper.[2] We follow Biber et al. (1999) in using the term _lexical bundle_ . 

In their _Longman Grammar of Spoken and Written English_ (1999), Biber et 

Lexical bundles are recurrent expressions, regardless of their idiomaticity, and regardless of their structural status. That is, lexical bundles are simply sequences of word forms that commonly go together in natural discourse. (Biber et al. 1999:990) 

Lexical bundles are identified empirically, as the combinations of words that in fact recur most commonly in a given register. Three-word bundles can be considered as a kind of extended collocational association, and they are thus extremely common. On the other hand, four-word, five-word, and six-word 

Lexical bundles in Early Modern English dialogues 47 

bundles are more phrasal in nature and correspondingly less common. (Biber et al. 1999:992) 

Biber et al. point out that “[u]sually they are not fixed expressions, and it is not possible to substitute a single word for the sequence; in fact, most lexical bundles are not structurally complete at all” (1999:989). For example, the lexical bundle ‘I don’t think’ is clearly structurally incomplete, lacking complementation, and is not replaceable by a single item. On the other hand, a classic idiom such as ‘a slap in the face’ is a complete noun phrase, and is replaceable by ‘an affront’. Moreover, idioms are not necessarily common: ‘I don’t think’ occurs over 400 times per million words in conversation (Biber et al. 1999:994), whereas ‘a slap in the face’ is “used occasionally in fiction (less than five per million words), but [it is] very rarely attested in the other registers (including conversation)” (Biber et al. 1999:989). 

Biber et al. derived by computer analysis the most common three-word lexical bundles in both conversation and academic prose. Table 1 displays their results. According to Biber et al. (1999:994–996), conversation has more very common lexical bundles than academic prose, and it also makes use of a larger stock of lexical bundles than academic prose. Moreover, the lexical bundles of conversation and academic prose have different structural correlates. In conversation, most lexical bundles are parts of declarative clauses (with a subject pronoun followed by an extended verb phrase) or interrogatives, and about 90% include part of a verb phrase. In academic prose, over 60% of lexical bundles are parts of noun phrases or prepositional phrases. 

In addition to these observations, two further points raised by Biber et al. are of interest to our study. Firstly, many of the lexical bundles in conversation 

Table 1. The most common three-word lexical bundles in conversation and academic prose, per million words (Biber et al. 1999:994)

|---|---|---|---|
||occurrences|occurrences||
|Conversation|_I don’t know_|_I don’t think,_|_I don’t want, don’t want to, don’t know_|
|||_do you want_|_what, and I said, I said to, I want to, you_|
||||_want to, you have to, do you know, you_|
||||_know what, have you got, what do you, I_|
||||_mean I, have a look_|
|Academic prose|–|–|_in order to, one of the, part of the, the_|
||||_number of, the presence of, the use of, the_|
||||_fact that, there is a, there is no_|



48 Jonathan Culpeper and Merja Kytö 

which consist of subject pronoun plus verb phrase can be considered what Biber et al. call _utterance launchers_ . These are “expressions which have a special function of beginning a turn or an utterance” and which generally “have a role not only in propelling the conversation in a new direction but also in providing the speaker with a planning respite, during which the rest of the utterance can be prepared for execution” (1999:1073). Secondly, Biber et al. point out that some shorter lexical bundles are incorporated into longer bundles. For example, it is clear from their results that the three-word lexical bundle ‘I don’t know’ has a relationship with longer lexical bundles: 

I don’t know I don’t know what I don’t know what to I don’t know what to do 

This is also a feature of our Early Modern English data. 

Biber et al. grouped their lexical bundles according to grammatical equivalences. This suited the goals of their project, a descriptive grammar, and the nature of their data, half of which was written academic prose. Our concerns were partly to investigate the pragmatic nature of our speech-related data, and, as a consequence, we wished to examine the functional aspects of lexical bundles. To this end, we drew inspiration from alternative approaches to word combinations represented by Aijmer (1996) and, more particularly, Moon (1998a) in their book-length studies. Although these works are based on material drawn from corpora, as is the case with Biber et al., unlike Biber et al. they pay particular attention to the communicative functions of word combinations. Though not deaf to grammatical considerations, they categorized their data according to functional equivalences. It should be noted that neither of these studies focusses on frequency-based lexical bundles. 

Aijmer (1996) examines _conversational routines_ , manually identifying speech acts and their linguistic realizations, and then doing computational work to discover the general frequencies of those linguistic items in her corpus. She provides a speech act-based pragmatic account of word-combinations which have become pragmatically specialized, that is, associated with particular pragmatic functions, such as apologizing, thanking, requesting, and offering. Examples of frequent apology phrases are ‘excuse me’ and ‘(I’m) sorry’, and of gratitude expressions ‘thank you’ and ‘thank you very much indeed’ (Aijmer 1996:44–45, 91). Moon (1994, 1998a) focusses on _fixed expressions_ and _idioms_ , that is, relatively holistic units such as ‘out of the blue’, ‘beg the question’, 

Lexical bundles in Early Modern English dialogues 49 

‘come and go’ and ‘up to scratch’. She searched her corpus with a list of items derived from the _Collins Cobuild English Language Dictionary_ (1987).[3] Her general approach is broader than Aijmer’s. According to her: 

The text functions of fixed expressions may be classified according to the way in which they contribute to the content and structure of the text. In the text under consideration, four functions are seen, according to whether the expression primarily informs (conveys new information), evaluates (conveys speaker/writer’s opinion or attitude), modalizes (conveys speaker/writer’s attitude towards the truth value of his/her utterance) or organises and functions as a discourse signal. To these four functions may be added a fifth, situationally bound, typically found in spoken interaction, and typically lexicalized as a convention or closed-set turn: this covers fixed expressions that show a speaker’s reaction to something in the extralinguistic situation, for example a greeting, valediction, apology, request or warning (…) (1994:126) 

Moon claims that her functions can be related to Halliday’s (e.g. 1978:116ff.) three semantic or functional components of language (called _metafunctions_ in systemic theory): ideational, interpersonal and textual.[4] Clearly, Moon’s informational category falls within the ideational function, and her situational, modalizing and evaluative categories fall within the interpersonal function. Moon (1994:127; also 1998a:218) indicates in a diagram that her remaining category, organizational, is also placed within the ideational function. Note here that, although the informational and the organizational categories both fall within the ideational function, they belong to different subcomponents. Halliday had proposed a sub-division for the ideational function: the experiential subcomponent relating to processes, participants and circumstances, in other words, the organization of experience (1994:106–107), and the logical subcomponent relating to “general logical relations” pertaining to groups of words (1994:179–180; see also Halliday 1978:48–49, 148–149). But Moon’s organizational category is not unproblematic. In her (1998a) monograph, she forms two sub-categories for organizational expressions: those that organize the propositional content and may indicate logical relations (for example ‘in spite of’, ‘on the grounds that’, ‘in the event’), and those that organize the text at a metadiscoursal level (for example, boundary markers such as ‘by the way’, ‘that’s that’, ‘that’s all’). Some of the organizational expressions Moon deals with seem to overlap with the textual function, which relates to the thematic, informational and cohesive structure of the message (Halliday 1994:179, 334–339). Later in her book, Moon refers back to her organizational categories and states that they “provide grammatical cohesion” (1998a:279), 

50 Jonathan Culpeper and Merja Kytö 

something which is clearly subsumed within the textual function (cf. Halliday 1978:120, 1994:334–339). The truth of the matter is that Moon’s organizational category is split over the ideational (more specifically, the logical subcomponent) and textual functions.[5] 

Finally, we should mention Bengt Altenberg’s work on the _phraseology_ of spoken English (e.g. Altenberg & Eeg-Olofsson 1990; Altenberg 1998). Altenberg focusses on frequency defined recurrent word-combinations, just as we do, and combines both grammatical analysis and functional analysis. But unlike us Altenberg prioritizes grammatical analysis. Only after distinguishing a number of broad grammatical categories (full clauses, subdivided into independent and dependent clauses; clause constituents, subdivided into multiple and single constituents; incomplete phrases) does he classify his data into major functional types (responses, subdivided into e.g. thanks, reassuring expressions, acknowledgements, agreements, disclaimers; epistemic tags; metaquestions, etc.). In line with our research goals, we prioritized functional analysis, for the simple reason that any one function is achievable by a number of different grammatical forms, and only after the formation of the functional group did we note grammatical characteristics. We also note that Altenberg’s prior grammatical analysis was only viable because considerable processing and (partly manual) filtering of his results had taken place, something which is well beyond the scope of our current paper (see Altenberg & Eeg-Olofsson 1990:6–18, for a full description of procedures). 

## 3. Data and methodology 

## 3.1 Collection of data 

In the Corpus of English Dialogues 1560–1760, trial proceedings and drama comedies are text-types in play format, the former representative of authentic and the latter of constructed language use (by _authentic language use_ we refer to speech events that, as far as court records tell us, took place in real life). So that we could more easily illuminate diachronic shifts, we created an early data set, 1560–1640, and a late data set, 1680–1760. All in all, the texts sampled amounted to nearly 460,000 words. Table 2 displays the distribution of words in the data sets. 

WeshouldacknowledgethatthelowwordcountforEarlyTrials(owingtothe great difficulty in procuring reliable records of trials for this period) will circum- 

Lexical bundles in Early Modern English dialogues 51 

Table 2. Distribution of words in our data 

|Table 2.Distribution of words in our data||
|---|---|
|Data set|Word total|
|Late Trials (1680–1760)|211,426|
|Early Trials (1560–1640)|40,727|
|Late Comedy Drama (1680–1760)|104,494|
|Early Comedy Drama (1560–1640)|102,817|
|Grand total|459,464|



scribe conclusions we can draw from that data set. Normalized figures — figures that support any comparative comments we make — are given in the Appendix. 

We extracted a list of frequently co-occurring three-word combinations with the computer program _WordSmith Tools_ . However, this was just the first step. Like Biber et al., we developed some methodology for a particular wordcombination to qualify as a lexical bundle for analysis. A lexical bundle had to recur at least 10 times, and instances had to appear in at least three different texts. Those that failed these criteria were excluded, as were lexical bundles which involved non-speech (stage directions, for example). In order to make the task of analysis manageable, we decided only to consider the top 50-ranked lexical bundles in each data set. Finally, we analysed each lexical bundle for spelling variants, and made any necessary adjustments to our frequencies. 

## 3.2 Analytical procedure 

The starting point for our classification was the data. First, we analysed a data sample, and tried to form groups according to functional equivalence. This required scrutinizing a concordance of each lexical bundle, paying attention to its discoursal, pragmatic, and textual context. The clearest groups related to particular speech acts, with relatively distinct contours in functional terms. Thus far, we proceeded very much in the manner in which Aijmer classified her data. However, after the first round of analysis, a good number of lexical bundles were left unaccounted for. We then turned to Moon and proceeded to group more of the data. In some cases, however, our data-generated groups did not fit Moon’s scheme. This is likely to be a consequence of the fact that our data is very different from Moon’s. The bulk of Moon’s 18-million-word corpus was made up of newspapers (59.5%), and there is very little transcribed speech (3.1%). As Moon (1998a:49) admits, the lack of spoken data also accounts for why she says little about speech acts. In cases where our groups did not fit Moon, we sometimes drew inspiration from Halliday, or simply devised our 

52 Jonathan Culpeper and Merja Kytö 

own group. Clearly then, the way we formed groups was primarily data-driven, whilst secondarily those groups were informed by eclectically drawing upon existing frameworks. Finally, we organized all our groups to fall within Halliday’s three functions — interpersonal, ideational and textual — which acted as broad umbrellas. We then considered the grammatical characteristics of the functional groups. We will introduce all our groups during our analysis in Section 4. 

Of course, the challenge for any functional analysis is to ensure replicability of results. A particular problem is that language is multi-functional and sometimes ambiguous. As Halliday (1973:108) comments: “In general (…) we shall not find whole sentences or even smaller structures having just one function. Typically each sentence embodies all functions, though one or another may be more prominent”. Our procedure was to identify the prominent function of a lexical bundle (if there was one) independently, and then cross-check. We also operated a criterion whereby at least 75% of the instances of a lexical bundle had to be functionally equivalent for it to gain membership to a particular category. Lexical bundles with a number of minor functional patterns were placed in our ‘mixed’ group, and lexical bundles which had no clear pattern were placed in ‘unclassified’. Needless to say, some re-thinking in terms of category formation and some reshuffling of examples between the groups was needed as new sets of data were developed. The groups we identify are overlapping and not mutually exclusive. By way of illustration, a lexical bundle can appear in more than one category. For example, in Trials the lexical bundle ‘it is not’ (39 occ. in Late Trials and 14 occ. in Early Trials) appears in the group Denials and Rejections, because it has a relatively stable and identifiable function in that discoursal context, whilst in Early Drama Comedy it appears in the category of Unclassified instances (13 occ.), because it does not overwhelmingly perform one particular function. 

## 4. Analysis and discussion 

Tables 3–6 display the results for each data set. Figures in brackets indicate the raw frequency of the lexical bundle. All in all, we included in the analysis 4,754 instances of lexical bundles (see Appendix). We have, broadly speaking, ordered our groups so that they move from Halliday’s interpersonal function, through ideational, and, towards the bottom of each table, textual (see 2 and 3.2 above). For reasons of space, we will spend most time discussing Table 3, and then for the other tables focus on comparative aspects. 

Lexical bundles in Early Modern English dialogues 53 

## 4.1 Late and Early Trials 

We will start with comments on the results obtained for Late Trials (see Table 3). Generally, we will start by offering brief definitions of the macrogroup, and then we will comment on the specific sub-groups, drawing attention to particular textual, pragmatic and grammatical characteristics. 

In Late Trials, the group Speech Act Fragments contains lexical bundles which are strongly associated with a particular speech act. For Requests, the lexical bundles capture conventionalized indirectness strategies used by the witnesses: the use of the modal ‘may’, the verb ‘desire’, and the formula to request permission ‘beg leave to’. For Commands, the two lexical bundles are clearly part of the four-word bundle ‘give an account of’, which is what we have in earlier work (cf. Culpeper & Kytö 2000b) called a _prompter_ — an imperative command given by a judge to elicit crime narrative. For Denials and Rejections, the lexical bundle ‘it is not’ quite often appears at the beginning of the witness’s turn in the form ‘It is not true/my meaning/etc’. It always negates a proposition stated or implied by the previous speaker (usually the judge). 

The group Modalizing Fragments includes such sub-groups as Downtoners/Boosters and Certainty markers. The lexical bundles included here are nearly always used by witnesses, in order to, presumably, balance the need to sound convincing with the need not to be untruthful. 

The group Discourse Fragments, in fact, relates only to parts of the adjacency pair Questions and Answers in all of our text types. Lexical bundles in Questions include _yes–no_ question fragments and _wh_ -question fragments; and lexical bundles in Answers are always negative answers. Note that some of these lexical bundles — ‘I don’t know’, ‘do you know’, ‘what do you’ — are the same as those which Biber et al. identified as particularly frequent in presentday conversation. Moreover, nearly all of these Question and Answer lexical bundles, as well as nearly all of the lexical bundles in all of the groups we have considered so far, have the structural correlates which Biber et al. found to be common in conversation, namely, declarative clauses with a subject pronoun followed by an extended verb phrase, or interrogatives. In addition, these lexical bundles almost always occur in turn-initial position. They are the utterance launchers mentioned above in Section 2 with reference to Biber et al. (1999). 

The group Narrative Fragments includes both narration of events and report of speech. With this group we move away from the interpersonal function to the ideational. We note that Halliday explicitly associated speech report with the ideational function (see 1978:49). For Narration, lexical bundles

include such features as narrative _and_ , existential _there_ , and the use of _went_ and _saw_ to describe the movements and sightings given in the accounts of the 

Lexical bundles in Early Modern English dialogues 55 

witnesses. For Reporting Clause Fragments, lexical bundles always include a subject pronoun and a speech report verb. Such lexical bundles are, of course, utterance launchers. For Reported Clause Fragments, lexical bundles are characterized by third person pronouns and the _that_ -complementizer — typical features of indirect speech report. All lexical bundles in the sub-groups here are past tense and often involve third person pronouns — well-known features of third person narration. 

The group Circumstantial Fragments contains lexical bundles giving information about the extralinguistic situation, whether the court room or the past situation described by a witness. This group relates to Halliday’s _circumstantial_ type of relational process (see, for example, 1994:106–109, 119, 130–131), which is part of the ideational (specifically, experiential) function. They are typically adverbial or prepositional in form. Three sub-groups are clearly distinguished, Place, Time, and Directional — movement towards or away from a particular entity (for example, ‘He went out of the house’). Regarding the groups Mixed and Unclassified instances, we briefly observe for Unclassified a grammatical sub-group, namely, lexical bundles with an element of the verb _be_ . 

We now proceed to discuss the results yielded by Early Trials (Table 4). In the group Speech Act Fragments, for Requests we see different lexical bundles from Late Trials. ‘I beseech you’ is a conventionalized request formula, somewhat like ‘I pray you’, but, according to the evidence we have, more formal, at least for the early period. ‘I beseech you’ only occurs sufficiently frequently to pass our criteria for inclusion in the Early Trials list of lexical bundles, whilst ‘I pray you/thee’ only achieves inclusion in Early Drama Comedy. Of course, we should remember here that our word count for Early Trials is much lower than for the other data sets (see Table 2): more data might have resulted in the inclusion of ‘I pray you/thee’ in the Early Trials table. We are on much firmer ground in pointing to the absence of ‘I pray you/thee’ in the Late Trials table. ‘I beseech you’ is a relatively independent unit, usually preceding an imperative verb, e.g. ‘I must submit my self to your Opinions: _I beseech you_ , weigh what Case I stand in’ (Trial of Norfolk, 1571, p.86). ‘Give me leave’, containing an imperative verb, is a relatively direct request for permission compared to the declarative ‘beg leave to’ (preceded in all cases by ‘I’ or ‘we’, sometimes with interpolating elements, such as modal verbs) in Late Trials. However, unlike ‘beg leave to’, it frequently occurs after the requestive frames ‘I beseech you’ or ‘I pray you’.[6] Finally, a sub-group in this section, but not in Late Trials, is Solemn Oaths. However, further exploration is needed to discover whether

oaths of this kind are generally more typical of early courtroom discourse as opposed to late. 

There are no Modalizing Fragments in Early Trials, and the group Discourse Fragments consists of three very closely related lexical bundles in the Questions sub-group — bundles which belong to the formal act of asking whether the defendant is guilty or not. Similarly, the group Narrative Fragments is reduced to one lexical bundle under Reported Clause Fragments. Of course, one reason for these low numbers of lexical bundles is that we have the fewest number of words for Early Trials, and thus not enough candidates were available to pass our criterion of 10 instances to warrant inclusion. However, normalized figures in the Appendix and other work we have done on hedges and questions would support the idea that the text type has changed (cf. Culpeper & Kytö 2000a, 2000b): the Early Trials are less interactive, hence there are fewer lexical bundles that we could label utterance launchers. 

Lexical bundles in Early Modern English dialogues 57 

Finally with regard to Early Trials, we found it necessary to introduce two new groups. Topical Fragments relate to Halliday’s _topical theme_ (see, for example, 1994:52–54), which is part of the ideational (specifically, experiential) function. As Halliday defines it, there is overlap with our Circumstantial group, since the topic can refer to the process itself, participants in the process, or the circumstances. Hence, we restrict our understanding of Topical to participants, in order to prevent that overlap. Note that examples of both the Circumstantial and Topical groups tend to be parts of complex noun phrases (usually involving a genitive or _of_ -construction) — i.e. they are elaborated packets of information. Our other new group, Organizational Fragments, organize the text or signal the discourse structure: it is a group inspired by Moon (1998a), and most clearly relates to Halliday’s textual function. The lexical bundle ‘the matter of’ signals the relevance of the following topic (cf. Moon 1998a:235); and ‘according to the’ is used to appeal to an authority (often a particular law). 

Finally, viewing the grammatical mix in Early Trials, we note a proportional increase in lexical bundles which are parts of noun phrases or prepositional phrases compared with Late Trials. Remember that these were the structures that Biber et al. associated with academic prose. Again, we appear to have evidence the text type has become more conversational over time. 

## 4.2 Late and Early Drama Comedy 

We now turn to Drama Comedy (see Tables 5 and 6), and a much less formal kind of discourse, often taking place in a domestic setting. 

As with Trials, the majority of the lexical bundles in the first halves of the tables, working downwards, can be described as utterance launchers. However, we have lexical bundles associated with different speech acts. Assertions appear in both Late and Early Drama. ‘I warrant you’ of Early Drama and ‘I assure you’ of Late Drama are functionally equivalent: both are used by the speaker to offer a guarantee. Both lexical bundles appear in our lists for early and late periods. However, ‘I assure you’ fails to make it above our threshold of 10 instances for the early period, and ‘I warrant you’ fails to make it above our threshold of 10 instances for the late period. One possibility is that ‘I assure you’ has been taking over the function of ‘I warrant you’. Supporting evidence is the fact that there are more than twice as many ‘I warrant you’ instances in the whole Shakespeare corpus compared with ‘I assure you’. Also, ‘I assure you’ seems a less conventionalized unit: it was often more integrated into the grammar with

In Early Drama, lexical bundles associated with two other specific speech acts — Requests and Thanks — appear. The requestive frame ‘I pray you/thee’ was clearly alive and well in this period. One reason why it might not appear in Late Drama is the development of shortened forms like ‘prithee’ or ‘pray’, which, of course, would not show up in our data (cf. Busse 1998). Regarding Thanks, we will briefly note that the lexical bundle ‘I thank you/thee’ occurs 30 times in our early data, but only six times in our late, suggesting a decline in this specific thanking formula. 

For the group Modalizing Fragments, we note a wider array of lexical bundles and sub-groups compared to Trials. This is perhaps not surprising, given the relatively restricted nature of courtroom discourse. Specific discourse 

60 Jonathan Culpeper and Merja Kytö 

fragments rarely occur in drama, early or late. We expected to find fragments of greeting routines, for example. And perhaps we will when we study two- and four-word lexical bundles. Similarly, narrative fragments are rare. Third-person narrative clearly does not play the central role here that it plays in Trials. The single example we do have — ‘I told you’ — is usually a reminder, often with accusatory tone, of what a character has said. Of course, this also functions to remind the audience. 

We had to develop a new group for Drama Comedy, both late and early, namely, Expressives (clearly, an interpersonal group). Given that we are dealing with a genre which is designed to be dramatic, this is no surprise. Instances of ‘for heaven’s sake’, not uncommon in everyday conversation today, already emerge in our Late Drama (12 occurrences). 

Regarding the Unclassified group, we shall briefly note that Drama Comedy, both early and late, is quite unlike the other text types in that in the ‘be’ fragments sub-group the first person pronoun often precedes the ‘be’ element. Many of the instances of these lexical bundles — indeed, all of the instances of ‘I am a’ — are followed by a noun phrase. Self-characterization seems to be a key aspect of this text type. 

## 5. Conclusion 

In this study we have revealed the three-word lexical bundles that characterize our data. More specifically, we have shown how they relate to specific functions in our four data sets. We have also shown how lexical bundles distribute across our text-types and commented briefly on their grammatical properties. We have also presented evidence for diachronic shifts, specifically with regard to trial proceedings, which contain a higher density of features characteristic of spoken interaction (e.g. utterance launchers) in later periods. Moreover, our data attest lexical bundles — ‘I don’t know’, ‘do you know’, ‘what do you’ — identified as particularly frequent in present-day conversation. 

A particular thread through the lexical bundles in our data is the use of parenthetical _subject pronoun + present tense verb + second person pronoun_ constructions, as in ‘I pray you’, ‘I beseech you’, ‘I warrant you’, ‘I assure you’, ‘I tell you’, ‘I thank you’ and ‘I promise you’ — all examples in our data. Of course, each of these merits detailed study. However, we have already identified some characteristics. Our evidence suggests that high situational formality may partly determine the use of ‘I beseech you’, as opposed to ‘I pray you’. We have 

Lexical bundles in Early Modern English dialogues 61 

further evidence that some parentheticals can take over the function of other parentheticals, as ‘I assure you’ seems to take over the function of ‘I warrant you’ in relation to the speech act of assertion. A possible explanation for this particular shift is that ‘I warrant you’ may have become socially stigmatized, whereas ‘I assure you’, with its Latinate heritage, still had strong associations with the legal register.[7] (Consider the fondness of the Nurse in Shakespeare’s _Romeo and Juliet_ for the expression ‘I warrant (you)’). We have also provided evidence of the currency of particular parentheticals in particular periods, thus ‘I pray you/thee’ was frequent in Early Drama, but ‘I thank you’ was in decline by Late Drama. Finally, the fact that these parentheticals are such a strong characteristic of our data, but not at all a characteristic of present-day conversation (cf. Biber et al. 1999), is something that deserves explanation. Busse (1998), discussing the decline of ‘I pray you/thee’ and the rise of ‘please’, points out that this involves a shift from orientation towards the speaker to orientation towards the hearer, and further mentions in a footnote that this may relate to a shift from a positive politeness culture to a negative one (cf. Kopytko 1993). Our evidence certainly supports the idea that the frames for speech acts were often speaker oriented, and thus is possible evidence for a positive politeness culture.

* We are grateful to Teresa Fanego for valuable comments on an earlier version of this paper. 

1. See Moon (1998a:2–25, 1998b) for further examples and a fuller description of many of the terminological categories mentioned here. 

2. For further discussion of the concept of _recurrent word combination_ , and in particular how it relates to the notion of _collocation_ , see Altenberg & Eeg-Olofsson (1990:3–5). 

3. One might note here that Moon’s decision to base her searches on a list from the _Collins Cobuild English Language Dictionary_ (1987) was partly influenced by the fact that she had privileged access to the data of that dictionary, having been one of its editors. Of course, that dictionary is itself corpus-based. However, there is no precise information in the dictionary on how fixed expressions were retrieved. What we do know is that there is no simple computational means available. For an illuminating discussion of the computational difficulties relating to retrieving fixed expressions from a corpus, see Moon (1998a:Chapter 3). 

4. Halliday (1978) contains a somewhat fuller exposition of his functions compared with more recent work. We note that his more recent work in functional grammar (e.g. 1994) maintains his view of the functions as mapped out in (1978). 

5. This is not to say that the problem is simply Moon’s. Widdowson (2000:15–17), for example, notes particular problems with separating out the textual function from the ideational and interpersonal. 

6. The fact that we say here that ‘I beseech you’ and ‘I pray you’ occur in the Late Trials data is not incompatible with early statements in this paragraph: they occur in Late Trials but not sufficiently frequently to be counted as lexical bundles for inclusion in our tables. 

7. We are grateful to Vivian Salmon for this observation.

- Culpeper, Jonathan & Merja Kytö. 2000b. “Gender Voices in the Spoken Interaction of the Past: A Pilot Study Based on Early Modern English Trial Proceedings”. _The History of English in a Social Context_ ed. by Dieter Kastovsky & Arthur Mettinger, 53–89. Berlin & New York: Mouton de Gruyter.

   - _Meaning_ . London: Edward Arnold.
