## Changing documentation in the Third Edition of the _Oxford English Dictionary_ Sixteenth-century vocabulary as a test case[*] 

## Philip Durkin 

Oxford English Dictionary 

## 1. Background 

Attempts to characterize the development of the vocabulary of English in various historical periods have, understandably, often taken as their basis the documentation provided by the _Oxford English Dictionary_ ( _OED_ ). The methodology has typically been to take a sample of all or some of the words with a first date falling within a given period, and to compile statistics based on the etymologies provided for these items by the _OED_ . For the Early Modern period, Barber (1976) does this for a 2% sample of the full text of the _OED_ , taken directly from the printed version of the first edition; in the second edition of his book (1997) he supplements this with a slightly smaller sample of words with sixteenth- and seventeenth-century first attestations drawn from the CD-ROM of the second edition of the _OED_ . A much larger scale study is that of Wermser (1976), although this is not based directly on _OED_ data, relying instead on the _Chronological English Dictionary_ ( _CED_ ) of Finkenstaedt, Leisi & Wolff (1970), which in turn uses the data of the _Shorter Oxford English Dictionary_ ( _SOED_ ) as its basis. Wermser’s data thus ultimately derives from the _OED_ , as this is the basis of the _SOED_ . However, _SOED_ is a work of abridgement, as is shown in its selection of lemmas to include from the parent work and _CED_ in turn includes only _SOED’_ s headwords, excluding all subsidiary lemmas; as an illustration of the degree of selection involved, only 36% of the _OED2_ lemmas included in the present survey appear in _CED_ .[1] _SOED’_ s abridgement of _OED_ is also evident in its often greatly curtailed treatment of etymologies, although this is rectified in Wermser’s work, where _OED’_ s etymologies are used (although only for a 

66 Philip Durkin 

_CED_ -derived wordlist). Wermser’s data, and to a lesser extent Barber’s also, has been used extensively in studies of the vocabulary of Early Modern English, as for instance in Görlach (1991) and Nevalainen (1999), although not without significant reservations as to the nature of the data. 

Work has now been going on for several years on the third edition of the _OED_ ( _OED3_ ), which will ultimately provide the first ever full, top-to-bottom, revision of the dictionary, taking as its starting point the integrated edition, _OED2_ , of 1989 which brought together the first edition, the last fascicle of which was published in 1928, and its four-volume supplement of 1972–1986. The preliminary results of the _OED3_ revision work are being published in quarterly releases of at present a little over 1,000 entries per quarter. The present study will examine the results of this revision work in the first two releases of revised material, comprising the alphabetical sequence from m, where revision work began, to mamzer. This is admittedly a very small portion of the dictionary as a whole, but it provides sufficient material to illustrate how revision work on all areas of the text, and for present purposes especially the quotation evidence and the etymologies, is transforming the dictionary’s record of the vocabulary of English. Within these alphabetical ranges of material, I have chosen to restrict my survey to items having a sixteenth-century first date in _OED2_ or _OED3_ , or in both. The selection of the sixteenth century as the sample period is influenced by the attention that the Early Modern period has attracted in studies such as those mentioned above, and also by the awareness of the possibilities of antedating the _OED_ record that has already been created by work such as that of Schäfer (1980, 1989) or McConchie (1997). However, within the Early Modern period the selection only of those words first attested within a particular century is motivated simply by the desire to provide a sample of a convenient size for detailed examination; given this restriction, the sixteenth century seems a particularly attractive candidate for this sort of close attention, in the light of the sharply increasing rates both of new word formation in general and of borrowing as a percentage of the overall increase which Wermser’s analysis suggests for almost the whole of the century.[2] 

## 2. Changes to the dating of words 

The combined total of items with sixteenth-century first quotations in either _OED2_ or _OED3_ in the range m-mamzer comes to 310, containing 245 items which have sixteenth-century first dates in _OED2_ and 283 which have sixteenth- 

Changing documentation in the Third Edition of the _OED_ 67 

century first dates in _OED3_ . As will be seen immediately from the high combined total of 310, there have been a good many losses from the _OED2_ list as well as additions to the _OED3_ list. The lists include all lemmas in the alphabetical range with sixteenth-century first dates, including derivatives and compounds which are subsumed under a parent headword; this is because the presentation of such items as subsumed lemmas in _OED_ is chiefly a matter of convenience of presentation (to prevent proliferation of very short entries), and does not have any particular significance for the historical linguist. (Although not all such items are given explicit etymologies in _OED3_ , an etymology is given wherever a foreign or internal parallel may be suspected to have provided a model for the formation, and therefore all such items not given etymologies may be assumed to be, on present knowledge, straightforward compounds or derivatives). Similarly, multiple parts of speech are treated each as a separate item, regardless of whether they have separate headword status in the dictionary. 

Appendix A gives the full list, divided into the following categories: (1) items with unchanged sixteenth-century first date in both _OED2_ and _OED3_ ; (2) items antedated within the sixteenth century; (3) items with a sixteenth-century first date in _OED3_ as a result of antedating; (4) items no longer having a sixteenth-century first date in _OED3_ as a result of antedating; (5) items with a re-dated first quotation but remaining in the sixteenth century; (6) items no longer having a sixteenth-century first date in _OED3_ as a result of re-dating of the existing first quotation; (7) items now having a sixteenth-century first date in _OED3_ as a result of re-dating of the existing first quotation; (8) items with _OED2’_ s first quotation rejected; (9) newly added items. 

There are thus two main categories of date change: antedatings, i.e. cases where an earlier first citation has now been added (categories 2–4), and redatings of the existing first quotation (5–7). 

Taking each of these categories in turn, for the Early Modern period the most fruitful sources of antedatings are: firstly, quotations elsewhere in the _OED_ itself; secondly, our own file collections, including the fruits of reading of a good deal of primary material for this period, especially non-literary sources such as wills, inventories, etc., as well as published antedatings in such collections as Schäfer (1980, 1989) or Bailey (1978), or the many smaller collections published over the years in the journal _Notes and Queries_ ; thirdly, the historical dictionaries, the _Middle English Dictionary_ ( _MED_ ), the _Dictionary of the Older Scottish Tongue_ ( _DOST_ ) and also, very significantly, the quotation data collected for the now abandoned period dictionary of Early Modern English, another of the projects which, like _MED_ and _DOST_ , grew out of a series of initiatives by Sir 

68 Philip Durkin 

William Craigie, one of the _OED’_ s four original editors; and finally, and increasingly, electronic corpora, especially for this period Chadwyck-Healey’s _Literature Online_ .[3] 

The second, perhaps less familiar, major category of date changes consists of cases where the existing first quotation has been re-dated on bibliographical grounds. This is commonly the case with citations from Shakespeare’s plays, which are for _OED3_ dated by the date of the earliest print in which the word in question occurs, rather than as in _OED2_ by the presumed date of first performance (as for instance at madcap _a_ .; all of the examples in my list in fact show relatively small adjustments, as the items in question are all now supported by the earliest relevant quarto). Unlike antedatings, bibliographical re-datings most commonly result in a later dating than that in _OED2_ , although a rather dramatic counter-example is provided by mahonnet _n_ ., where bibliographical work on the single citation now gives a date 75 years earlier than that in _OED2_ . (In a number of cases, the re-dated quotation is now replaced by another citation, earlier than the revised date but still later than the _OED2_ date; in my list I have simply described such instances as re-datings where the net result is a date later than that in _OED2_ , reserving the term ‘antedating’ for cases where an earlier date results overall). The bibliographical revision of citations for _OED3_ is in fact an ongoing process, working concurrently with other revisions of entries, but proceeding work by work rather than alphabetically entry by entry. This will lead in course of time to further changes in some of the entries discussed here: for instance, at machete _n_ . further bibliographical work on the writings drawn together by Hakluyt has led to further revision of the first date for this word to _c_ 1575 (rather than 1589 as in Appendix A.5 below), and this will be reflected by further updating of the _OED3_ entry during the course of 2001. 

There is also one item, majolica _n_ ., which stands alone in this sample as a case where the first edition’s first quotation has been rejected as not being a valid illustration of English use (being taken instead simply to show use of the parent toponym in an English context). 

Of the 245 _OED2_ items with sixteenth-century first dates, 142 (58%) have the first date unchanged in _OED3_ , while the remaining 42% have changed first dates, 61 (25% of the total) as a result of antedating, 41 (17%) as a result of redating of the existing first quotation, and one as a result of the existing first quotation being rejected. Among those with changed first dates, 27 (11% of the total) as a result no longer have sixteenth-century first dates. (For more detail on the size of the dating changes, see the ‘before’ and ‘after’ dates given for each item in Appendix A). 

Changing documentation in the Third Edition of the _OED_ 69 

Of the 283 _OED3_ items with sixteenth-century first dates, 218 (77%) are inherited from _OED2_ (albeit in many cases with changed first dates), while 38 (13.5%) have entered the sixteenth century as a result of antedating and four (1.5%) as a result of re-dating of the existing first quotation. 23 items (8%) are newly added (see Appendix A.9 for full listing of these).[4] 

Concealed behind my figures for _OED2_ changes — and allowing a slight excursus outside the sixteenth century — are a number of items which have ‘leapfrogged’ the sixteenth century entirely, and which I have listed in Appendix B. Eight items here are antedated from the seventeenth century to the fifteenth, and two right from the nineteenth century to the fifteenth. Interestingly, six of these items are now earliest exemplified in medical contexts, four having their earliest citations from one of the English Chauliac translations. 

Wermser’s data is based on fifteen-year sample periods from within centuries, including two sixteenth-century sample periods, 1510–1524 and 1560–1574. Here my sample becomes very small, but within these two subperiods taken together, _OED2_ has a total of 46 items, of which only 29 (63%) remain within the same sub-period in _OED3_ , where they are joined by a further 20 items which are new or have revised dates, so that only 59% of the tokens in the relevant sub-periods in the _OED3_ data remain from _OED2_ . 

To add an etymological slant, one may perhaps wonder whether the changes in the data would be found to affect largely items of a particular type of etymology, with perhaps fairly trivial compounds proving more volatile in terms of their dating than foreign loanwords (as might be the case if such items were assumed to have been less well represented by the reading undertaken for the first edition of the _OED_ ). Taking the loanwords in my range m-mamzer in isolation suggests that this is not the case. There are 102 items in the sample which are taken to be (entirely or partly; definitely, probably, or perhaps) loanwords in _OED3_ (so almost exactly a third of the complete sample).[5] Of these, 80 have sixteenthcentury first dates in _OED2_ , among which 44 (55%) have an unchanged first date in _OED3_ , while 24 (30%) have been antedated and 12 (15%) re-dated, leading to 13 items (16% of the total) no longer having sixteenth-century first dates as a result of either antedating or re-dating. In _OED3_ the remaining 67 items are joined by four newly added items, 13 antedatings, and two re-datings. 

Looking again at the finer detail of Wermser’s fifteen-year sample periods 1510–1524 and 1560–1574, one finds nine loanwords remaining in the same fifteen-year period in _OED3_ as in _OED2_ , but seven leaving the relevant period, while another seven items enter one of the two periods. So at this level of fine detail, only 56% of the sample tokens are common to the _OED2_ and _OED3_ data. 

70 Philip Durkin 

Loanwords thus show changes very similar to the average for the sample as a whole, and the overall rate of change is sufficient to demonstrate that considerable caution should be exercised when using _OED2_ dates for sixteenth-century items for statistical purposes. A slightly wider lesson to draw would be that any dictionary dates should be treated with a certain amount of caution, except where facts made explicit about a particular word’s history help to support the date given. (Overall, approximately a third of _OED2_ words and senses are being antedated during the course of work on _OED3_ , and it is hardly to be expected that many of the dates arrived at for _OED3_ will never prove capable of further improvement, although the work done for _OED3_ should at least ensure that the likely margin of future antedating is substantially diminished). 

## 3. Changes to etymologies 

Aside from changes to the dating of items, the etymological component of each _OED_ entry is also being completely reviewed, often giving rise to changes which have implications for surveys such as those of Wermser and Barber. 

Firstly, there are in _OED3_ 23 newly added items with sixteenth-century first dates, comprising nine compounds (such as macedonian phalanx _n_ .), seven derivatives (including two straightforward derivatives, machiavellized _a_ . and malicefully _adv_ ., and five where the first component is a name, for instance magdeburgian _n._ and _a_ ., or magnesian _n. and a_ .[1] ), three items formed by conversion (e.g. malicious _n_ .), and four loanwords (macrocephalus _n_ ., macte _int_ ., makaf _n_ ., and mamertine _n_ .). 

Of the remaining 287 inherited items, 252 have essentially the same basic etymology as in _OED2_ — that is to say, the outline transmission is the same, even where, as typically for _OED3_ , a good deal of further information has been added, including cases where a significant foreign-language parallel is now noted. For instance at mahometize _v_ . a sixteenth-century French parallel is now noted which could perhaps have provided the model for the English word (although this is presented no more strongly than with ‘Cf.’ because the dating of the French word within the sixteenth century is uncertain). Similarly, maintainment _n_ . is now presented as perhaps after Anglo-Norman and Middle French parallel forms, while post-classical Latin and Spanish analogues are also presented:[6] 

Changing documentation in the Third Edition of the _OED_ 71 

[< maintain _v._ + -ment, perh. after Anglo-Norman _maintenement_ , _maintement_ aid, preservation, champerty, Middle French, French _maintenement_ protection, support. Cf. post-classical Latin _manutenementum_ support, champerty (from _c_ 1140 in British sources); also Spanish _mantenimiento_ mantiniment _n._ ] 

Thirty-five items, or 12% of the inherited items, do have substantial changes in the basic etymology, sufficient in most cases (depending on how qualifying comments such as ‘prob.’ and ‘perh.’ are interpreted) to change their assignment in the broad categorizations found in Wermser and Barber (broadly, into loanwords, derivatives, compounds and conversions or derivatives by zeromorpheme, although with slight differences of detail in each case), or to change the immediate donor language for a loanword (see the further breakdown of loanwords by immediate donor language given by both Wermser and Barber).[7] See Appendix C for the full listing. 

Six items show changes in the method of internal formation, with for instance madbrain _a_ . now etymologized as a compound rather than as a conversion from the corresponding noun (which is in fact attested only slightly later); similarly at mail _v_ .[3] and main _n_ .[3] probable conversion replaces the former “origin obscure”. Three entries are conversely now given a clear initial statement that the etymology is unknown or uncertain, hence normally disqualifying them from further consideration in this sort of broad statistical approach (cf. Barber 1997:220). 

Of the remainder, seven items are newly identified as loanwords, while four items are no longer given as loanwords, and a further five items have the immediate donor language changed. 

Among those items newly identified as loanwords, maecenas _n_ ., magellan _n_ . and malabar _n_ . are all presented in _OED2_ (implicitly) as being derived directly from names, whereas now earlier use in the same extended uses can be shown to have existed earlier in other languages and hence in each case a loanword etymology now appears much more likely than independent derivation directly from the name. This well illustrates a fundamental principle in _OED3’_ s etymologies, that the etymology will never (as sometimes in _OED2_ ) seek to present only the ‘underlying’ or ultimate etymology, but will always endeavour to give all of the stages in the transmission of the word in as much detail as possible. So at maecenas _n_ . the _OED3_ etymology documents the fact that the generic use is found already in classical Latin and also in Middle French (with the date for use in Middle French being given, as the periodization of Middle French adopted extends to the end of the sixteenth century and a more 

72 Philip Durkin 

precise dating is therefore required to demonstrate that the French word is attested earlier than the English one), hence the revised etymology is presented as either from Middle French or directly from the Latin word in generic use:[8] 

[< Middle French _mecenas_ (1526; French † _mécénas_ , later _Mécène_ (1559)) or its etymon classical Latin _Maece¯na¯s_ patron of literature < the name of Gaius _Maece¯na¯s_ (d. 8 B.C.), a Roman knight, friend of Augustus, statesman, and patron of Horace and Virgil.] 

Among items no longer identified as loanwords, I would pick out major _n_ .[2] (formerly major _n_ .[1] ; the military rank). Here a substantial antedating of the English word, from 1643 to 1575, combines with dating of the French word to 1660 (provided by modern lexicographical resources for French unavailable to the original editors of _OED_ ) to demonstrate quite conclusively that the _OED2_ etymology as a French loan cannot stand; hence the revised etymology as a shortening of _sergeant-major_ , perhaps after use of the corresponding Spanish word in similar senses (again giving an indication of date for these, as also for the Dutch and German parallels): 

[Shortened < sergeant-major _n._ , perh. after Spanish _mayor_ major _a._ and _n._[1] (16th cent. in the senses ‘commander, quartermaster’; now only in American Spanish, in which it may have been reintroduced from English). Cf. Dutch _majoor_ (earlier also _major_ ) and German _Major_ (replacing the older term _Oberstwachtmeister_ ), both late 16th cent. < Spanish, and evidently dating from the Dutch war of independence; also French _major_ (1660), Italian _maggiore_ (1618).] 

Similarly, among those items where the immediate donor language has changed, I would draw attention to madefaction _n_ ., where the date now available for the French word (1765, as opposed to 1583 for the English word) again demonstrates that it cannot have been the English word’s etymon unless there is a major gap in the French lexicographical record. 

Another common type of entry where the immediate donor language has changed is where an ‘exotic’ loan can now be shown to be via a European intermediary, as at macana _n_ ., where intermediary transmission of a Taino word via Spanish is identified on the basis of dating provided by Spanish dictionaries combined with the obvious historical context. Here one might argue that in fact _OED3_ is rather providing a greater level of detail than revising the underlying etymology, although for basic statistical analyses of the immediate donor language of loanwords the result is the same. 

Changing documentation in the Third Edition of the _OED_ 73 

In addition to lexicographical resources available for other languages, another major tool is often waiting to be unlocked in our own English quotation data, in the form of citations which are from translated sources. Clearly each needs to be researched carefully in order to ascertain what the reading of the translated source text is (a procedure which will often generate complex bibliographical problems of its own), but the fruits of such work are shown by for instance magusian _n_ ., where checking the French source of the single citation demonstrates that _OED2’_ s etymology as an English derivative formation (magus _n._ + -ian) was in need of review: 

[< Middle French _Magusien_ (1581 de Mornay) < classical Latin _magus_ magus _n._ + French _-ien_ -ian.] = magian _n._ 1587 SIR P. SIDNEY & A. GOLDING tr. P. de Mornay _Trewnesse Christian Relig._ xxxiii. 616 The Magusians (…) are giuen to Incest after the custome of their Moother country Persia.] 

In this same sample an almost identical case is provided also by mahonnet _n_ ., where _OED2_ ’s etymology as a derivative of mahone _n_ . is also to be rejected on the grounds of the relative dating of the two words (1524 for mahonnet _n_ . but only 1572 for mahone _n_ .). 

In the examples considered so far, the importance thus emerges of documentation, and especially dating, for foreign-language cited forms, whether this is derived from other lexicographical resources or from research arising from the English citations themselves.[9] (Occasionally the preferred option will be to present a word as derived from another word, English or foreign-language, which is in fact attested only later, but this will in all cases be flagged very clearly in the _OED3_ etymology). 

The final significant category of changes to etymologies is where the etymology is now presented as ‘mixed’, whether partly from one immediate donor and partly from another, or partly a loanword and partly an internal development (and in some instances for ‘partly’ substitute ‘either/or’, both types being found in the present sample). Coleman (1995) notes for fifteenthcentury items the increased incidence of this sort of etymology in _MED_ entries as compared with _OED2_ , and such etymologies are certainly increasing in _OED3_ for both the Middle English and Early Modern periods.[10] In this sample there are eight items which are for the first time presented as partly from one immediate donor and partly (or alternatively) from another, and five items which are for the first time given mixed internal and/or loanword origin. 

74 Philip Durkin 

For the first type, I would draw attention again to maecenas _n_ . (quoted above), where both the Middle French word and its classical Latin etymon are presented as possible etymons; very possibly, both have played a part in the development of the English word. In fact, this is fairly certain in the case of the Latin word, since checking the original of the added first quotation (1542 N. Udall tr. Erasmus _Apophthegmes_ ) shows that, although Udall’s translation is often very loose, here it does reflect use of _Maecenas_ in Erasmus’ Latin text. Similarly at mace _n_ .[3] we have a further example of how checking of a translated source provides probable evidence of mixed descent (unlike macana _n_ ., here retaining the possibility that immediate contact with Malay is likely to be responsible at least for some of the later uses): 

[< early modern Dutch _maes_ ( _masen_ (plural) in source translated in quot. 1598) or its etymon Malay _mas_ < Sanskrit _ma¯sha_ (see masha _n._ ).] 

In the remaining category, items now given mixed internal and/or loanword origin, I would draw attention finally to malevolent _a_ ., where _OED2’_ s French loanword etymology is rejected on the grounds of the infrequent attestation of the most closely matching French form in the relevant period, with the English word now being presented as either a loan directly from classical Latin or alternatively as a formation from the already well-established English noun (and hence as an early native formation in -ent on the pattern of loaned pairs of - _ence_ / - _ent_ words from French and Latin): 

[Either < malevolence _n._ (see -ent) or < classical Latin _malevolent-_ , _malevolens_ ill-disposed, spiteful < _male_ ill (see mal-) + _volens_ willing (see volent _a._ and _n._ ); cf. _malevolus_ (see malevolous _a._ ). Cf. earlier benevolent _a._ ; classical Latin _malevolens_ is contrasted with _benevolens_ already in Ennius (3rd-2nd cent. B.C.). Middle French _malivolent_ is recorded twice in 16th cent.; cf. the more common Anglo-Norman _malvoillaunt_ , _mauvuilant_ , Old French _mauvoillant_ (12th cent.), Middle French, French _malveillant_ . 

In sense A. 2 prob. after post-classical Latin _malevolus_ malevolous _a._ in this sense.] 

The supposition that the word is an internal formation is perhaps supported by the fact that the earliest attestation of the English adjective is in Barclay’s translation ultimately of Brandt’s _Das Narrenschiff_ , where the corresponding noun is also attested and illustrated in the _OED_ entry: in this instance, checking the Latin and French translations of this text likely to have been used by Barclay 

Changing documentation in the Third Edition of the _OED_ 75 

as his immediate source reveals no corresponding word (although it is perhaps notable that Barclay’s syntax here shows postposition of the adjective, “worde malyuolent”, perhaps suggesting that the word is apprehended as belonging ultimately to Romance lexis, even if a precise foreign-language model may not in fact have existed). 

## 4. Conclusions 

The _OED_ provides very full documentation for the vocabulary of the Early Modern period, now improved greatly by fresh targeted reading of additional (especially non-literary) sources, the fruits of the secondary scholarship of the last century, and the collections prepared for the Early Modern English Dictionary project. I hope to have shown how an abridged wordlist, such as one extracted from _SOED_ via _CED_ , will not give nearly so clear a picture of the vocabulary of the period (especially when one bears in mind that a major factor in _SOED’_ s inclusion policy is the survival of the word to the present day, rather than its currency and frequency in Early Modern English). I hope also to have shown something of the extent of the changes in _OED3’_ s documentation for this period, both in revisions to dating (through addition of new material and reassessment of existing data) and through changes to etymologies. 

Beyond this, I hope also to have demonstrated some of the possible pitfalls in making uncritical use of dictionary data, especially where complex data such as that provided by the _OED_ is to be used for statistical purposes. The dates provided by historical dictionaries are the best that have been arrived at after a process of research (in the cases of both the first edition of the _OED_ and _OED3_ , two of the biggest research projects that have ever been undertaken in the humanities); however, as comparison of the dating of words in _OED2_ and _OED3_ shows,thedatesgivenwilloftenbesusceptibletosubstantialrevisionwhenfurther systematic research is undertaken. Caution is therefore advisable when making use of such data for statistical purposes, combined if possible with an awareness of what data is available to the historical lexicographer for each period. 

Likewise, although perhaps not so often remarked upon previously, any historical dictionary’s etymologies (or indeed those of a more narrowly etymological dictionary) are also dependent on the available data, which is greatly improved since the compilation of the first edition of _OED_ , but is also likely to show further substantial improvement in the future (especially in the area of historical dictionaries and corpora for major donor languages).[11] I hope to have 

76 Philip Durkin 

illustrated the extent to which the richer and fuller data available to _OED3_ in this area, as well as a tighter methodological assumption that etymological argument should always take full account of the available data, in fact leads to an increasing number of cases where some honest doubt must be entertained as to a word’s immediate origin. Such honest doubt, conveyed through alternative etymological explanations, and through qualifying adverbs such as ‘probably’ and ‘perhaps’, clearly presents difficulties for a simple count of different etymology typesrepresentedinagivenhistoricalperiod,butsuchdoubtsareconveyedbythe lexicographer as a result of close attention to the available data, and to neglect them is to leave out of consideration an important part of the dictionary’s documentation. A major aim of _OED3_ is to make the dictionary’s methodology more transparent at all levels, and particularly in the etymology section of each entry; it is to be hoped that this, together with the revised data, will provide a powerful tool for future studies of the development of English lexis. 

## Appendices 

## _Appendix A_ 

1. _Items with unchanged sixteenth-century first date in both_ OED2 _and_ OED3 _(_ OED3 _headword form given where this differs; date of first attestation follows after the headword):_ ma. _n_ . 1579, mab _n_ . (now mab _n_ .[1] ) 1557–8, macaronical _a_ . 1585, mace _n_ .[3] 1598, macerate _v_ . 1547, macerated _ppl. a_ . ## Notes 

* I am very grateful to John Simpson and Edmund Weiner, respectively Chief Editor and Principal Philologist of _OED3_ , for having read drafts of this article and made many useful suggestions. Responsibility for any errors of course remains my own. 

1. In this connection compare Nevalainen (1999:454, fn.3) on the possible effects on Wermser’s data of the extremely selective _CED_ -derived wordlist. (Although my sample is very small and restricted only to a single century, it is perhaps of interest to note in this connection that it gives a figure for the proportion of loanwords as a percentage of the whole which is very similar to Barber’s, and not Wermser’s). 

2. See Wermser (1976:24, 42). 

3. On the projected Early Modern English Dictionary and the history of its materials, see Aitken (1987:96–98); on the use now being made for _OED3_ of the materials prepared for this dictionary see Durkin (1999:32–33). 

4. Among these, four items were illustrated in _OED2_ but not lemmatized, while two were lemmatized but did not have their own illustrative quotation paragraph, making inclusion in this sort of analysis impossible. Two items were first added in the _OED Additions Series_ in 1997, and are now integrated into the main sequence for the first time in _OED3_ . 

5. In order to facilitate comparison with Wermser’s study I include for present purposes in the count of loanwords formations from a foreign-language base plus an English suffix, but omit formations from English elements on the model of a foreign-language word (see Wermser 1976:33–37). See Görlach (1991:138) for criticism of Wermser’s practice in this area. 

6. Cf. note 5 above on the question of whether such items should be considered loanwords. 

7. Barber (1997:219–220) implies that etymologies beginning with ‘perh.’ or ‘prob.’ are included in his study: “For some words the OED editors consider more than one possible etymology: if they consider one particular etymology as the likeliest, that etymology is accepted for the sample”. Wermser (1976:44) likewise ignores all such qualifying formulae. 

8. All quotations taken from OED Online (www.oed.com). 

9. For an overview of available lexicographical resources for foreign-language cited forms see Durkin (1999). 

10. Barber also notes the existence of etymologies given from either French or Latin (Barber 1997:228), but takes them to be so rare as to be statistically irrelevant in his sample. This would not be the case for those parts of _OED3_ so far completed. 

11. See Durkin (1999) for discussion of some major projects in progress which will have a significant impact for _OED’_ s etymological work.
