# A linguistic history of advertising, 1700–1890 

## Manfred Görlach 

University of Cologne 

## 1. Introduction 

## 1.1 General remarks 

The modern period of English has created a large number of new types of communication; this is an obvious consequence of new media, of the increase and international differentiation of written or printed texts and of the effects of a growing (social and geographical) mobility of people and the numerical increase of the messages transmitted. In the course of events, some text types have entirely disappeared, their functions and names having been superseded by modern developments, and have thereby become historical, as has recently happened to the telegram. On the other hand, the emergence of new text types is a much more frequent phenomenon and it is also in many ways more remarkable. One of the most striking instances is the rise of the modern newspaper. This can be seen as a conglomerate supertype as well as a cluster of more or less clearly distinct individual text types which have come to form a symbiotic ecosystem. Clearly each individual text type can have a definite position allocated to it and can have formed, over the last three centuries, specific forms conventionalized between writers/compilers and readers. Instances of such individual types are the leader, political comment, news report, weather forecast, letter to the editor, astrological prognostication, birth and death notices, obituary, cartoon, crossword puzzle, classified, and of course the commercial advertisement. Research into what defines a text type functionally and with which linguistic features the type is correlated (and why) and how the form and concept have changed over time, have been among the most rewarding fields of recent linguistic investigation, a quest that is only beginning 

84 Manfred Görlach 

to provide a comprehensive view of the discipline in its synchronic and diachronic, national and international perspectives (cf. Görlach 2001), especially when we contrast the rich tradition of research into literary genres already available. Discussing the history of an English text type has a particular relevance for me, because the first contribution to the discipline that I made took the form of a paper at the 6th ICEHL (May 1990) at Helsinki (published as Görlach 1992, and partly reprinted in Görlach 1995b) in which I developed a typology of text types before I exemplified my reflections by way of a detailed analysis of the cooking recipe and the cookery book. 

## 1.2 Text types and their names 

It can be expected that when a conventional link becomes established between a textual function and a certain form, this combination will be given a name designating the genre. Therefore, legal forms of written documents like a law, bill, last will, etc. have well-defined conventions attached to them, and though in non-technical speech text types may be less well-defined, they are normally describable with a set of features which can be extracted from semantic contrasts of their _signifiés_ (cf. Görlach 2001, where more than 2,000 designations of text types are listed). 

As with all other lexical items, forms and contents can change over time. Thus, types can split (a cooking _recipe_ now being named differently from a medical _prescription_ ), new meanings can become distinct by clipping (as a _dedicatory letter_ developed into a _dedication_ ), and new types can be designated by an older term which has come to be specialized in the process. The latter change has obviously affected the _advertisement_ . The word, borrowed from French in the fifteenth century, clearly relates to an action intended to draw someone’s attention (cf. _OED_ s.v. _advertisement_ n. 2), which by metonymic extension then came to designate the object that serves this purpose, i.e. a ‘notice’ ( _ibid._ , sense 4). Accordingly, many eighteenth-century books have advertisements right at the beginning, a text type which in modern diction we would prefer to call a _preface_ , _foreword_ or _introduction_ . What these obviously share with the modern meaning of the term ‘advertisement’ is the address to the reader phrased in a way that raises and keeps his attention, a shared element that also serves to explain how it was possible for the modern content to evolve. 

A linguistic history of advertising, 1700–1890 85 

## 1.3 History of research and methodology 

Among the great wealth of literature on the topic of advertising, only a few books are relevant for linguistic investigations, while most other works deal exclusively with commercial aspects, or present an incoherent amalgam of various themes without providing a systematic or comprehensive account. I found the following books most enlightening: Elliott’s _History of English Advertising_ (1962), Sampson’s _History of Advertising from Earliest Times_ (1874 —and, largely based on this slightly rambling account, Turner’s _Shocking History of Advertising_ of 1952). The only consistently linguistic interpretation in book form (which is, however, largely based on twentieth-century material) is Leech’s _English in Advertising_ (1966); Gotti’s paper (1994) contrasts analyses of selected advertisements from the _Review_ , the _Spectator_ and the _Tatler_ . Recently, Gieszinger’s (2000) very detailed study of advertisements in _The Times_ summarized the development of the text type between 1788 and 1996; the analysis is somewhat limited by the fact that it is confined to selective data from a single newspaper and that some sub-types are excluded (medical, book and auction advertisements). 

For this exploratory study, my text corpus had to be necessarily restricted. I have analysed the large number of advertisements in Defoe’s _Review_ (1704–1713) and contrasted them with those found in a yearly run of _The Newspaper_ (1844), a regional weekly paper written for country gentlemen. This was complemented by specimens from books on the history of advertising (Sampson 1874; Turner 1952) and on typographical developments in the field (Wood 1967; Lewis 1969; Hindley & Hindley 1972). 

A historical linguist will for his analysis, then, have to piece various bits together and attempt to correlate two sets of parameters or features (cf. the methods of classical sociolinguistics): 

1. evidence from social and cultural history, such as: 

- 1a. the topics advertised, 

- 1b. the audience addressed, 

- 1c. the place of advertisements (for instance in newspapers, posters, hoardings — and modern radio and TV advertisements), 

- 1d. the legal/financial/commercial conditions of advertising, 

- 1e. the expectations of readers, 

- 1f. the strategies employed by advertisers to reach an intended audience, and 

- 1g. the role of non-verbal constituents of the advertisement, especially illustration. 

86 Manfred Görlach 

2. the properly linguistic components selected for the purpose, such as 

- 2a. spelling and typography, 

- 2b. vocabulary (including collocations and meaning), 

- 2c. syntax (especially the use of block language), 

- 2d. style (text type, formality, intelligibility, expressiveness), and 

- 2e. traditions, intertextuality, quotes and allusions. 

## 1.4 Periods in the history of advertising[1] 

Starting from the first advertisements in precursors of the modern newspaper, the _Mercuries_ of c. 1625, the frequency and commercial importance of the genre greatly increased by 1700, with topics like lost horses and patent medicines appearing from 1650 onwards. The appropriate style did not take long to develop. In the _Mercurius Politicus_ of 20 December 1660 we find an advertisement in which the medicine in question is already praised as the “most excellent and approved dentifrice” (Elliott 1962:18). 

For reasons of style, content and the expected readership it is appropriate to divide the topic into four periods, of which the second and third will be dealt with in this paper: 

1. The beginnings of advertising to 1700, 

2. The first heyday of the text type to 1830, 

3. The expansion phase to 1890, 

4. The period in which newspaper specimens coexisted with radio and, later, film and TV advertisements, and in which the function of the relevant texts changed dramatically. 

The second phase in the eighteenth century is characterized by a limited readership, an important mediating function of coffee houses, fashionable and expensive articles offered, and a notable restraint in typographical means. Although typical exaggerations of advertising style, or ‘puffing’ (as it was called) are obvious, these are still part of typographically inconspicuous texts largely relying on lengthy exposition, with only the excessive praise of the unique quality of the product diverging from ‘normal’ descriptive texts. 

Typical topics include the praise of commodities like coffee, tea, tobacco, cloths, medicines, transport (horse-coaches), books (often disguised in the form of reviews), amusements, jobs, matrimonials and lotteries. Then as now the selection of goods offered depended on the expected readership. It is no surprise that in Defoe’s _Review_ (1704–1713) advertisements for books and 

A linguistic history of advertising, 1700–1890 87 

patent medicines (cf. Doherty 1992; Fries 1997) predominate; the types of advertisements in later eighteenth-century newspapers diverge considerably as far as topics and diction are concerned.[2] However, what these have in common with their predecessors of eighty years before is the total absence of graphic designs which makes the texts indistinguishable from other text types, such as newstexts and official announcements on front pages — where advertisements are commonly found. 

The other period treated in my paper starts in the 1830s and ends with the competition of modern media in the 1890s. The beginning of the phase is best defined extralinguistically by technological developments, an expanding readership and a greatly increased circulation of newspapers in the 1830s (though distribution was still hampered by the Stamp Act, repealed in 1855).[3] Perhaps the most striking Victorian innovation is the creative combination of fanciful and often ingenious illustrations combined with (initially) still extensive texts, which, however, tend to become shorter — to be rapidly digested — as time becomes more precious. As it happens, later texts (such as those from _The Newspaper_ of 1844, p.2) can be very traditional in typography and diction — apparently exhibiting a ‘cultural lag’, the readers of the paper coming from agricultural circles in the province. 

This developed into the stage where the product name, or the producer, was the only text left, as illustrated by Pears’ soap from 1870 onwards (compare illustrations in Hindley & Hindley 1972). This is, of course, the stage where the linguist withdraws and the psychologist takes over. Another innovation is represented by the use of rhymes which became popular for advertisements from the early nineteenth century. These can range from provincial doggerel to the patriotic ‘Buy British’ advertisement for Bryant’s matches. Another innovation was tried for Eno’s Fruit Salts which used extensive quotations mainly from literary sources: 

Three quarters of his space would be taken up by high-flown quotations of man’s unconquerable mind (…) The underlying thesis (…) was the sin of allowing the human intellect to be harnessed to a sluggish gut (…) Now and again the compiler would throw in an uplifting poem which had taken his fancy. (quoted from Hindley & Hindley 1972) 

Is this a possible source of Indian advertising? As pointed out in Görlach (1995a:202ff.), in Indian advertising it is not strange to find advertisements for sarees and other worldly commodities that combine quotations from Keats, Shakespeare, Milton or Wordsworth with technical tradenames and vocabulary. 

88 Manfred Görlach 

Apart from all the more or less universal features provided by the psychology of selling, period style is obviously an important characteristic. Elliott (1962:58–59) comments on nineteenth-century tendencies: 

No one can read the advertisements of this period without marvelling at the cumbrous Latin compounds, the grotesqueries of ‘Greek’ with which the advertiser sought to impress his public. Teeth were stopped with ‘mineral marmoratum’ or ‘mineral succedaneum’; raincoats were ‘siphonias’; hair cream was an ‘aromatic regenerator’; hair dye was an ‘atrapilatory’. There were ‘pulmonic wafers’ for the chest; there were Aethereal Oleine, Elmes’ Arcanum, Winn’s Anticardium, Olden’s Eukeirogenion, and Rypophagon Soap. Some critics feared (as many have feared since) that the English language would never recover from the abuses of the advertiser. 

## 1.5 The evidence provided by playful distortions 

It is well known that modern advertese is often and easily imitated; cf. Leech (1966:6): “The fact that people are able to parody advertisements shows that they have some operational knowledge of advertising English” — but it is interesting to see how early such parodies evolved. At the same time when advertisements in Defoe’s _Review_ provide the first large body of examples, a description in the _Tatler_ (No. 224, 14 September 1710) uses the form to parody the stylistic overuse of evaluative adjectives. The irony is apparent only if the incongruity of text type and linguistic form is recognized. In a different way, a poem by William Woty satirized the genre of the advertisement by the use of rhymed verse. Obviously struck by the similarities of the ‘poetic’ diction used in some advertisements and stereotyped poetic phrases, he wrote his “The auctioneers; a town eclogue” (1780) poking fun on the advertising jargon in property advertisements by taking into his poem as much of the original phrasing as possible, but adding rhyme and metre. His Preface starts: 

The _following_ petite _Piece_ , (though[1] its Beauties may not be numerous, or perfected with the nice Touches and Embellishments of Art, nor written in a[2] bold and great Stile, nor altogether[3] adapted with skilful Utility) _strongly indicates what_ may be _accomplished by an abler Pen_ . The author is conscious that he is inferior to the conspicuous originals in some _Minutiæ_ , but he flatters himself that he has retained, and concentred in one grand, striking point of View, the curious, large, rich, genuine, elegant, capital, magnificent, valuable & desireable beauties. 

A linguistic history of advertising, 1700–1890 89 

Footnotes refer to the sources of the Preface: 

> 1See the Advertisement which ANNOUNCES the sale of Busbridge: “Its numerous beauties are perfected with the nice touches and embellishments of Art”. 

2“Nature hath composed the ground in a bold and great Stile”. — DITTO. 

3“The offices are adapted with skilful Utility”. PALL MALL RHETORIC. (Woty 1780) 

Woty took up the topic of advertising again in his poem “The patent” (1780:163–174). In the late eighteenth century mock advertisements by fictive characters like ‘Timothy Quid’, ‘the Great Isaac Factotum’ and ‘Tony Pinch’ became a staple feature in jest books; they derived their funny effect from the use of uneducated spelling and incoherent mixtures of goods and services offered (e.g. “Curious country shop bills” in Anon., c. 1795:28–29). 

The principle of a misuse of the genre conventions is modified in a nineteenth-century advertisement in the form of a public notice (Wood 1967) as a poster, using the typography of official announcements and largely also the text type specific diction in order to capture the readers’ attention and retain it to the point that the commercial interest is put across. However, to _advertise_ with the help of a non-serious text is clearly a modern development (cf. 4.5 below). 

## 1.6 Contemporary criticism 

As with other forms of persuasive strategies, misuses were bound to arise — Plato, we remember, wanted to ban rhetoric and rhetoricians from the republic. It is significant that the first criticism came almost immediately after the start of the modern advertisement, in the _Mercuries,_ which from 1622 “were printing advertisements, not only of books and freaks, but of lost horses and the earliest patent medicines” (Turner 1952:16). 

Medicines, in particular, provided a source of complaints (and even of legislation). Defoe, in his _Journal of the Plague Year_ , pointed out the huge number of quack medicines produced against the 1665 pestilence and advertised in characteristic descriptions such as: 

Infallible preventive Pills against the Plague. 

Never-failing Preservatives against the Infection. 

Sovereign Cordials against the Corruption of the Air. 

Exact Regulations for the Conduct of the Body, in case of an Infection. Antipestilential Pills. 

90 Manfred Görlach 

Imcomparable Drink against the Plague, never found out before. An Universal Remedy for the Plague. The Only-True Plague-Water. The Royal Antidote against all kinds of Infection. (Defoe 1722:36) 

(Note in particular the descriptive adjectives used — formally not all superlatives, but semantically equivalent to them.) 

The situation was analysed with great insight — and ironically commented on — by Addison in the _Tatler_ of September 14, 1710 (No. 224). He quoted from Defoe’s _Review_ an advertisement for spirit of lavender written in a Ciceronian manner — apparently no appropriate genre-specific style had been found for the text type: 

The hig(h)est Compounded Spirit of Lavender: the most Glorious (if the Expression may be us’d) Enlivening Scent and Flavour that can possibly be In Vapours, sick Fits, Faintings, etc. smelt to, or dropt upon a bit of Loaf Sugar, and eaten or dissolv’d in Wine, Coffee, Tea, or what Liquor you please, so charms the Spirits, delights the Guts, and gives such Airs to the Countenance, as are not to be imagin’d but by those that have try’d it. The meanest sort of the thing is admir’d by most Gentlemen and Ladies, but this far more, as by far it exceeds it, to the gaining among all a more common Esteem. Is sold only (in neat Flint Bottles fit for the Pocket) at 3s. 6d. each, at the Golden Key in Warton’s-Court, near Holborn-Bars. (here quoted from a later reprint in the _Review_ , November 30, 1710, VII, 107 & 428; also quoted by Gotti 1994:108)[4] 

Finally, Addison also pointed to modest innovations in typographic (not linguistic) style to attract the reader’s attention: 

Asterisks and Hands were formerly in great Use for this Purpose. Of late Years the N.B. has been much in Fashion; as also were Cuts and Figures, the invention of which we must ascribe to the Author of Spring Trusses. I must not here omit the blind _Italian_ character, which being scarcely legible always fixes and detains the eye and gives the curious reader something like the satisfaction of prying in a secret. (quoted from Turner 1952:26–27; also in Gotti 1994:114) 

A hundred years later, when advertising had become a huge industry, Johnson, in the _Idler_ of 1759, complained more strongly about negative aspects of advertising: 

Advertisements are now so numerous that they are very negligently perused, and it is therefore become necessary to gain attention by magnificence of promises, and by eloquence sometimes sublime and sometimes pathetic. Promise, large promise is the Soul of an Advertisement. 

A linguistic history of advertising, 1700–1890 91 

With all his criticism, Johnson found that “[t]he trade of advertising is now so near to perfection that it is not easy to propose any improvement”(1759, both quoted from Turner 1952:29). However, he was careful to warn against excesses, especially since advertisements stood side by side with international news on the front page, often without any typographical distinction. Such criticism did not of course stop the practice; we are therefore not surprised to find, some 80 years later, complaints of a very similar kind: a contributor to the _Athenaeum_ , July 17, 1839, found: 

There can be little doubt that the stupidest cluster of trashy papers, the most insignificant articles, may by dint of eternal paragraph be forced into sale. It could not otherwise happen that Day and Martin, Rowland, Colburn and Bentley, Eady, Warren and those after their kind could lavish so much money in the praises of their oils, their books, their pills and their polish if there did not exist a class of human being who are greedy of belief. It is the duty of an independent journal to protect as far as possible the credulous, confiding and unwary from the wily arts of the insidious advertiser. (1839, quoted from Turner 1952:54 and Elliott 1962:54) 

## 1.7 The development of a new text type 

The term ‘advertisement’ was still vague, or polysemic, in the eighteenth century. As a consequence, the genre of the commercial advertisement published to praise, and thereby increase sales of, a particular product had to be formed by the development of characteristic typographical features in combination with a specific diction — which in turn gave the term ‘advertisement’ greater precision, and influenced the practice of text writers to conform to the emerging conventions and the readers’ expectations. Since the linguistic characteristics will be analysed below, it will be useful here to name the other constituent features that became more or less obligatory: 

1. The place of the text in a journal or on the front page of a newspaper, usually headed “advertisement(s)” in order to distinguish it from other text types surrounding it. 

2. The name of the product named first, often highlighted by the use of capitals, italics or bold typeface — often making up for the omission of a proper headline. 

- 3a. A description of the commodity in glowing terms (for adjectives see 2.2 below) involving superlatives or semantically equivalent terms; 

- 3b. in the case of medicines, the enumeration of the ills, often described in 

92 Manfred Görlach 

great detail and in the most dismal terms possible, and the assurance that 

4. Authorities quoted, commonly in the form of “recommended by the eminent doctor X”, but independent testimonials also come to be appended (introduced by “This is to certify”). 

5. Place where sold (later: how to be obtained), in what quantities and for what price. 

6. Warnings against cheap, spurious, fraudulent counterfeits and imitations. 

Note that there was no legal objection against comparing products with those offered by competitors, and denigrating alternative medicines was common practice. 

In most cases, these advertisements were endlessly repeated, without change of wording, but a few (such as those by the Kirleus widow and the oculist Read in Defoe’s _Review_ ), show a great deal of variation and diachronic intertextual connections. Sampson (1874:395–397) traced the successive advertisements of Mr Patence, “Dentist and Dancing Master”, which appeared between 1771 and 1775 in the _Gazetteer_ and the _Morning Post_ , each containing more incredible accounts of his achievements, ending with the dictum “Envy may snarl, but superior Abilities assists ( _sic_ ) the Afflicted”. 

We are not sufficiently informed about the authors of these texts properly to interpret their (socio-)linguistic relevance. Many must have been composed by the very people offering their products and services, but others were certainly written, or at least revised and polished, by hack writers in quest of a quick buck. Sampson (1874:394) draws attention to an incompetent text in the _General Advertiser_ for June 21, 1749, praising the healing effects of snuff “which was supposed to cure lunacy”. Making fun of the advertiser’s failure to formulate his message in intelligible English, Sampson aptly comments: “Certainly [the stuff] has an effect on the ideas with regard to the construction of sentences”. 

## 2. Linguistic analysis: The eighteenth century 

## 2.1 The rhetorical background 

Although the importance of rhetoric has continually decreased from the Renaissance onwards, the discipline still had a very strong hold on eighteenth-century concepts of stylistic appropriateness and language use, the stress shifting from a mechanical categorization of figures back to the more comprehensive system 

A linguistic history of advertising, 1700–1890 93 

of rhetoric as defined by writers in antiquity. If the orator’s objective was to convince, persuade and delight, advertising is one of the written genres typically affected by such concerns: it had fewer of the thematic restrictions inherent in political or even religious persuasion, so that the motive could be put across in a more straightforward form. However, the system of rhetoric has not been used in the analysis of advertising by eighteenth-century commentators or in recent research — modern scholars might in fact prefer a model based on pragmatic approaches or aspects of semiotics (in particular, where visual representation has a special relevance). 

The rhetorical refinement also differs from one advertisement to the next. However, it is obvious that a text like the following was possible only in a time dominated by rhetorical strategies: 

The Golden Odiferous Essence of Johannes Rosarius, M.D. & Philochym. Which is of wonderful Use in all Cephalick Distempers, it takes away all Swellings of the Limbs, or Body, that proceed from any violent Cold; together with all manner of Bruises, Chilblains, Deafness of the Ears, Palsie, Numbness of Legs or Hands, and is of great Efficacy in all Rheumatick Pains; it gives present ease in the most raging Pains of the Cholick, with many other Uses not here mention’d, which you will find in the Papers given with the Bottle. This great Arcanum wants no such Enconiums to set it off, as are found mention’d in the Writings of those Ignorant Empyrick Fellows, daily Published in our Publick News-Papers, the Author being more intent on Matter than Words, on Things than Expressions. Charity obliges me to believe this Learned Person, as by his Writings he appears to be, would never have Published this Noble Panacea, had it not first, by a successful use of it, been well approv’d of by himself. Sold by Sam. Saunders, Periwig-Maker, in Star-Court in Cheapside near the Counduit. Price 1 s. the Bottle. (The _Review_ , 6 March 1705) 

The conspicuous features are the following: 

1. The use of superlatives and strongly positive adjectives: _golden_ , _wonderful_ , _great_ , _successful_ ; _all manner_ , _all_ ; and corresponding negatives in the pains it heals: _violent_ , _most raging_ . 

2. The use of learned words which are employed without any factual need: _odiferous_ ‘smelly’, _cephalick_ ‘head’, _Arcanum_ ‘mystery’, _Enconium_ and _Panacea_ ‘praise’. 

3. _Praeteritio_ , i.e. (claimed) failure to praise the article: _wants no such Enconiums_ and the reason given (“Concentration rather on Matter than Words, on Things than Expressions”). 

94 Manfred Görlach 

In other cases the paragraph structure is closely modelled on legal texts, with sections indicated typographically: 

Whereas a Proposal has been made by the Author of this Paper, for Subscriptions to a Book in Folio, Entitled, _Jure Divino_ ; or, _A Satyr upon Tyranny and Passive Obed(i)ence_ . To which, Abundance of Gentlemen having long since Subscrib’d, and the Time seeming long for its coming out, some very Good Reasons having obliged the Author to put a stop to it. 

These are to give Notice, That the said Book is now in the Press, and shall go on to be Printed with all Expedition: And for the further satisfaction of the Subscribers, the Sheets may be seen by any that please to give themselves that Trouble, at Mr. _Matthews’_ s, as they come out of the Press. 

All those Gentlemen, who have Collected Subscriptions for this Book, are Desir’d to send in an Account of what Number they have taken, there being no more to be Printed, than shall be given Notice, as Subscribed; and, if they please, to Pay the Subscription Money to Mr. _Matthews_ , in _Pilkington Court_ , _Little Britain_ , Receipts will be given there for it. (The _Review_ , 11 September 1705) 

The use of _Whereas a Proposal has been made_[5] (…), _These are to give Notice_ (…), _All those Gentlemen_ (…) _are Desir’d_ sounds odd today — probably because they were in fact intentionally transferred from legal text types, possibly to add authority to persuasion. 

## 2.2 Adjectives 

We expect to find some continuity in the use of adjectives in positive and especially superlative form which serve to stress the excellence of the products and create trust in the advertiser. The following are among the most frequent in the advertisements in Defoe’s _Review_ : the _famous_ , _noble_ medicine praised is of course _excellent_ and _most perfect_ , but certainly _incomparable/unparallelled_ and above all _effectual_ and possibly _absolutely infallible_ . Its application is _easy_ , the taste _pleasant/pleasing_ and sometimes _charming_ , _delightful_ , _pretty_ , _surprising_ and _wonderful_ . It is _recommended/approv’d_ by _eminent_ , _experienced_ , _famous_ doctors. 

Apart from the laudatory character of these adjectives, the fact that the superlative _most excellent_ is sometimes used, and other adverbs are also employed as intensifiers even more strongly highlights their appeal. Other adjectives are used more selectively; they include _capable_ , _clear_ , _convenient_ , _diverting_ , _durable_ , _easy_ , _exalted_ , _expeditious_ , _exquisite_ , _fine_ , _free_ , _good_ , _grateful_ , _harmless_ , _new(est)_ , _proper_ , _pure_ , _rational_ , _real_ , _safe_ , _skilful_ , _sovereign_ , _successful_ , _sure_ and _useful_ . Moreover, positive qualities are of course highlighted by 

A linguistic history of advertising, 1700–1890 95 

negative adjectives used for contrast, or in the ills the advertised product is promised to cure. 

## 2.3 Syntax and word-formation 

The involved syntax in many of these texts is perhaps the most astonishing feature. Rather than praising the product in short sentences,[6] the authors take particular care to develop their argument in carefully constructed long sentences often including a number of adverbial clauses and rhetorical figures like antithesis. Two characteristics appear to be particularly close to legal conventions — possibly a feature to increase credibility: 

1. The introductory formula _whereas_ … (cf. 2.1 above) is sometimes used (though not frequently). 

2. The enumeration (for instance of the diseases that can be cured) is structurally close to legal extensional definitions. 

The absence of any unusual word-formations coined ad hoc and with the aim to capture the reader’s attention is truly noteworthy, in contrast to modern practice (cf. Section 4.5). 

## 2.4 Other formulaic expressions 

The repetition of formulas draws our attention to conventions of the period style which may have become obsolete since then. One of the most notable is the opening formula by which the producer or salesman “begs leave to offer/to inform or to acquaint (his subscribers)/to solicit the attention”, or “respectfully assures the Ladies”, or “most respectfully intreats the public to observe”. (The practice continues into the nineteenth century when “Burbidge and Healy beg respectfully to inform the Horticultural world”, _The Newspaper_ 1844). An advertiser sometimes “very respectfully informs the Nobility and Gentry”, thus explicitly mentioning the potential readership. To heighten the appeal, it is often stated that cures come about as if it were by miracle, “by a charm”, “by enchantment”, “by immediate Divine Assistance”, “by God’s Blessing” or generally “to admiration”. Warnings of cheap imitations and forgeries, especially for medicines handed on from one generation to the next, and of course claimed to be as harmless as they are effectual, range from neutral/legalistic formulations to fanciful allegations of competitors. 

96 Manfred Görlach 

## 2.5 Summary 

Apart from excesses in the patent-medicine trade, the linguistics of advertisements were surprisingly modest. The formal tone and elevated style of most of them was obviously directed at informing and convincing readers rather than coaxing and persuading them. This agrees with the inconspicuous presentation which was not intended to catch the readers’ eye — they must have been prepared or made desirous to have the information beforehand. The style and presentation thus clearly reflect the type of leisurely (often coffee-house) browsers, different from the wider Victorian or modern audiences. In addition, the Stamp Act, valid between 1712 and 1855 (charges were reduced in 1836), further restricted the currency of newspapers, and with it, the readership of advertisements. 

The situation is summarized by Turner: 

[In the eighteenth century] the advertisements in all these publications were directed only at a limited circle: the frequenters of coffee-houses, where the newspapers were read. There was little or no advertising of household goods. The advertiser was content to offer the wealthy their coffee, their tea, their turtles, their books and wines and wigs. (Turner 1952:24) 

## 3. The nineteenth century 

When shifting our focus to early nineteenth-century advertisements, we are struck by a further increase in Latinate semi-learned diction in both product names and in the claimed qualities and applications. In _The Newspaper_ (1844), directed at an audience of (gentleman) farmers, the products can be “curvilinear” for “Orchidaceous Houses”, and “Epiphyte-houses” are offered to gardeners. Rowland’s _Kalydor_ (whatever the meaning of the name, possibly meant as ‘beautiful gift’), a “Royally Patronised and universally adopted Specific, is a balmy, odiferous, creamy Liquid, composed chiefly of extracts from the most rare Flowers and Herbs from an Eastern clime (made from an) amalgamation of costly exotic materials”. The carefully planned rhetoric of the advertisement aims at persuading through a combination of authority, elitism, rareness and exoticism, pushing to the background even the more genrespecific praise that it is “the mildest and most efficacious preparation ever known”. Since the majority can’t be wrong, Rowland’s _Macassar Oil_ is supported by “a reputation unparallelled (…) still on increase in public estimation”. 

A linguistic history of advertising, 1700–1890 97 

Warnings against “fraudulent counterfeits” (thus Rowland’s phrase) include expressions like “in lieu of the unsightly rubbish made by slopsellers” or “the cupidity of _unprincipled Shopkeepers_ , who vend the most _spurious trash”_ , a denigrating tradition kept up all through the nineteenth century. Compare the caution formulated in another mid-nineteenth-century text: “Beware of the rubbishy, so-called magnetic socks that are sometimes stocked by chemists on account of their low price”. As far as evaluative adjectives are concerned, advertisers apparently used whatever means of further stylistic exaggeration was available, employing words like _brilliant_ , _efficacious_ , _infinitely surpassing_ , _superior excellence_ and _unrivalled_ ( _The Newspaper_ 1844). 

The most conspicuous features of the period style of advertising continued right through the Victorian period — although the data become divided between advertisements that employ illustrations and those which do not. An additional innovation is that advertisements came to be inserted, often in remarkable numbers, as front and end pieces in books. Thus, the end papers of _Good Things_ of 1887[7] are typical representatives of advertising directed at popular audiences. The multilingual lexis used is most conspicuous where simple contents are explained to simple readers. The advertisement for _Yorkshire Relish_ employs the words “viands palatable”, “piquancy”, “ _au naturel_ ”, “concocting” — very much in the style of Mrs Beeton, the most famous nineteenth-century writer of cookery books, who in her cookery book of 1861 included a glossary of French words (cf. Görlach 1995b). Innovations include a more eye-catching variation of typefaces, a clearer lay-out broken up into short sections and a further vulgarization of medical half-knowledge eclipsed in irresponsible captions like “Do not let your child die!”, “Do not untimely die” and “None now need to despair of life (…) The most extreme cases need not despair”. 

Another nineteenth-century feature is the use of literary devices (see Section 1.4). Thus, advertisements could be accompanied by half-philosophical reflections bolstered up by quotations from eminent writers. Thus Eno dug up a passage from the seventeenth-century essayist Sir William Temple on health and long living to introduce the value of good food (and how Eno’s Fruit Salt helps to achieve this). Even more ambitious is the same firm’s “Contemplation”, supported by an illustration of a cliché philosopher and various quotations of poetry including a scrap from Milton, but innovating in the form of mock advice to “would-be suicides” to “always avoid Eno’s Fruit Salt”. Finally, in an age of globalization, it is interesting to see that Eno, in 1887, provided “Directions in Sixteen Languages, How to Prevent Disease” (see reproductions in Hindley & Hindley 1972). 

98 Manfred Görlach 

## 4. Changes in advertising style 

## 4.1 Sociolinguistic changes 

The major determinant in advertisements and their relation to the potential buyers of the products is the change of readership. In the eighteenth century, only a small section of English society was able to read, had the leisure to do so extensively and the money to afford expensive periodicals. These facts clearly determined the range of commodities advertised and the style used. Good prose style being largely defined by the English used in Addison’s, Steele’s, Defoe’s and Swifts’s essays, it is no surprise that advertisements share many typical features with these. Advertising had to sound respectable to have an effect on the educated readership.[8] That they were manipulated is also true. Tucker (1967:83) comments: 

All the familiar tricks of the men simply out to sell are there — exaggeration, pseudo-science, vulgarity, careless structure: some of it funny, some pompous, some stupid. But a modern reader misses the puns, the clever twists to familiar word-patterns, the alliteration, rhyme or juggling with spelling, the adaptation to the language of various kinds of reader that enliven the good modern advertisement. 

Changes in advertising style are conspicuous around 1830. Again, the change is not unexpected. Cheaper paper and printing created a much larger reading public (although still somewhat delayed by taxation) — and this was also more mobile. Newspapers bought in the new WW Smith’s bookshops and read while waiting for or taking the train were consumed in a way quite different from how _The Spectator_ or _The Rambler_ were read a hundred years earlier. Since the message of the advertisement has to be taken up in a hurry, or at a glance, the new reading style must affect the presentation in the direction of more illustration and less text. However, the change is not as straightforward as might be expected: for one thing, more rural or provincial audiences may still prefer the more ‘informative’ style of advertising, as illustrated by the texts printed in _The Newspaper_ of 1844. An excerpt from the _Pall Mall Gazette_ throws an interesting light on the sociolinguistic differences between the reading styles in towns and the countryside and the consequences for advertising: 

For the most part, readers in towns and suburbs only glance at the exciting portions of the papers, and then cast them aside. Readers in villages, read every line, from the first column to the last, from the title to the printer’s address. Local papers are ploughed steadily through, just as the horses plough the fields, 

A linguistic history of advertising, 1700–1890 99 

and every furrow of type conscientiously followed from end to end, advertisements and all. (…) in advertising for country people, one fact must be steadily borne in mind, that they are slow to act; that is the advertisement, to produce any result, must be permanent. (November 1881, quoted from Collins 1886:32) 

On the other hand, Victorian advertisements, and newspaper texts in general, are still much more verbose than modern ones. In many cases the advertiser’s strategy is now divided in two steps: first attract attention, and when you have gained it, use it to pass on detailed information. This explains typographical excesses like those collected in Hindley & Hindley, such as the advertisement for electric warming of shoes in the form of a sole (1972:7.2). 

## 4.2 Contrasts with coexisting styles 

Diachronic developments exclusively based on differences exhibited in individual text types tend to be misleading, unless they are contrasted with other texts of the same period. The stylistic range, which makes the choice of a particular diction a deliberate selection from coexisting styles and thereby illustrates the distance of advertese from other prose texts, is at least as distinctive — however difficult it may be to reconstruct the necessary communicative competence in the period language which includes a reconstruction of the impact on readers affected by the connotations of advertese. Although eighteenth-century advertising language may strike us as unusually formal, we need to measure its degree of formality in comparison to expository texts of the period. 

## 4.3 Changes in lexis 

The adjective has long been seen as the most distinctive element in advertising style. This is partly because many adjectives are evaluative, and partly because they are gradable: _good_ involves the advertisement writer’s evaluation, _best_ adds comparison and a particular emotional appeal. Leech compiled a hitlist of adjectives in the advertisements of the 1960s (a list which may well be historical by now, but I do not know of any comprehensive recent study, even one contrastive to Leech’s). The ranking (1966:152) he found for the twenty most common adjectives is as follows: (1) _new_ ; (2) _good/better/best_ ; (3) _free_ ; (4) _fresh_ ; (5) _delicious_ ; (6) _full, sure_ ; (7) _clean, wonderful_ ; (8) _special_ ; (9) _crisp_ ; (10) _fine_ ; (11) _big_ ; (12) _great_ ; (13) _real_ ; (14) _easy_ , _bright_ ; (15) _extra_ , _safe_ ; (16) _rich_ . 

Counting the frequency of these adjectives is, however, only half the story: the classes of nouns with which they collocate is at least as distinctive. For 

100 Manfred Görlach 

instance, the fact that _good_ is almost exclusively used with food may come as a surprise. Less astonishing is the fact that negatives are avoided ( _bad_ , _dreadful_ ), and that ‘gradation’ is also achieved by the use of intensive adjectives, replacing _good_ by more expressive items. 

A comparison with the adjectives used in the eighteenth century brings out significant contrasts. Many modern words were then not used at all ( _delicious_ , _special_ , _crisp_ , _bright_ , _extra_ ), others were less frequent than they are now ( _new_ , _good_ , _free_ , _fresh_ etc.). This could partly be because of the different products offered, but is probably more indicative of a more ‘respectable’ style employed in advertising. By contrast, the advertisements I analysed from the nineteenth century (concentrating on _The Newspaper_ of 1844 which had practically no illustrations, thus reflecting eighteenth-century conventions) had a very low incidence of evaluative adjectives, with the exception of patent medicines. Instead, there was much verbosity and Latinate expression, again not unexpected in nineteenth-century prose. 

## 4.4 Changes in syntax 

Tendencies in syntax have gone from carefully constructed long sentences in the eighteenth century to more loosely built, but still long ones in the nineteenth, (ultimately) to short and often insufficiently connected ones in PDE. In Leech’s convincing descriptive formula, styles have gone from discursive to disjunctive. The change is noticeable in other genres, but most clearly seen in advertising. The text type now supplies many instances of ‘block language’, that is, incomplete syntactical patterns — if it is not downright asyntactical. These changes go together with other features which are also found in Biber & Finegan’s analyses (1992, 2001) of the development of written registers like fiction or personal letters: formal, impersonal, involved, ceremonious styles which combine Latinate expressions, high frequencies of passives and explicitly formulated statements have come to be replaced by forms reflecting more direct and often more personal address. Since the change is largely a twentieth-century one, we might assume it has to do with advertising in other media, and in fact we can see advertisements in newspapers coming closer to the elliptical syntax traditionally used for posters and hoardings from early times onwards. However, the new conventions in newspaper advertising seem much better explained by a change in life-style, in communication patterns and a breakdown of social classes and certain educational expectations. 

On the other hand, very verbose styles of advertising can survive, even in 

A linguistic history of advertising, 1700–1890 101 

popular tabloids of recent years where we might possibly least expect it — as modern specimens from various dailies serve to illustrate. 

## 4.5 Changes in seriousness — and an outlook beyond 1890 

Many early advertisements are boring reading: they lack the visual attraction we now connect with the genre, but also the experimental use of deviant wordformations and (largely) the punning and other forms of word-play which are among the most conspicuous features in modern advertese.[9] This change started in the nineteenth century when various eye-catching devices were tried. However, the technique is largely a very recent development — it became frequent after 1950, and reached its peak after the 1970s. Many of the linguistic means employed in such diction would in former times have been categorized by the prospective customer as a parody, and certainly have not helped to create the trust in the product that is necessary to take the decision to buy. The forms that such word-play can take are infinite; they range from slips of the tongue to rhyme and alliteration, misunderstanding of polysemous words and homonyms, mis-spellings, distorted quotations and other forms of intertextuality: there is no end to modern specimens. The first examples I remember from the 1950s — excepting the flat phrase “My goodness, my Guinness!” — include one for Newcastle Pale Ale (1959): 

## Thirsty days has dry September, October too, _and_ dull November. _NEWCASTLE PALE ALE_ 

Hermerén quotes from the 1990s the advertisement for Ballantine’s Scotch, accompanied by the tune of “my darling Ballantines” (1999:124) and for Dillon’s Bookshop “Foiled again? Try Dillons” (1999:136) — and many of us will remember verbal playfulness from the London underground from the 1970s onwards.[10] 

All this illustrates changes of attitude that seem to make Leech’s characterization of advertese dated — although the statement is only thirty years old. He still claimed in 1966: 

1. An advertisement is of necessity honest in declaring its purpose. The mention of the brand-name is usually sufficient to identify an advertisement for what it is, and, in addition, regulations at least partially safeguard the public against camouflaged advertising. 

102 Manfred Görlach 

2. The advertiser has to buy his way to the public’s attention; budgeting economy of means against results, in terms of sales returns, is an especially important consideration for him. (Leech 1966:26) 

To my mind, such uses are connected with new typographical styles for advertising in the twentieth century — and the tedium caused by excessive praise of common products. However, we may be misled by the fact that our judgment is based on intellectual, largely middle-class attitudes. The change just sketched appears to have happened only to some layers of society while the old type persists in others. Many advertisements in more popular papers have surprisingly long texts to them, are mainly expository and certainly not witty. Gieszinger (2000:39) notes the increase in length in modern advertisements also for _The Times_ ; this points to a growing diversification in relation to the product described, and may reflect period-specific changes. The persuasion of the allegedly objective, very detailed information contained in these texts is obviously seen as the best means of success, and it comes as a surprise that the long texts are aimed at people who do not read much. 

## Notes 

1. All period boundaries in historical disciplines are open to objections. The one suggested here has no particular claim to originality or validity, and may well have to be refined by future research. I have found 1700 a useful borderline because it fits with the publication of the first regular newspapers from 1702 on. 

2. I have here used the pages reproduced in Morison (1932). They comprise a large proportion of announcements of plays and concerts (under “public amusements”), and otherwise provide a mixture praising _inter alia_ money on bond, a sauce for cold meats, spring cloaks, beaver hat wardrobe, patent bedsteads, silver pens — and anti-scorbutic medicine (thus _The Star and Evening Advertiser_ no. 1, May 3, 1788). 

3. Nevett (1982:25) points out the restrictive consequences of threefold taxation before 1853–1861, _viz._ the stamp duty relating to the size of the sheets, the paper duty depending on the weight of the newspaper and the advertisement duty for each item. 

4. Also compare Addison’s ironic treatment of the topic in the _Spectator_ No. 547 in which he imitates “the Stile and Phrase of the like Ingenious Compositions which we frequently meet with at the end of our News-Papers”, and see Gotti’s analysis (1994:114–115). 

5. The _OED_ (s.v. _whereas_ 3) does not relate the use to any specific text types, but the rankshifting function as a noun ( _whereas_ 4) at least supports the assumption of a transfer from legal texts. 

A linguistic history of advertising, 1700–1890 103 

6. Gotti (1994:104) points out that there are texts in which “the development is obtained by means of juxtaposition, with short sentences following each other”, but this type is clearly a minority option, possibly frequent only in book and theatrical advertisements. 

7. The book, apparently handed out free of charge by the producers of Yorkshire Relish, baking powder, egg powder, custard powder, but also plate powder and lavender water (Goodall, Backhouse & Co. of Leeds, 1998) is a collection of disguised advertisements, comprising a series of 100 recipes all containing advice on how to use one of the Goodall products for successful meals; cf. the facsimiles in Görlach (1999:282f.). Even the producer’s name sounds like an advertising trick! 

8. This style is correlated with indirect persuasion, by which the advertisers often do not appear in person, and potential customers are advised to try a product rather than being approached in a more straightforward way (Gotti 1994; Gieszinger 2000:9). 

9. As stated above, rhetorical elements played a central role in eighteenth-century advertising — but not jokes and other forms of verbal playfulness (as is so common in present practice). The two categories should therefore be kept separate: Gieszinger found the use of rhetorical patterns throughout, but jokes used only from 1896 onwards (2000:174). 

10. It seems remarkable that the change in style has become international. Over the last ten years, punning (normally disliked in my native German) has become acceptable in German advertising, especially if the text contains English elements, as in _Dämmershoppen_ for late shopping hours (cf. Görlach 1994).
