## When did English begin? 

Angelika Lutz University of Erlangen-Nürnberg 

## 1. The Norman Conquest and Middle English 

Historians and historical linguists have always been both attracted and repelled by the idea of subdividing continuous long-term developments into clear-cut periods. For linguists, such a task appears even more challenging than for historians, since although a political event may change life for an entire population rather abruptly, the possibly ensuing linguistic changes will take more time. In particular, if a country is conquered by foreigners who speak a different language, the establishment of a new adequate communicative system will require some time to evolve, irrespective of the linguistic solution chosen. This is one reason why the imagined dividing lines between adjacent historical periods and those between adjacent linguistic periods hardly ever coincide. 

England at the borderline between the early and high Middle Ages is certainly a case in point. With regard to political and military history, the Norman Conquest is known to have resulted in a radical change: the AngloSaxon political establishment quickly lost its power, and the Anglo-Saxon episcopacy was soon replaced by a continental, mostly Norman episcopacy. By 1086 at the latest, after William the Conqueror’s cruel suppression of the uprising in the North, the foreign feudal system was firmly established throughout the country.[1] This historical dividing line between pre-Conquest AngloSaxon England and post-Conquest Norman England has frequently been associated with a linguistic dividing line between the periods of Old English and Middle English, respectively. This is so in spite of the fact that Henry Sweet developed his tripartite periodization of English on the basis of the purely linguistic criterion of the degree of morphological synthesis. Since Henry Sweet’s days, there has been repeated discussion about the question when his “period of levelled inflexions” precisely began. Sweet himself had changed his 

146 Angelika Lutz 

mind over time. In the second edition of his _History of English Sounds_ (1888), he saw the end of Old English at around 1100 and the beginning of “fully developed Middle English” at around 1150, with a transition period of five decades in between, but in his _New English Grammar_ (1892), his “Transition English” spanned the entire twelfth century.[2] Kemp Malone (1930) predated the transition period between Old and Middle English to the second half of the tenth century and the beginning of Middle English to around 1000, likewise on morphophonological grounds, and most recently, Peter Kitson (1997) has argued for a return to Sweet’s 1892 dating of the beginning of Middle English to around 1200. The handbooks have generally tended to a dividing line around 1100.[3] It is not my intention to challenge any of these views, as they are based on sound textual evidence. I would merely like to stress that these rather widely differing suggestions for the beginning of Middle English, which moreover quite rightly differ for different dialects, make it obvious that the levelling of inflexions cannot be associated with the Norman Conquest. The two developments have nothing to do with each other.[4] 

## 2. The special character of the French contribution to the English lexicon 

The most important linguistic effect of the Norman Conquest is known to have been of a different kind. This radical shifting of power to foreign rulers who spoke a variety of French resulted in a transformation of the English lexicon of such a magnitude that the antiquaries of the sixteenth and seventeenth centuries distinguished between their own English and ‘Saxon’ as two basically different languages.[5] When Sweet established his periodization of English on the basis of morphophonological reduction, this did not affect the assessment of the Norman Conquest as being of prime importance for the extreme lexical differences between Old English and Present-day English. Yet in my view, the influence of Old French on the lexicon of post-Conquest English still tends to be underestimated. In particular, I would want to suggest that more attention needs to be paid to the linguistic fact that this lexical influence was not only particularly strong but really different in kind from other important foreign influences. In quantitative terms, this is most clearly demonstrated by Manfred Scheler (1977) in his assessment of the etymological origins of the various contributions to the lexicon of Modern Standard English. The figures of his chart are based on three strongly differing dictionaries — the _Shorter Oxford_ 

When did English begin? 147 

_English Dictionary_ ( _SOED_ ) containing about 80,000 entries of words belonging to all stylistic levels; the _Advanced Learner’s Dictionary_ ( _ALD_ ) with a little less than 30,000 words of both simple and higher styles; and the _General Service List_ ( _GSL_ ), which contains only the most frequent words (c. 4,000).[6] This comparative assessment allows for a stylistically differentiated analysis of foreign influences on the English lexicon. Table 1 is a selective version of his chart, which concentrates on Scheler’s percentages for his _Inselgermanisch_ (i.e. the portion of the Modern English lexicon that is based on its inherited WestGermanic core), for French, Latin, Greek, the non-French Romance languages and the Celtic languages. Other sources of lexical influence have been left out of account here.[7] 

Table 1. Etymological origins of the lexicon of Modern Standard English 

||_SOED_|_ALD_|_GSL_|
|---|---|---|---|
||(80,096 words)|(27,241 words)|(3,984 words)|
|_Inselgermanisch_|22.20%|27.43%|47.08%|
|French|28.37%|35.89%|38.00%|
|Latin|28.29%|22.05%|9.59%|
|Greek|5.32%|1.59%|0.25%|
|Italian, Spanish, Portuguese, Provençal|1.86%|1.60%|0.20%|
|Celtic languages|0.34%|0.25%|–|



The figures in the first line demonstrate that the lexicon of Modern Standard English cannot be viewed as that of a Germanic language: if we count lexical types, Scheler’s _Inselgermanisch_ amounts to only 47% even in the most basic vocabulary, and in relation to the entire English lexicon this portion makes up less than a quarter. Thus, the inherited, West-Germanic portion of Modern Standard English is remarkably small on all stylistic levels and it is the larger, the smaller and more everyday the vocabulary is that is being considered. 

The French contribution comes remarkably close to the inherited portion on all stylistic levels. Its shares of 28% in the most detailed dictionary and of 35% in the general vocabulary of an educated speaker in fact exceed those of _Inselgermanisch_ , and even in the most basic vocabulary, the French portion of 38% comes quite near the inherited share of 47%. These figures contrast with those for Latin listed in the third line: if we consider the entire lexicon, the Latin and French portions are equally large and together make up close to two thirds of the Modern English lexicon. However, in contrast to the French share, that of Latin decreases with the size of the lexicon considered, and for the basic 

148 Angelika Lutz 

vocabulary it amounts to less than 10%. The same quantitative relations, though on a much smaller scale than for Latin, hold for Greek and for the nonFrench Romance languages. And they also hold for the Celtic languages, but in that case it is the extremely small percentages that deserve mention, especially in view of the fact that the contact with the Celtic languages extended over a much longer period than that with any other language except Latin. 

## 3. The linguistic effects of a conquest 

This stylistic difference between the French and the inherited portions of the lexicon on the one hand and the Latin, Greek, the other Romance and the Celtic portions on the other hand is due to different contact situations: the figures for Latin etc. are characteristic of cultural borrowing which makes itself felt more strongly in special sections of the vocabulary than in the general and basic vocabulary, and the percentages reflect the range and intensity of the cultural appeal of the donor language for the speakers of the recipient language. By contrast, the particularly large share of French in the basic vocabulary of Modern Standard English cannot be attributed to its cultural appeal alone but results from forced linguistic contact exerted by the speakers of the language of a conquering power on that of the conquered population. Ordinary borrowing, guided by the wish to acquire new things and concepts and, together with them, the appropriate foreign terms, could not have led to such an extreme effect on the basic vocabulary of the recipient language. 

Characteristic lexical fields for such forced contact are loans that most directly reflect a conquest and the resulting overthrow of the pre-existing political establishment. That such influence is due to forced contact becomes most obvious in cases in which the language can be shown to have had a differentiated terminology for the same concepts prior to the conquest. In the case of medieval England, this can be shown most clearly for legal terms, as the laws of Anglo-Saxon England are very well evidenced in the vernacular.[8] The small selection of examples in Table 2 for pre-Conquest legal terms, taken from the _Thesaurus of Old English_ (Roberts & Kay 1995), and their post-Conquest equivalents concentrates on words which belong to the general vocabulary of educated speakers and are thus within the lexical range of dictionaries such as the _Advanced Learner’s_ . The Old English terms that were totally replaced by post-Conquest terms have been marked with a following cross.[9] 

This selection of examples is not representative in any strict sense but in my 

When did English begin? 149 

Table 2. Old English vs. Modern English legal terms 

|OE_do¯m_|–|ModE_judgment_|
|---|---|---|

view provides a realistic picture of the linguistic facts. Most Old English terms were given up entirely, some survived but nevertheless frequently lost their technical, legal meanings and were replaced by technical terms of French origin, as was the case with _doom_ and _deem_ . The few words at the bottom of the list that survived as technical terms were possibly supported by Frankish or Old Norse cognates that formed part of the Norman legal customs of the time.[10] Thus, taken together, Table 2 shows an almost complete replacement of the Old English, Germanic terms by Norman French terms for the same legal concepts due to the change of power; the rules were simply re-set in the language of the foreign rulers.[11] 

That the influence of French on the English lexicon is particularly strong in the lexical fields of law, government, administration and military power has long been common knowledge, but so far the handbooks, including Scheler’s study, make hardly any distinction between words that reflect a more advanced stage of cultural development[12] and words that have found their way into English only on account of forced contact, that is due to the fact that medieval Norman French, as the language of the rulers of England, functioned as a 

150 Angelika Lutz 

linguistic superstratum for English for an extended period of time. A superstratum typically exerts an excessively strong influence on the lexicon of the substratum language in all sections of the general vocabulary and, more particularly, in lexical areas that pertain to political and military rule.[13] By contrast, a substratum language has little lexical effect on the superstratum yet may exert far-reaching structural influence on the superstratum, that is if many speakers of the substratum abandon it for the superstratum, as happened in the case of the Celtic languages in the British Isles over the centuries.[14] 

The terms _superstratum_ and _substratum_ are not used in any of the handbooks that deal most extensively with lexical contact between French and English.[15] Scheler in fact emphasizes the cultural aspects of the linguistic contact with Old French even where dealing with typical superstratum terms. Thus, although most of his examples for legal language (e.g. _judge_ , _judgement_ , _just_ , _justice_ , _perjury_ , _court_ , _sentence_ , _verdict_ , _defendant_ ) have straightforward equivalents in pre-Conquest English, he characterizes them as “Spiegelbild des tiefgreifenden Einflusses der franz.-agn. Kultur und Zivilisation auf das spätmittelalterliche England”.[16] 

## 4. The persistence of superstratal effects: Tyndale’s and Luther’s translations of the Bible 

Before addressing the question of when this superstratal relationship between French and English became fully effective, i.e. when ‘Saxon’ ended and English began, it seems useful to attempt a comparative assessment of the linguistic effect of this superstratal influence on post-Conquest English, namely by way of a comparison of two early Modern vernacular Protestant Bible translations, one by William Tyndale into English and the other by Martin Luther into German. We know that both Tyndale and Luther expressly aimed at good, idiomatic versions of the Bible and that, eventually, their translations were highly praised.[17] However, a lexical comparison of St Paul’s letter addressed to the people of Rome (Chapters 1–3, complete listing) reveals that idiomatic English and idiomatic German of the sixteenth century differ to the extreme. The material presented in Table 3 demonstrates that Luther’s Early Modern German rendition (right-hand column) shows very little etymological agreement with the Latin equivalents from the Vulgate and the translation by Erasmus (lefthand column):[18] his few loans from Latin and French in Tables 3c and 3d (printed in italics) are mostly old-established technical terms. Basically, good, idiomatic German of this text type was of Germanic origin. 

When did English begin? 151 

By contrast, in Tyndale’s text loanwords (printed in italics) clearly predominate over words of Germanic origin. The Latin and English stems of the words in Table 3a agree etymologically, and at first sight Tyndale’s expressions look like direct loans from Latin.

Thus, taken together, Tyndale’s sixteenth-century lexicon supports the statistical findings by Scheler for Present-day English for a general and basic type of vocabulary: French and Latin expressions clearly predominate over words of Germanic origin, with French providing the lion share. Comparative, quantitative assessments of this kind suggest that the sixteenth-century heated debate in England about the employment or avoidance of Latin loans[19] was really of marginal linguistic significance: the English lexicon had long before ceased to be Germanic and had developed into “a singular mixture of Germanic and Romance elements”.[20] 

## 5. Post-Conquest England and the delayed appearance of the romance 

The handbooks are agreed that this mixture is a result of the Norman Conquest but that the influence of French became visible with considerable delay. After the Conquest, England turned functionally trilingual for more than two centuries. Basically, French became and remained the language of the ruling classes until well after the political separation of England and Normandy in 1204, Latin remained the language of scholarship and the church but also extended its sphere of usage into areas of administration of church and state, and English soon lost its pre-Conquest importance as a written standard language but remained the language of the lower classes.[21] It is also agreed that the intake of French loans on a significant scale set in long after the Norman Conquest and that the influx of French words was exceptionally large in the fourteenth century, when the French-speaking ruling classes of England gradually switched to English and in that process imported large numbers of words from French into English (for figures see Scheler 1977:52; Baugh & Cable 1993:173f.). The political and cultural status of England in the period prior to the political separation of England and Normandy is aptly described by A.G. Rigg (1992:6): “In the late eleventh century England was in many ways simply a colony of Northern France. In the reign of Henry II (1154–89) the English Channel almost disappeared politically and culturally”. 

How exactly this political and cultural status of England is reflected in the English lexicon of the period will remain open to interpretation and debate in 

156 Angelika Lutz 

many details. I will nevertheless attempt to show that, both from a lexical and a more general cultural point of view, ‘Saxon’ English ended long after Middle English as the period of levelled inflexions began. I will do this on the basis of a small selection of texts that are otherwise well known as examples for the Early Middle English period, and I would like to begin by pointing to a text type that did not appear in the English vernacular during this quasi-colonial period but only long after the separation of England and Normandy, namely the romance. This text type, which was typically addressed at courtly audiences, developed in France in the twelfth century, from where it spread to other European countries. Yet whereas the great Middle High German romances were composed around 1200, in England romances were produced almost exclusively in French until the late Middle Ages.[22] This much-delayed appearance of the romance in English writing reflects the political fact that during an extended period of time the ruling classes of England were not English. 

## 6. The Anglo-Saxon character of Early Middle English texts 

The delayed appearance of the romance is in agreement with the facts that the texts and text types for which we do have evidence in English from that period preserve the Old English, Germanic character of the lexicon practically intact and that their lexical conservatism is accompanied by various features of a cultural orientation towards the Anglo-Saxon past which is alien to Late Middle English texts. A small selection of extracts, mostly taken from Dickins & Wilson (1951), will suffice to make the point. In the case of my first example (4a), an extract from the famous annal s.a. 1137 of the _Peterborough Chronicle_ (cf. Dickins & Wilson 1951:4f.), the textual and stylistic connections with the Anglo-Saxon past are obvious, as it continues a type of vernacular annal writing that had been current in England at least since the times of King Alfred (cf. Clark 1970:lxxiv–lxxxvii). The Peterborough Continuations of the _Anglo-Saxon Chronicle_ (s.aa. 1122–1154) are otherwise frequently adduced to demonstrate the remarkable degree of inflexional decay that East-Midlands English had reached less than a century after the Conquest (cf. Clark 1970:lii–lxix). Yet their lexicon is almost purely Germanic,[23] and the Germanic and some older Latin terms that have been underlined and glossed in this extract on the basis of Dickins & Wilson (1951:245–330) testify to the degree of foreignness of its lexicon to modern readers. 

Several legal terms (e.g. _suikes_ , _manred_ , _treothes_ ) and numerous general terms (among them remarkably many verbs, e.g. _underfangen_ , _uuenden_ , 

When did English begin? 157 

Table 4. The Anglo-Saxon character of twelfth- and thirteenth-century texts 4a. The _Peterborough Chronicle_ s.a. 1137 (extract) 

Ðis gære for þe king Stephne ofer sæ to Normandi and ther wes underfangen, forþi ðat hi uuenden ðat he sculde ben alsuic alse the eom wes, and for he hadde get his tresor; ac he to-deld it and scatered sotlice. Micel hadde Henri king gadered gold and syluer, and na god ne dide me for his saule thar-of. þa þe king Stephne to Englalande com, þa macod he his gadering æt Oxeneford, and þar he nam þe biscop Roger of Sereberi, and Alexander biscop of Lincol and te canceler Roger hise neues, and dide ælle in prisun til hi iafen up here castles. þa þe suikes undergæton ðæt he milde man was and softe and god, and na iustise ne dide, þa diden hi alle wunder. Hi hadden him manred maked and athes suoren; ac hi nan treuthe ne heolden. Alle he wæron forsworen and here treothes forloren, for æuric riceman his castles makede and agænes him heolden, and fylden þe land ful of castles. Hi suencten suyðe þe uureccemen of þe land mid castel-weorces. þa þe castles uuaren maked, þa fylden hi mid deoules and yuele men. þa namen hi þa men þe hi wenden ðat ani god hefden, bathe be nihtes and be dæies, carlmen and wimmen, and diden heom in prisun efter gold and syluer, and pined heom untellendlice pining, for ne uuæren næure nan martyrs swa pined alse hi wæron.

_undergæton_ ) are unintelligible to speakers of Present-day English, several appear at least stylistically awkward and inappropriate to modern readers, and they would have done also for Tyndale or Chaucer. The text might therefore be characterized as clearly Middle English with regard to its degree of inflexional decay but, at the same time, as Anglo-Saxon with regard to its mostly Germanic lexicon. 

The proclamation issued by Henry III in 1258 in English, Anglo-Norman and Latin (4b) was an isolated attempt at using English as an official language of the court alongside French and Latin almost two centuries after the Conquest, which was presumably prompted by the nationalist reaction to foreign rule led by Simon de Montfort (cf. Dickins & Wilson 1951:7f.; Rothwell 1975:367f.): 

158 Angelika Lutz 

## 4b. The _Proclamation of Henry III_ (extract) 

Henri, þurh Godes fultume King on Engleloande, Lhoauerd on Yrloande, Duk on Normandi, on Aquitaine, and Eorl on Aniow, send igretinge to alle hise holde, ilærde and ileawede, on Huntendoneschire. þæt witen Še wel alle þæt we willen and vnnen þæt, þæt vre rædesmen alle, oþer þe moare dæl of heom þæt beoþ ichosen þurh us and þurh þæt loandes folk on vre kuneriche, habbeþ idon and shullen don in þe worþnesse of Gode and on vre treowþe, for þe freme of þe loande þurh þe besiŠte of þan toforeniseide redesmen, beo stedefæst and ilestinde in alle þinge a buten ænde. And we hoaten alle vre treowe in þe treowþe þæt heo vs oŠen. þæt heo stedefæstliche healden and swerien to healden and to werien þo isetnesses þæt beon imakede and beon to makien, þurh þan toforeniseide rædesmen, oþer þurh þe moare dæl of heom, alswo alse hit is biforen iseid; and þæt æhc oþer helpe þæt for to done bi þan ilche oþe aŠenes alle men riŠt for to done and to foangen. And noan ne nime of loande ne of eŠte wherþurh þis besiŠte muŠe beon ilet oþer iwersed on onie wise.

With the exception of _Duk_ and some names, its lexicon is purely Germanic, and this is again most obvious in expressions relating to law and government (e.g. _holde_ , _rædesmen_ , _treowe_ , _isetnesses_ ) and in verbs with various general and technical meanings ( _willen_ , _vnnen_ , _hoaten_ , _werien_ ), most of which have French equivalents in the glosses based on Dickins & Wilson (1951:159f.). 

LaŠamon’s _Brut_ is remarkable for several reasons. The poem, composed by a parish priest from Worcestershire some time after 1200, is based on an Old French source (Wace’s verse translation of Geoffrey of Monmouth’s _Historia Regum_ ) whose subject-matter forms part of the romances dealing with the Matter of Britain, yet the form of the _Brut_ can be viewed as a late reflection of Old English heroic poetry.[24] The text, which is extant in two manuscript versions dated to c. 1250 and 1270, respectively, consists of some 16,000 alliterating long-lines.[25] The older version C, with its preference for archaic and specifically poetic words and phrases and with attempts at lexical variation of important terms, has an epic-heroic tone that is strongly reminiscent of Old 

When did English begin? 159 

English poetry, although the purely accentual long-lines that are frequently enriched by assonance differ from traditional pre-Conquest poems. The extract in 4c from the older version C, which for reasons of space has been printed in half-lines, exhibits a purely Germanic vocabulary:[26] 

4c. LaŠamon’s _Brut_ : “The Battle with Colgrim” (extract)

In a poem composed long after the Conquest, this seems particularly noteworthy in the field of warfare and leadership (e.g. lines 1 _fehte_ , 2 _ferden_ , 3 _kenpen_ , 4 _uæie_ , 6 _beornes_ , 10 _feond_ , 16 _aðelest_ ). The younger version O does awaywithmanyoftheoutrightlypoetic andarchaicfeaturesofCbut nevertheless 

160 Angelika Lutz 

preserves the Germanic character of the lexicon practically intact.[27] 

The _Proverbs of Alfred_ , which were probably composed in the late twelfth century and are extant in four thirteenth-century manuscripts, reflect Old English didactic poetry.[28] King Alfred of Wessex, who in the introduction is praised as the wisest man of England, issues good advice to his subjects as a kind of moral institution for the English people — more than a century after the Norman Conquest. The metrical form of this text as preserved in the four manuscripts with considerable variation combines features of alliterating longlines and alliterating short-lines with some end rhyme.[29] The extract in 4d, taken from Arngart (1979–80:10, 24), shows that the lexicon of the _Proverbs_ is almost purely Germanic: 

|Table 4d.|_The Proverbs of Alfred_, verse vi|Arngart’s translation|
|---|---|---|
||þus cwað Alfred.|Thus quoth Alfred|
||“þan erl and þan eþeling;|“To the earl and the atheling|
||iberþ under þe king,|it belongs under the king|
||þat lond to leden:|to govern the land|
|5|mid laŠelice deden.|with lawful deeds.|
||And þe clerc and þe cniht;|And the cleric and the knight|
||demen euenliche riht.|judge impartially.|
||For after þat man soweþ;|For according as a man sows|
||al swilch he scal mowen.|even so he shall reap.|
|10|And efrilces mannes dom;|And everyman’s judgment|
||to his oŠere dure cherreþ.”|comes back to his own door.”|



Arngart’s parallel translation stays very close to the original text, even in problematic cases such as lines 2 _atheling_ , 4 _land_ and 5 _deeds_ , where the French loans _prince_ , _country_ and _actions_ would have been more appropriate (cf. _OED_ s.vv.), but he cannot avoid using several other French terms relating to government and law ( _govern_ , _judge_ , _impartially_ , _judgment_ ). 

_The Owl and the Nightingale_ , presumably composed in the early thirteenth century, represents a unique combination of progressive and conservative cultural features. The poem, which is extant in two manuscripts, is the earliest English vernacular example of debate poetry, a text type that was developed in the context of the Latin-based educational and legal system of the High Middle Ages.[30] Its metrical form, four-stress rhyming couplets with occasional alliteration, may be viewed as an adaptation of French octosyllabic couplets.[31] Nevertheless, the poem exhibits features that are of relevance in the present context. The two contestant birds frequently cite examples of King Alfred’s 

When did English begin? 161 

wisdom in order to support their own case, as is here illustrated by one example, the tone of the poem is popular, not at all courtly, and its lexicon is almost purely Germanic.[32] 

Table 4e. _The Owl and the Nightingale_ (Stanley 1972:lines 680–688) 

- 680 Her is to red wo hine kon, counsel F/advice F, knows E Vor neuer nis wit so kene So þane red him is a wene; counsel F/advice F, in doubt F þanne erest kumed his Šephede cunning E/astuteness FL/cleverness ?D þonne hit is alre mest on drede. doubt F 

- 685 For Aluered seide of olde quide– proverbs F/sayings E An ut hit his of horte islide; slipped E/escaped F “Wone þe bale is alre hecst calamity FL þonne is þe bote alre necst;” relief F/remedy FL/help E 

Taken together, these and other texts of the period demonstrate that, from a lexical view-point, the language preserved its Germanic character practically intact well into the thirteenth century, independently of the ongoing inflexional decay. 

## 7. An alternative periodization for the lexicon: Anglo-Saxon vs. English 

It is obvious that Henry Sweet’s tripartite periodization of English, which is based on the degree of morphological reduction, cannot be applied to the lexical development of the language. For the lexicon, we need a separate, bipartite periodization distinguishing Anglo-Saxon (comprising Old and Early Middle English) from English (comprising all later stages), which reflects the lexical and cultural facts. Post-Conquest England was practically a foreign colony for an extended period of time. As a consequence, Anglo-Saxon English eventually lost most of its pre-Conquest status as the language of law and government to superstratal French. But this superstratal influence of French hardly made itself felt in the few Early Middle English texts that were produced duringthe periodoffunctional trilingualism— evenin typicalsuperstratum areas of the lexicon, as could be demonstrated e.g. with terms relating to administration, law, and heroic fighting in such texts. During this extended period of time, several generations of inhabitants of post-Conquest England preserved their Anglo-Saxon lexicon and, more generally, seem to have looked back to their Anglo-Saxon cultural heritage when they expressed themselves in their own 

162 Angelika Lutz 

tongue.[33] 

This is not only shown by texts that are usually listed as evidence for Early Middle English but also by a considerable body of post-Conquest copies of Old English texts which reflect the pre-Conquest status of English as an all-purpose _Schriftsprache_ .[34] In my view, the two bodies of textual material represent two sides of the same, Anglo-Saxon, coin. 

By contrast, the English we are familiar with is basically a creation of the late thirteenth to fifteenth centuries when the French-speaking ruling classes of England gradually switched to the language of those they governed and, in the course of that process, imported their superstratal lexicon in large quantities.

11. Pollock & Maitland (1968:80) quite rightly contrast the English development with the situation in Germany: “It would be hardly too much to say that at the present day almost all our words that have a definite legal import are in a certain sense French words. The German jurist is able to expound the doctrines of Roman law in genuinely German words, (…) but an English or American lawyer who attempted this puritanical feat would find himself doomed to silence”. For the preservation of the Germanic legal terms in Modern Standard German consider, e.g. the equivalents of the English terms for the etymological groups ‘perjury’ ( _Meineid_ , _Meineidiger_ , _einen Meineid schwören_ ) and ‘guilt’ ( _Schuld_ , _schuldig_ , _schuldlos_ ).

Other definitions, e.g. Lehiste (1988:60, “Former primary language …”) and Hock & Joseph (1996:274, “Where prestige is unequal …”) are too vague to distinguish between the greatly differing contact effects of French and Latin on the English lexicon. 

14. Cf. Hock & Joseph (1996:274), Vennemann (1998:245–248; 2001). Lexical borrowing from a substratum language into the superstratum is practically restricted to linguistic contact in a habitat which is alien to the conquerors but familiar to the conquered population, e.g. in colonial North America and Australia between British colonists and indigenous tribes. In the British Isles, however, Anglo-Saxon conquerors and subjected Celts ‘shared’ a familiar European habitat. 

15. This is true, e.g. for Scheler (1977), Burnley (1992), Baugh & Cable (1993) and Leisi & Mair (1999). The only exception is Görlach’s _Sprachgeschichte_ (1994:131, 133), which provides definitions of the terms yet does not explicitly apply them to the situation in England. 

16. Scheler (1977:55): ‘a reflection of the far-reaching influence of French and AngloNorman culture and civilization on late medieval England’. Such influences have often been viewed in terms of lexical rejuvenation and enrichment, very recently by Clanchy (1998:35): “In the long term it can be argued that the Norman Conquest, so far from damaging the English language, gave it new life: first by releasing it from official constraints and then by enriching its vocabulary with numerous words derived from French and Latin”.

Barlow (1966:137) summarizes the situation at court in Norman and Angevin England as follows: “Throughout the Norman period (…) the French formed a fairly exclusive circle. It is possible that Henry I and his nephew, Stephen, could understand a little English; but they did not speak it: they had no need to”; and he continues: “The Angevins were more truly French than the Normans had been in 1066, if only because in the course of that century French culture had itself become more distinctive. Under the early Angevins the attractions of the French court became so strong that England became almost a French province” (p.160). Calin (1994:9) distinguishes between “the later Middle Ages, when French was an acquired language of prestige and culture, and its impact upon the English vernacular was very important” and the situation during the twelfth century: “The Norman and Angevin monarchs were truly Frenchmen as well as Englishmen, and more occupied with their French holdings and French concerns than their insular ones”.

life in twelfth-century England. Yet both scholars fail to explain why in England romances were written mostly in French until very late whereas in other countries poets produced romances in their native tongues.

## What’s afoot with word-final C? Metrical coherence and the history of English[*] 

C. B. McCully University of Manchester 

In the most recent contribution to work on the history of stress in the Germanic languages, Lahiri et al. (1999) propose to account for the apparent facts of the distribution and development of stress in Old English (OE), Old High German (OHG), and many of the Nordic languages by appealing to a structure informally called the Germanic foot (Dresher & Lahiri 1991; Lahiri et al. 1999:404, fn. 1), more formally stated as a resolved moraic trochee, see (1): 

(1) _The resolved moraic trochee_ (Lahiri et al. 1999) 

**==> picture [270 x 104] intentionally omitted <==**

**----- Start of picture text -----**<br>
F F F<br>s<br>s w s w s w w<br>(m m) (m m m) (m m m m)<br>æ þ el cy ni ng we se n de<br>**----- End of picture text -----**<br>


The example OE _wesende_ shows that the branch of the postulated Germanic foot that is sister to initial S may dominate a bimoraic rhyme, that is, the quantity of the medial syllable of the maximal string may be either light or heavy (L or H). Assignment of the Germanic foot is by a left > right parse, and initial syllables parsed into such a string are stronger (that is, more stressed than) any other syllables parsed into the relevant foot (Lahiri et al. 1999:338). Each example above shows that initial-maximal syllabification is the norm, while the bimoraic parsing of OE _æþel_ ‘noble’ shows that consonants in absolute word-finality (henceforth C#) are left unparsed. The key assumption 

174 C. B. McCully 

throughout, following Dresher & Lahiri (1991), is that stress assignment is, in the relevant periods of the languages in question, quantity-sensitive. Some further assumptions of Lahiri et al.’s (1999) model are tabled under (2): 

- (2) _Assumptions consequent on the Germanic foot_ (Lahiri et al. 1999) 

   - iteration: “[In Common Germanic] the direction of parsing (…) was left-to-right, with main stress assigned at the left edge, while in the modern languages the parsing is from right to left, with the rightmost foot being the most prominent” (1999:345) 

   - final destressing: non-branching final feet are deleted, thus OE _æþeling_ ‘prince’ [SWW after destressing] but _æþelinges_ (gen. sing.) [SWSW] 

   - extrametricality: (of final consonants, Lahiri et al. 1999:348ff.), driving Trisyllabic Shortening in Middle English (EME * _he¯ringes_ > _heringes_ , cf. PDE _herring_ ) 

Lahiri et al. are careful to stipulate that as far as English is concerned, C# extrametricality (CE) may only have entered the language later than final destressing hastakenplaceinOldEnglish,andmayprovidearationaleforTrisyllabicShortening in Middle English. The relevant passage of argument is reproduced here: 

## _CE in Early Middle English?_ 

[T]his shortening process can be explained if we take into account the possibility of consonant extrametricality and assume that the foot at this stage was still the same resolved moraic trochee and that the language preferred to maximise the stressed foot. _We believe that this was the beginning of consonant extrametricality which made the final syllable light._ Recall that the final foot in Old English was defooted if it did not branch (…) and word final long vowels had been shortened earlier (…). Thus, final closed syllables did not bear stress and behaved as if they were light, which quite naturally led language learners to posit consonant extrametricality. Extrametricality led to two significant changes in the metrical pattern [of Middle English] — a great many final stranded syllables, and an increase in words where the second foot was branching while the main stressed foot was not. 

(Lahiri et al. 1999:348–349; emphasis supplied) 

Later in their paper, which marshals an impressive amount of cross-linguistic data, Lahiri et al. suggest that alongside C# extrametricality in English, final syllable extrametricality also developed in ‘the continental languages’: 

[Middle High German and Middle Dutch] did not undergo trisyllabic shortening but only open syllable lengthening. One possible hypothesis is that (…) syllable extrametricality (and not consonant extrametricality) was introduced 

What’s afoot with word-final C? 175 

probably due to the influence of Romance loans (…). If this is correct, then it is clear why in words like Middle Dutch _water_ , the initial syllable was lengthened: to be able to have a regular moraic trochaic stressed foot (H). But what of words such as _weduwe_ > _we¯duwe_ , or _vedere_ > _ve¯dere_ ? Even after the final syllable was extrametrical, the first two syllables would make an appropriate moraic trochee (LL). Perhaps the answer is the same as that in Middle English, namely that the foot was still the resolved moraic trochee and after consonant extrametricality, the pressure to make the main stressed foot branching led to lengthening (…). The change of foot structure to a regular moraic trochee in all probability happened after the degemination and various levellings took place — indeed after the absorption of the Romance loans and a change in the direction of parsing. (Lahiri et al. 1999:358–359) 

Lahiri et al. therefore seem to envision an historical process whereby (a) final defooting was reinterpreted as (b) CE, which then interacted with loan processes, which in turn interacted with the development of (c) syllable extrametricality and/or (d) lengthening and shortening, eventually interacting with the development of a ‘regular moraic trochee’ and a contact-induced change in the direction of phonological parsing. In this scenario, final defooting and (resultant) CE provide the basis for the different routes most of the members of the Germanic language family took to optimizing the moraic trochee and developing the right>left parse. The remainder of this paper is devoted to showing why this scenario is unlikely. 

In the first place, it is unclear why CE is assumed to have taken place so late. The idea that consonants in absolute word finality are in some sense invisible to parsing is of course well attested in the literature (especially Hayes 1982, 1995; Prince 1983 among many other works), which everywhere makes the point that for extrametricality to ‘work’ at all, it must be ordered before parsing takes place. (Extrametricality is also intrinsically ordered. In models having both CE and final syllable extrametricality, as is assumed for PDE, CE is crucially ordered first.) Ga˛siorowski (1998), for example, restates a common view when, replying to the question ‘why should word-final consonants be weightless?’, he writes: 

Presumably because they easily become syllable onsets when derivational and inflectional suffixes with an initial vowel are added, and because languages tend to maintain moraic correspondence between identical stems with different suffixes. A final consonant is therefore ignored in counting moras, serving as a potential mooring-place for suffixation. (1998:127–128) 

176 C. B. McCully 

A simple case in point would be OE _æ.þel_ ‘noble’, with initial-maximal syllabification and supposedly extrametrical ·lÒ, but _æ.þe.lin.ges_ , gen. sing., where /l/ has resyllabified but where /s/ is now extrametrical. 

If CE is indeed motivated by potential (re)syllabification, then the ‘initialmaximal’ principle was surely operative in the Germanic language family long beforetheEMEperiod,ratherthanbeingtheinterpretationoffinaldestressing,as suggested by Lahiri et al. (1999). Interestingly, Dresher (1999) makes the same point, and in doing so attempts to salvage the notion of the Germanic foot which, it will be recalled (see (2) above), over-generates rather unfortunately on some final syllables. The consequence of the pattern of iteration, and supposed subsequent destressing, is shown below, where the boldened strings are the targets: 

- (3) _Final destressing in Germanic_ “Defoot a final weak nonbranching foot” (Dresher & Lahiri 1991:260) 

F F F mm m m m m m æ þ e l i n g oþØ e r c y n i n g u m 

It is at least undesirable to have stress turning up on inflectional endings depending merely on whether those endings happen to be closed by a final consonant ( _-um_ ). Although Dresher (1999) attempts to solve the problem by quite properly ordering CE before parsing, his reanalysis fails, at least for Old English, since there are many words whose final syllable is parsed as W(eak) even though the word-final string remains heavy after final CE has supposedly applied: 

- (4) _Destressing as an effect of CE?_ “Final consonants are extrametrical. (Extrametricality does not apply when the final consonant is required to supply the second mora in monosyllabic words such as _scip_ .)” (Dresher 1999) 

|Example|Underlying|CE|Stress|
|---|---|---|---|
|wæter|[LH]|[LL]|[SW]|
|sta¯nas|[HH]|[HL]|[SW]|
|werudes|[LLH]|[LLL]|[SWW]|
|cyningas|[LHH]|[LHL]|[SWW]|
|But||||
|ha¯lı¯g|[HH]|[HH]|[SW]|
|wı¯sdo¯m|[HH]|[HH]|[SW] etc.|



What’s afoot with word-final C? 177 

The conclusion must be that while reanalysing final destressing as an effect subsequent to CE (rather than the other way round) is superficially legitimate, some version of final destressing would, on this model, still be necessary to prune over-generated Germanic feet at the right edges of words. 

More is at stake here than arguments peculiar to the status of C#. In particular, note that the repair strategy of destressing seems necessary only because a given rule over-applies. Note also that CE is a stipulation that has a further entailment, in as much as its operation must be suspended in the case of lexical monosyllables whose rhymes are composed of VC# (OE _scip_ ‘ship’). Dresher (1999) defends this entailment on the grounds of minimality (the C# required for stress to obtain in e.g. _scip_ ), but this is a further mere stipulation, and like all unique stipulations, it weakens the grammar. 

Also at stake is the Germanic foot itself. On this model, feet are created iteratively, but the process over-generates — the (resultant) structures are (partly) weight-sensitive, but this entails repair strategies; and those repair strategies both imply, and interact with, additional machinery (extrametricality). Such a Byzantine modelling process itself becomes impeachable. Synchronically, on this model, one has a picture of consonants sometimes undergoing extrametricality, sometimes not, depending on the nature of the preceding rhyme. Again synchronically, some inflections turn up as stressed, some as unstressed, depending either on the composition of the inflection, the form of the preceding syllable, or (merely) the length of the word that is being scanned. Some root-medial syllables attract stress because they are heavy, but can only do so if their root-initial sister is light. Further, and in diachronic terms, to argue that syllable extrametricality in English ultimately develops from the reinterpretation of final defooting as CE is distinctly suspect, particularly when the necessity for defooting only comes about because of accidental properties of metrical mechanics, rather than from the nature of the linguistic material being parsed. In sum, the Germanic foot, and the processes by which it is assigned, may turn out to be the phonological equivalent of the camel produced by the unedifying cerebrations of an academic committee whose remit was to design a horse. 

In Dresher & Lahiri’s classic (1991) paper, the resolved moraic trochee allows apparently unrelated pieces of phonology — High Vowel Deletion (HVD), stress patterning, resolution in poetic (OE) metre, and Sievers’ Law in Gothic — to be brought together under a single unifying rubric, and the processes themselves to be analysed as responsive to a unique environment, namely that provided by the (head of the) Germanic foot. As they note (1991:270ff.) the 

178 C. B. McCully 

Germanic foot is an addition to the (universal) inventory of feet, see in particular Hayes (1995): 

- (5) _Universal foot inventory_ (L = light syllable; H = heavy syllable; m = mora) 

   - i. Syllabic trochee 

      - (x .) (x) 

   - ‘Form H L if possible; otherwise form H.’ 

   - ii. Moraic trochee 

      - (x .) (x .) 

   - ‘Form m m if possible, where m m is either LL or H.’ 

   - iii. Moraic iamb 

(. x) (. x) 

- ‘Form m m if possible, where m m is either LL or LH.’ 

Unfortunately, as Hutton (1998) points out, there are a number of significant problems with the basic hypothesis of the Germanic foot, as well as in the procedures entailed by its application. In universal foot typology, for example, one would expect the Germanic foot to be supported by evidence from other languages. But such evidence is ambiguous. Perhaps the strongest support comes from Cayuvava, where stress is (again, arguably) assigned by iterative R>L parsing which looks for the structure (m m m): 

- (6) a. _Cayuvava stress_ (Hutton 1998:855; Hayes 1995:309ff.) The Germanic foot (m m m)? ki.(hí.ße.re) ßa.ri.(é.ki.mi) (pò.po.he).(cé.ßa.ka) 

However, as Hayes shows, the same ternary pattern may be equally scanned by weak local parsing, maintaining foot binarity and allowing for extrametricality of final syllables: 

- (6) b. _Cayuvava stress_ (Hutton 1998:855; Hayes 1995:309ff.) Weak local parsing ki.(hí. ße).·reÒ ßa.ri.(é.ki).·miÒ (pò.po).he.(cé.ßa).·kaÒ 

As well as being susceptible to an alternative analysis based on a universal typology that has no place for the Germanic foot, the Cayuvava data also lack the quantitative distinctions (i.e. syllable structure is always CV) that would support the application of the Germanic foot. Hutton (1998:856ff.) also shows 

What’s afoot with word-final C? 179 

that data from e.g. Bani-Hassan Arabic, and from Latin — data which might otherwise be susceptible to an analysis through the Germanic foot — are at best equivocal. Bani-Hassan Arabic avoids clashing stress word-internally, and this is not an effect predicted by the Germanic foot. In (classical) Latin, on the other hand, if the Germanic foot were a constituent of the phonology, one would expect to find [LLL] and [LHL] feet alongside [H] and [HL], but the former patterns are apparently unattested. Hutton’s conclusion is that “it is difficult to find any unambiguous support for the Germanic foot outside the Germanic languages” (1998:857–858). 

The problems encountered by an analysis of Germanic stress that uses extrametricality (in the sense of Lahiri et al. 1999), rules, and weight-sensitive, iterative construction across a string parsed on a single derivational level, seem severe (see also McCully 1999, 2002). In the next part of this paper I show how one familiar model, drawn from Optimality Theory (OT; Prince & Smolensky 1993), not only provides additional motivation for the ‘invisibility’ of C#, but also makes the need for extrametricality or repair strategies redundant, at least for Old English. Such a theory also implies that the history of stress in the Germanic languages may well turn out to be a quieter and more persistent affair than the synchronic crises of parameter-switching entailed by rule-based models. (For an over-elaborate attempt to handle the history of OE and ME stress in terms of parameter-switching, see McCully 1997.) 

In terms of the visibility or otherwise of C#, several possibilities are open, among them the Null Parse (Prince & Smolensky 1993:48), local non-parsing (Prince & Smolensky 1993:65), or ‘Weak C’ (Bermúdez-Otero 1999). 

The Null Parse entails that in terms of input¤output correspondence, no structure at all is assigned to the input (and thus, constraints may never be violated, since they will be vacuously satisfied). As Prince & Smolensky show, however, Null Parse, while technically available to the grammar, is not apparently operative in a wide range of ‘real’ linguistic applications (1993:48). (It is very clear that for Old English, syllables have input structure, including initialmaximal syllabification, and this procedure alone makes Null Parsing illegitimate.) Underparsing or non-parsing is perhaps more promising. Prince & Smolensky (1993:65) explicitly adopt this tactic for the scansion of word-final closed syllables in (pre-Classical) Latin, as shown in (7): 

180 C. B. McCully 

- (7) _Local non-parsing of C in pre-Classical Latin_ (Prince & Smolensky 1993) a. Non-parsing of µ 

**==> picture [92 x 57] intentionally omitted <==**

**----- Start of picture text -----**<br>
σ σ<br>µ µ = µ<br>V V<br>**----- End of picture text -----**<br>


(where WSP>>Parse µ , therefore, _inter alia_ , LH is parsed as LL) 

- b. Non-parsing of C 

**==> picture [63 x 58] intentionally omitted <==**

**----- Start of picture text -----**<br>
σ<br>µ µ<br>V C<br>**----- End of picture text -----**<br>


(Examples: _canis_ (VC# > V#), _voluptates_ ( … VCVCC … > VCV.C); Prince & Smolensky 1993:65) 

In the last instance, the mora linked to C# is analysed as non-associated with the σ node, and this part of the phonological grammar is driven by the (attestable) assumption that for this variety of Latin, violations of mora parsing are licensed by its low rank in the constraint hierarchy, cf. its higher rank in Classical Latin (where morae count as equally visible to a non-superordinate weight-to-stress principle [WSP]). In this variety, the ‘invisibility’ of C# is aligned with underparsing of morae elsewhere, and therefore does not require a separate statement. This would seem to be an analytical gain, but problems again arise: since C# is also ‘extrametrical’ in Classical Latin (a dialect where Parse-µ is undominated by WSP), we cannot adopt this solution for that synchrony, i.e. CE would still require stipulation. Further, in Classical Latin, lexical monosyllables of the form #XV# are unattested, and ‘extrametricality’ would still have to be suspended for lexical monosyllables by fiat, if it were assumed to exist at all. Nor, cross-linguistically, should we assume non-parsing of this precise kind for Old English: there’s no evidence for syllabically unparsed morae elsewhere, cf. (7a) above, and so the extrametricality of C# cannot be aligned with a wider, and productive, process of mora-dropping. 

Given that erstwhile C# is incontrovertibly attached to following material under initial-maximal syllabification, one way of thinking about this segment’s position in (OE) syllable structure is to claim that it is attached not to the Rhyme node (Rh), but to the root of the Prosodic Word (PrWd) itself, as shown in (8): 

181 

- (8) _Attaching C and C# in Old English_ 

   - a. in # 

**==> picture [180 x 122] intentionally omitted <==**

**----- Start of picture text -----**<br>
Prosodic Word (PrWd)<br>Σ<br>σ<br>Rh<br>µ µ µ<br>o r d (OE word ‘word(s)’)<br>**----- End of picture text -----**<br>


- b. in initial position (/d/), and final position (/s/) 

**==> picture [234 x 122] intentionally omitted <==**

**----- Start of picture text -----**<br>
PrWd<br>Σ<br>σ σ<br>Rh Onset Rh<br>µ µ ] µ µ µ<br>o r d e s (OE wordes , gen. sing.)<br>**----- End of picture text -----**<br>


The reasons for attaching C# like this may be modelled in OT in terms of a wider theory of syllabification.[1] It seems clear, for instance, that stress-bearing entities in Old English are moraic trochees (stress is partly quantity-sensitive, but it is also sensitive to high-ranked morphological constraints, see below). This nets a pair of constraints: Troch (Trochee) and FtBin (Foot Binarity). Troch ensures that feet will be labelled [SW]. FtBin, which I here adopt in its original form (Prince & Smolensky 1993:47: “[f]eet are binary _at some level of analysis_ ”; emphasis supplied), ensures that feet are either minimal-optimal [µµ] or non-optimal [µµµ]. Alongside these constraints on foot form, there is a family of Parse constraints, including Parse-C (‘Consonants must be optimally parsed, that is, associated with a footed µ’). 

Recall that one of the central ideas in OT is that constraints may be violated in order to satisfy a higher-ranked constraint (Kager 1999:12). Clearly, though C# in (8a and 8b) has been aligned with a mora, that mora has not been parsed into a foot, and therefore Parse-C has been violated. If one asks what higher- 

182 C. B. McCully 

ranked constraint licenses this violation, the answer turns out to be a constraint that plays a starring role in English phonology (apparently, of all periods). That is Non-Finality (NonFin): phonological constituents in absolute finality (/_#) may not bear stress (although on lexical compounds, see below). In OE _word_ , for example, the string _-or_ ] _d_ ], with extrasyllabic /d/, satisfies both FtBin ( _-or_ is bimoraic) and NonFin ( _-or_ ] is not word-final). In order to maintain this state of affairs in Old English, C is associated to the highest possible node within the word consistent with non-violation of FtBin (‘Weak C’; Bermúdez-Otero 1999). On this account, many final C’s ( _word_ , _cyning_ , _sco¯p_ ‘poet’) will not be ‘extrametrical’ but simply extrasyllabic. Such C#’s will never participate in weight-tostress computation (WSP). 

Even so, there are some C#’s which _must_ contribute to weight, if only to satisfy FtBin. Recall those cases where ‘extrametricality’ had to be suspended — VC# lexical monosyllables. If for such strings we allow C# to attach to PrWd without further ado, then of course stress will never be assigned to the solitary remaining V. However, FtBin compels attachment of the final C to a final mora. Both V and C# participate in FtBin. This is so particularly because FtBin is itself dominated by a top-ranked (and undominated) constraint, Root (‘Lexical Roots must contain at least one syllable that bears stress’). The hierarchy that gets this out is seen in (9): 

- (9) _Partial constraint ranking for OE stress and syllabification_ Root>>NonFin, FtBin, Troch>>Parse-C, Weak C 

Applied to OE _scip_ , the ranking operates as in (10): 

(10) PrWd [relevant constraint: ROOT] Σ σ [relevant constraint: FtBin] Rh [relevant constraint: Parse-C, Weak C] µ µ V C 

(Weak C violated in order to satisfy FtBin; see fnn. 2 and 3) 

On this model, no extra stipulations have to engage the grammar. Where FtBin and/or NonFin are satisfied, C# may function as a mora visible within the word- 

What’s afoot with word-final C? 183 

final Rh, or may be extrasyllabic.[2] CE does not _per se_ feature in the phonology; the suspension of extrametricality is no longer to be arbitrarily stipulated for lexical monosyllables. In short, the present model appears to be more economical than its predecessors. 

If CE is not a feature of Old English (or indeed of Middle English or later Englishes), one might want briefly to ask about other forms of extrametricality (‘Noun’ or ‘Adjective Extrametricality’; cf. the remarks of Lahiri et al. 1999:358–359 on the development of ‘syllable extrametricality’ in the continental languages). I assume here, following the classic model of OT developed by Prince & Smolensky (1993) and Minkova (1997), that NonFin also plays a role in Middle English and later periods of stress assignment. NonFin leads simply to the _effect_ of extrametricality: 

**==> picture [235 x 10] intentionally omitted <==**

**----- Start of picture text -----**<br>
(11) NonFin in OE and subsequent periods of English stress<br>**----- End of picture text -----**<br>


**==> picture [308 x 141] intentionally omitted <==**

**----- Start of picture text -----**<br>
PrWd PrWd PrWd<br>σ σ σ σ σ σ<br>Rh Rh Rh Rh Rh Rh<br>µ µ µ µ µ µ µ ( µ ) µ µ ( µ )<br>o: þ e (r) a s p I (k) i: v (l)<br>(OE oþer¿ ) (PDE aspic ) (PDE evil )<br>(Final syllables will never bear main stress: NonFin>>Parse-C)<br>**----- End of picture text -----**<br>


As Prince & Smolensky show (1993:53), NonFin, in concert with (but ranked above) WSP, ensures that the poorly-formed trochee VCVV (e.g. in words such as Latin _amo_ /amo:/) is an optimal output, given the other, even worse, candidates. In fact, NonFin>>WSP — a state that apparently obtains in English throughout at least the OE and ME periods (Minkova 1997) — will render main word-stress on final syllables distinctly sub-optimal.[3] The only cases where word-final prominence, including secondary stress, unambiguously obtains are, it seems, the final elements of two-member compounds, and VV(C)# strings where these occur in the right branch of an otherwise well-formed trochee (i.e. not as themselves the head of a syllabic trochee). In the first set of these cases, Root,actingwithInitialProminenceinOE(Minkova1997),ensuresthatalthough they are final within their superordinate PrWds, the second monosyllabic 

184 C. B. McCully 

elements of compounds will receive stress. In the second set of cases, Initial Prominence, and Non-Finality, act so that VVX# behaves as if it is optimally VX#, and thus non-stress bearing. These contrasts are schematized in (12): 

- (12) _Non-Finality, Initial Prominence, and ‘extrametricality’_ a. Stress in compounds

[S S W]; Initial Prominence, NonFin)<br>**----- End of picture text -----**<br>


What’s afoot with word-final C? 185 

Putting these remarks together, and taking data from compound stress and secondary stress into account, one arrives at a revised constraint ranking for primary stress assigment in OE: 

- (13) _Revised (though still incomplete) constraint ranking for stress and syllabification in OE_ 

   - Root>>Initial Prominence>>NonFin, FtBin, Troch>>Parse-C, Weak C 

The ranking appears to hold good also for Old High German and Old Norse, with the rider that a high-ranked adjacency constraint (arguably, *Lapse) is active in Old Norse, whereas it is low-ranked in Old High German (and arguably still lower ranked in Old Saxon, see McCully 1999). If (13) is on the way to being correct, and if the ranking is persistent into Middle English (which, like the variety of pre-Classical Latin discussed above, shows limited innovation only to the extent of re-ranking of WSP — see here Minkova 1997), then one might ask about the status of the ‘Germanic foot’. In the terms developed here, the Germanic foot is an analytical chimaera: the moraic trochee, as an optimal product of FtBin, and acting in concert with Initial Prominence and NonFin, captures all the structures formerly adopted by the Germanic foot; and moraic trochees, too, help to explain the workings of Sievers’ Law (Bermúdez-Otero 1999). Given that this OT account is formally more economical than the ‘Germanic foot’ model of diachronic stress, and is apparently equally adequate to explain apparently unrelated phenomena (including poetic resolution, which turns out to be merely a special case of the moraic trochee, or FtBin acting in concert with Root>>Initial Prominence), then it would appear that the ‘Germanic foot’ should play no role in analyses of Germanic phonology. True, sub-optimal trochees exist in Old English (just as they do in PDE) — consider strings such as _cyning_ , and cf. the surface representation of PDE _rabbi_ — but the existence of these wretched structures should not be allowed to legitimize the postulation of a foot for which there is otherwise little evidence.

* This paper includes material originally prepared for the plenary lecture “Theorizing a mess of stress” at the 11th ICEHL in Santiago, 7–11 September 2000. I am grateful for discussion of some of these points to Donka Minkova, Bob Stockwell, Richard Hogg, Elan Dresher, and Ricardo Bermúdez-Otero, and would like to thank the conference organizers, in particular Teresa Fanego, for their kind invitation to speak at, and participate in, such a well-run and 

186 C. B. McCully 

intellectually productive event. I am also grateful to Richard Hogg, and to Monika Schmid, for having read earlier drafts of this paper and for having suggested several improvements in argument and style. Mistakes that remain here are wholly my own. 

1. And indeed, of grammar. Bermúdez-Otero (1999:25, fn. 4) suggests that in “recent OT work, edge extraprosodicity phenomena are generally viewed not as ‘invisibility’, but as ‘weak edge’ effects (Spaelti 1994). According to this conception, the right edge of the prosodic word is inherently weak, in the sense that designated rightmost constituents are preferably dominated by the fewest possible nodes”. 

2. This links with the theory of input optimization discussed in Bermúdez-Otero (1999:30ff.). Crudely, let a possible input set I be infinite (“richness of the base”, see also Kager 1999:19). Let there be a constraint hierarchy H whose best satisfaction is representation Ii, yielding output O. The learner therefore chooses Ii as the input representation for O (see also Inkelas 1994, cited in Bermúdez-Otero 1999). In the terms of this paper, learners therefore choose /Ip/ as the Ii rhymal structure for _scip_ (and not */p/ or */i/) because that representation best satisfies FtBin. In these (English) VC# examples, extrametricality therefore does not have to be ‘suspended’: extraprosodicity is a natural inference from input¤ouput processing. 

3. In present-day synchronies, compare British English _garage_ /’garI3/ with the end-stressed variety heard in many accents of American English. End-stressed bisyllables in (Present-day) English are driven at least partly by socio-linguistic factors (such as prestige, see Thomas & McCully, in progress). Given the historically high (and persistent) rank of NonFin in English, it is moot whether there is right-hand _default_ main stress in Present-day English, and it is entirely possible that notions such as ‘End Rule (right)’ may have to be re-thought. Burzio (1994:63ff.) for example makes an interesting case for superheavy syllables (only) bearing right-hand main stress in English — which, if true, would mean English shared that disposition with Present-day German.
