## Dan Michel 

## Fossil or innovator?[*] 

## John Scahill 

Keio University 

## 1. Introduction 

The _Ayenbite of Inwyt_ gives the student of Middle English two rare luxuries: a precise date — the colophon tells us that the book was completed in 1340 — and a precise provenance, Canterbury. Just as importantly, our sole manuscript has on any view a very simple textual history: both the regularity of the language and patterns of leaving gaps that are subsequently filled by the first hand or have the French word noted in the margin (Varnhagen 1879:37–39, fn.1) suggest a holograph, though there are, puzzlingly, some occasional corrections in the first hand to more Kentish forms, for which the readiest explanation is that the scribe was using an exemplar (Gradon 1979:10–11). Despite these, it will be assumed here that the manuscript represents — very copiously — the language of one person. 

What is more, I will speak of this person as Dan Michel, although the statement that _Þis boc is dan Michelis of Northgate ywrite an englis of his oŠene hand_ (1/5–6) might of course be that of a copyist.[1] This convenient designation does however imply that no significant gap can be found between authorial and scribal layers in the language, and that this language, like Dan Michel, comes from Canterbury. No evidence has emerged to refute either implication. There is a further possible implication: if this Dan Michel is the same as one named in earlier Canterbury documents, he was an old man by 1340, and the language should be viewed as that of the late thirteenth century, rather than the midfourteenth (Gradon 1979:12). Anthony Warner (p.c.) has observed that the identification may be worth reinvestigating, and the discussion that follows does not depend on any precise dating of the language; indeed, its failure to 

190 John Scahill 

agree with common notions of either Early or Late Middle English will become evident. For the language of the _Ayenbite_ seems consistent only with itself, mixing conservative native elements with innovative French ones, and if it amounts to a mere nonce-language, produced by the exigencies of (not particularly skilful) translation, it is no linguistic goldmine. However, I shall argue that in important aspects this language shows a thorough assimilation of native and foreign, which furnishes both evidence of linguistic developments in Kent and hints about the process of orthographic change. 

## 2. Gallicism 

The first impression, nevertheless, is of a striking mixture of conservatism and innovation, englishness and gallicism. Thus, a sequence such as _hi lokeþ zuich ane fieblene castel_ (‘they lock such a feeble castle’: 227/19–20) gives us in the recent loan-word _fieblene_ a distinctively Kentish digraph and a very AngloSaxon accusative masculine singular adjectival ending. Syntax and idiom, too, can give the same impression of exotic structures in native form. Thus, _deþ zuo moche be hare zenne_ (37/29) is scarcely intelligible until _zuo moche_ is recognized as equivalent to French _tant_ — giving the sense ‘so contrives by her sin’, and the strange placement of _is_ in _Þereuore þe uerþe stape is of þise uirtue: wylni to by y-knawe_ (‘therefore the fourth step in this virtue is to be willing to be known’: 132/22–23) is exactly that of the French original. 

In lexis, there are a substantial number of exotic borrowings for which the _Ayenbite_ is the earliest citation in the _Middle English Dictionary_ , such as _atrayt_ ‘leisurely’ and _papelard_ ‘sycophant’. Conversely, Dan Michel’s choice of words seems at times to strike us as puristic, but a more complex process may be at work: _aboutestondinges_ for ‘circumstances’ is used by no-one else, to judge from the _Middle English Dictionary_ , but can be seen as very literal translation — element for element, rather than word for word. At this point one might feel that whatever the purity of its text, the work is suspect as a linguistic corpus, verging on French dressed up in English forms, representing not so much a real _état de langue_ as what Dan Michel was prepared to reproduce if it was in his original: in effect only a passive repertoire. It is not in dispute that he is generally a very literal translator, who focuses on the wording of the phrase at hand, rather than the broader argument. A long holograph is not quite the treasure it might seem if it amounts to little more than a continuous gloss. 

Dan Michel 191 

## 3. Orthography: _s/z_ 

However, the appearance of unintegrated native and foreign components is not borne out at the level of orthography; rather we find detailed indications of how the different elements in Dan Michel’s language interacted. Three of his most distinctive practices are particularly instructive. The blizzard of _z_ s, as in (…) _he be þise zeue yefþes: bestrepeþ þe zeue zennes of oure herten_ (‘he by these seven gifts strips the seven sins from our hearts’: 127/27–28), sets the _Ayenbite_ manuscript apart from all other Middle English. Morpheme-initially, the distribution is straightforward: _z_ represents OE _s_ , while _s_ represents Old French or Latin _s_ , with the proviso that _z_ occurs only before vowels and the semi-vowel of words like _zuift_ ; before all consonants, including probably voiced ones such as _m_ and _l_ , only _s_ is found. Table 1 gives examples, with the number of tokens for words showing high frequency or variation.[2] 

Table 1. Morpheme-initial _s/z_ 

_sacrifice_ , _scin_ ‘skin’, _slep(-)_ 21, _smac/smak_ 36, _snaw_ , _spek(-)_ 100, _storm_ , _suspect zeuenty_ , _zix_ , _zuete(-)_ ‘sweet’ 57, _zuift_ , _zuin_ ‘swine’ _bezeche_ , _azide zouteres_ ‘shoemaker’s’, _zalmes_ ‘psalms’, _Zacarien_ ‘see’ _(-)z-_ 135, _(-)s-_ 31. But note this includes _yzy(-)_ 20, _ysy(-)_ 24. ‘set’ (verb) _(y)ze-_ 101, _(y)se-_ 4 ‘sin’ (noun) _zenne(-)_ 455, _senne_ 1 ‘so’ _zuo_ 477, _suo_ 9 ‘such’ _zui/ych_ 207, _sui/ych_ 1 ‘saint’ _za-_ 53, _sa-_ 152, _se-_ 4 

Dan Michel’s consistency in this rare distinction is remarkable: _z_ in non-OE words is confined to the alien _Zacarien_ ‘Zechariah’, and two pre-Conquest borrowings, ‘psalm’ and ‘shoemaker’. In the other direction there is only a small leakage: OE morpheme-initial _s_ very occasionally appears as _s_ , but mostly in prefixed forms; in particular, the figures for ‘see’ include 24 occurrences of _ysy(-)_ as against 20 of _yzy(-)_ . The word ‘saint’ is the only one over which Dan Michel shows real uncertainty: both _s_ and _z_ are common, evidently because the word has been borrowed twice, once in the pre-Conquest form _sanct_ , subject to voicing. 

Two interpretations are possible. One is that his language has acquired a new voicing contrast, which can only have come from French and Latin loans in initial /s/, after the voicing of OE initial /s/.[3] (Fisiak 1985 summarizes the 

192 John Scahill 

evidence, which indicates that the voicing of initial fricatives had affected Kent before Dan Michel’s time). The other two sources of fricative voicing contrasts in Middle English are degemination and loss of final /6/ (Kurath 1956). But in this dialect, as there is no regular medial degemination or loss of final /6/, loans are the only possible significant source of the contrast. On this view _z_ , from its rare use in initial borrowings such as _Zacarien_ , has been pressed into service to represent the contrast. From the thirteenth century it was available to do so, because the dental affricate that it had represented in French was lost (Pope 1934:§§193–194, §1183). 

The alternative interpretation is the proposal of Magnusson (1971:197) that the initial voicing affected all words, loans included; but the loans kept their inherited spellings. This is conceivable: modern dialect surveys show a moderate amount of fricative voicing in Latin and Romance borrowings (Anderson 1987:142, Map 108). The choice is of some moment. Are we dealing with the disorganization of an orthography through borrowing, or the reorganization of an orthography to represent the phonemic results of borrowing? The difficulty with Magnusson’s proposal is that the _z_ spellings are on any view highly innovative; and a new phonemic contrast is the only plausible stimulus for this innovation. 

The conclusion that there is a new phonemic contrast behind the innovation is corroborated by what happens medially in voiced environments, where the voicing contrast is neutralized, and there is free variation in spelling, regardless of origin; e.g. 18 occurrences of _prays_ - ‘praise’ as against 14 of _prayz-/ praiz-_ , though with some preference for _s_ , particularly in the common ‘as’, for which _ase_ occurs 957 times and _aze_ only 12. The variability in prefixed forms is an aspect of this word-internal variation. The fact that medial _z_ appears at all constitutes a further difficulty for Magnusson’s view that the _z_ spellings are simply inherited, for here they have no historical source, French or English. As they do not spring from phonemic necessity either, one might wonder why they occur. Evidently the phonemic change that gave rise to the use of initial _z_ has been thoroughly assimilated, so that the symbols for either member of the neutralized pair can occur medially (the innovative _z_ encouraged no doubt by the phonetic voicing). This makes it surprising that initial _z_ is never used before a consonant (as distinct from a semi-vowel), although analogy, modern dialect evidence, and forms such as _uless_ and _urend_ make it almost certain that the initial consonant was voiced. But Flasdieck (1958:361) observes that the only initial _z_ spellings known to Dan Michel would have been followed by a vowel; he evidently innovated on the basis of elements in known orthographies. 

Dan Michel 193 

This use of _z_ is very isolated: in a wide (though not complete) survey of south-eastern ME manuscripts I have found only three parallels. Cambridge, St John’s College 197 (G.30), a translation of _Le Manuel des Péchés_ , is placed by the _Linguistic Atlas of Late Mediaeval English_ in north-north-west Sussex (LP5700). Klaus Bitterling (1998:29) dates the manuscript to 1430–1440 (and the original translation to 1380–1400). In the early folios, _z_ - is common in words of AngloSaxon origin; thus, on ff.1–4, there are 19 such spellings, including all four occurrences of ‘seven’; 11 of the 20 occurrences of ‘sin’ and its derivatives, and two of the 12 occurrences of ‘so’. Bitterling (1998:29; cf. also 199–200) observes that there are some corrections of _z_ - to _s_ - by the original scribe; e.g. _zome_ to _some_ (f.63r) and _zey_ to _sey_ (f.69r). Perhaps for the same reason, _z_ - spellings gradually become rarer; on ff.76–80r there are only three, and none thereafter, except in the alien _Zabatany_ . Medial _z_ occurs occasionally throughout, regardless of origin, and it is worth observing that voicing of initial _f_ is rarely indicated. 

Hand B of Oxford, Bodleian 110 (which appears to me to be of the fifteenth century) twice spells ‘sin’ and its derivatives with _z_ , four times with _s_ . There are no other occurrences of _z_ (as opposed to _z_ -like _yogh_ ) in this or any of the other six hands that appear in the English texts. The _Linguistic Atlas of Late Mediaeval English_ (LP5651) places several of these hands, including B, in south-west Surrey. 

Finally, Cambridge University Library Ii.3.21, has in its text of Chaucer’s _Boece_ sporadic initial _z_ spellings.[4] Considering that initial fricative voicing appears to have affected virtually all southern England and parts of the southwest Midlands, it is remarkable that Dan Michel showed it so regularly in this case, when hardly anyone else ever did so at all. 

## 3.1 Orthography: _ku/qu_ 

The second idiosyncrasy is _ku_ in words of OE origin, in contrast with _qu_ in loan-words. As is evident from Table 2, there are exceptions, particularly in non-initial position, but the predominant distinction is clear. It also appears to be unique. In fact _ku_ is rare: the only other clear case in south-eastern Middle English is the Bodleian Digby 4 manuscript of _Poema Morale_ (Zupitza 1878) where it occurs twice, in variation with six _qu_ spellings; but the text contains no relevant words of non-OE origin. Unluckily the _Kentish Sermons_ of Bodleian Laud Misc. 471 (Hall 1920) contain no relevant words at all, though it should be mentioned that in British Library, Cotton Vespasian A.22, which Laing (1993) dates to the last quarter of the twelfth century or the first of the thirteenth, while 

194 John Scahill 

_c÷_ predominates, there is an isolated form _kuu¯_ , interpreted by Morris (1868:243) as “?kueðinde” (evidently ‘speaking’). 

Table 2. _ku/qu_ 

‘evil’ _kuead(-)_ 261, _quead(-)_ 24 ‘please’ _kuem-_ 11, _quem-_ 7, _yquem-_ 2 ‘queen’ _kuen(-)_ 4, _quen_ 1 ‘quench’ _kuenche_ 8, _aquenc-_ 2 ‘quick’ _kui-_ 2, _qui-_ 7 ‘testament’ _bekuydes_ 1, _bequide_ 1 ‘quaint’ and derivatives _ku-_ 0, _qu-_ 5 ‘quit’ _ku-_ 2, _qu-_ 6; ‘acquit’ _aku-_ 0, _aqu-_ 2 

Again, two explanations are possible: a phonemic distinction, or the importation of the French and Latin words with their graphs. The only probable phonemic distinction is a fricativization or devoicing of /w/ (an unstable sound in Middle English anyway) in the OE set, so that the /kw/ sequence had to be reintroduced into the language with the borrowings. The example of _s_ vs. _z_ shows that Dan Michel’s milieu was capable of innovatively rendering a new phonemic distinction occasioned by borrowing, and that if not the innovator himself he was at any rate capable of reproducing such distinctions rather accurately. Scahill (1997) argues for a sporadic sound change of the relevant kind in Middle English, but Laing (1998:279, fn.19) proposes a purely orthographic interpretation of the data. The degree of variability in individual words is perhaps rather high for a phonemic contrast, and it may be easier to suppose that both the imported words and the native have kept their graphs, though this explanation implies that _ku_ was better established in Dan Michel’s environment than the surviving evidence indicates. 

## 3.2 Orthography: _ss_ 

The use of _ss_ for the descendant of OE _sc_ is less exotic, but shows further aspects of the formation of Dan Michel’s orthography. In this case, no new phoneme is in question, at least since the appearance of the sound value [w] from earlier [sχ], generally dated to late in the OE period (cf. Flasdieck 1958:345–352; Luick 1964:§691). Both the graph itself, and some rhymes with /s/ in _The Owl and the Nightingale_ , have been accounted for by supposing that it stands for some sound more [s]-like than [w] (Onions 1936:101); but there is no reason to 

Dan Michel 195 

suppose that _ss_ was chosen for the spelling system of the _Ayenbite_ from an array of possibilities on grounds of phonetic suitability, and the rhyming practices of _The Owl and the Nightingale_ (as analysed by Stanley 1972:111–112) do not appear to be such that s:sˆ, say, was acceptable, while s:sh was not. 

## Table 3. _ss_ 

OE _sc_ : _ssake_ , _ssame_ , _ssip_ , _ssroud_ OE /s/ + consonant: _sslepe_ 1, _sspeke_ 1, _ssmak_ 1 Geminated after stressed short vowel: _nesssse_ ‘soft’ 1, _uisssse_ ‘fish (prep. case)’ 1, _esssse_ ‘ashes’ 2 Geminated /s/: _asse_ ‘ass’ Medial in French words: _passion_ , _punissi_ vs. _lessoun_ , _dissiplines_ 

Initially, expected /w/ is spelt _ss_ almost without exception; finally, single _s_ is fairly common, however. The genesis of this spelling can easily be guessed: _sc_ becomes ambiguous because it also represents an [sk] sequence in Norse, French and Latin borrowings, and no longer having either phonetic reality or OE scribal tradition to support it, becomes obsolete for [w]. The modern _sh_ spelling is found in for example Orrm and the homilies of Cambridge Trinity College 335, but is still uncommon at the date Dan Michel wrote, not yet being part of a set of established ‘Consonant plus _h’_ spellings. The common _sch_ requires three graphs to represent a single phoneme. Single _s_ , predominant in words of this origin in the _Kentish Sermons_ (22 cases out of 29) and the Digby 4 _Poema Morale_ (68 out of 73), is a seriously ambiguous replacement; while _ss_ is ambiguous only medially. At this date French has no [w], except in northeastern and eastern dialects, where it is typically denoted by _x_ (Pope 1934:§315, and §xvi, p.489); given the existing sound-value of _x_ , we can understand why this representation might not appeal. What French did offer was _ss_ for a palatal sound in a verbal suffix that came to be pronounced [w] in many cases in English. Dan Michel is probably not an innovator here: five of the remaining word-initial cases in the _Kentish Sermons_ are spelt with _ss_ , and I have also found the spelling in the four lines of English in a thirteenth-century Canterbury manuscript, Cambridge, Trinity College 108 ( _ssameles_ ), and in the Kentish sections of Oxford, Merton 248 (second half of the fourteenth century) — _ffless_ and _by ssad_ (‘be-shed, drenched’; cf. also _OED_ s.v. _beshed_ v.), alongside _shal_ . (The _Linguistic Atlas of Late Mediaeval English_ records some occurrences in manuscripts from Gloucestershire and adjacent areas). 

The question arises of why Dan Michel uses _ss_ initially, although patchy 

196 John Scahill 

modern dialect evidence and analogy might lead us to expect a voiced sound in most environments. (Fisiak 1985:13–14 argues that this fricative was indeed voiced within the ME period). But while there were certainly precedents for _ss_ , the theoretical analogous graph for [Š], _zz_ , never occurred initially in any orthography known to Dan Michel. (The absence of a phonemic contrast is also pertinent). He does go beyond any known precedent in his use of _ssss_ spellings for the sound doubled after a stressed short vowel as in _uisssse_ . (Flasdieck 1958:383–391 discusses this gemination). Not even Orrm, rugged pioneer and lover of double consonants, does anything as grotesque as using the same graph four times consecutively; here the analogy of the systematic doubling of other consonant graphs produced an undoubted innovation, without even a phonemic contrast to spur it. 

## 4. Kentish or idiosyncratic? 

The language of the _Ayenbite_ is, then, something more than the accidental outcome of closely glossing a French text in a conservatively English morphology and orthography. In detail, its orthography shows development through the interaction of native and foreign influences. We have seen one almost uniquely represented phonemic contrast (/z/ vs. /s/) that arose in this dialect through lexical borrowing; one unique spelling contrast ( _ku_ vs. _qu_ ) due to borrowing, whether or not the borrowing produced a new phonemic opposition; and one spelling ( _ss_ for /w/) with a probable French basis that Dan Michel carries through with unequalled consistency. We would very much like to know whether Dan Michel’sorthographywasofhisowndevising.Theamountoffirst-handevidence for his language is admittedly unrivalled, but a search has revealed no predecessors or successors, or any Canterbury or St Augustine’s school: it is hard to find parallels for many of the individual traits, never mind the whole ensemble. 

What is more, even when attention is turned away from orthography, the language may seem to be an idiosyncratic cocktail, combining elements from different periods of linguistic history: its heavily gallicized vocabulary is strikingly unlike that of the thirteenth-century West Midland texts that it resembles morphologically. Yet the same mixture of grammatical conservatism and lexical innovation is found in the _Kentish Sermons_ . It is best viewed as the product of southern traditions in a literary environment that was conducive to heavy borrowing. Admittedly, both works are translated from French, but so was LaŠamon’s _Brut_ . It seems worth entertaining the hypothesis that Kentish 

Dan Michel 197 

had become exceptionally receptive to loan-words by the thirteenth century, sociolinguistic conditions in post-Conquest Canterbury (and perhaps Rochester) being a likely factor, so as to allow considerable relexification. This could simply mean that the large influx of loan-words that according to most histories of English (e.g. Burnley 1992) began around the middle of the fourteenth century started earlier in Kent, perhaps because the phase of widespread language shift and bilingualism set in earlier there.[5] A preliminary survey of other Early Middle English manuscripts from the south-east has not uncovered much evidence, but the matter deserves further investigation. 

## 5. Orthographic change 

What is clear is that the _Ayenbite_ shows massive borrowing and a considerable amount of unidiomatic translationese in its syntax, but few signs of morphological transition; where the pressure of transition does manifest itself is in the orthography, with some related developments evident in the phonology. We tend to think of the transition from Old English to Early Middle English as characterized by morphological collapse, yet here the transition affects the morphology last and least. 

Angelika Lutz (2002) has observed that while the conventional periodization of English into Old, Middle and Modern is primarily based on morphology, a lexical periodization would establish a single major division, towards the middle of the fourteenth century, separating periods of essentially Germanic and heavily gallicized vocabulary. Rather awkwardly the _Ayenbite_ belongs in the later period lexically, but early in the Middle English period morphologically. The natural response to such apparent difficulties is to say that they are an artefact of our period divisions, which are as useful and as chimerical as dialect boundaries. In general, the different modules of language can change at different rates cross-dialectally; and in the particular case of Middle English, externally-driven lexical change and internally-driven morphological change proceeded rather independently. As Thomason & Kaufman (1988:308) observe: “Dialects of English most in contact with French underwent no simplification that can be traced to French; they are among the most conservative Middle English dialects, and no doubt would have been so with or without contact with French”. 

Can, then, orthography be treated as a module of language? Work on language change has tended to focus on the spoken language, implicitly 

198 John Scahill 

assuming that the ‘natural’ processes that apply to it are inapplicable to ‘artificial’ written language, a view indicated by Roger Lass (1997:65): 

We don’t seem to have much if anything of a theory of orthographic (as divorced from phonological) change. My guess is that there can’t be any such theory, because spelling as a language-faculty ‘module’ (…) is probably not nearly as autonomous (if it is at all) as phonology. The safest bet would seem to be taking orthographic variation as (roughly) mirroring phonological (…). That is the graphic shape that surfaces represents the phonological shape that would have surfaced if the utterance had been in another medium. 

But though orthography is relatively unautonomous, spelling systems are notoriously prone to coming adrift from the phonologies they purportedly represent, and even if every spelling system had a perfect one-to-one correspondence with the phonemes of the language concerned, it would remain true that there are infinite number of possible ways of spelling as one speaks, as observed by Merja Black (2000). Orthographic change may show patterns comparable to those of other kinds of linguistic change, partly because what orthographies represent is languages, and partly because they are affected by the same constraints. It is interesting that the segment of the orthography considered here shows resystematization from fragments of a variety of earlier systems; this is a phenomenon familiar in morphological history, in which area it has been called _bricolage_ (an example is the Germanic preterite, whose forms are assembled from earlier Perfects and Aorists.) At any rate, in a period of linguistic transition, we can observe in orthography processes that resemble those evident in other levels of language, without simply being a product of them. Two particularly pertinent to the _Ayenbite_ are that the situation of a low-status written language in a time of written trilingualism is particularly conducive to orthographic restructuring; and so is heavy borrowing, by giving the written language new graphs and the spoken language new phonemic contrasts.
