## Historical discourse analysis 

## Scientific language and changing thought-styles[*] 

## Irma Taavitsainen 

University of Helsinki 

## 1. Introduction 

My aim in this article is to discuss the development of scientific language and changing thought-styles in the light of theoretical and methodological issues of historical discourse analysis. My focus is on the transition from scholastic to empirical science. The perhaps unsolvable question of the role of language in thinking is not my concern here, though it is generally agreed that language use is a social action and plays an important part in construing reality and social roles, including professional identities (see e.g. Carter 1997:118–119; Gunnarsson et al. 1997:1; Hyland 2000:118). The empirical part of my article focuses on speechactverbsofreporting.Definitionsandargumentationarecentralelements in scientific discourse, but texts also contain metatextual passages to guide the reader. In this article I hope to demonstrate how linguistic analysis that combines the methodologies of discourse and genre analysis with electronic searches in a large database of medical texts can give us insights into changing thoughtstyles. The material covers three hundred years from Late Middle English to the Royal Society period. New linguistic developments take place in the spearhead of science, while a great part of medical writing retains the old style. 

## 2. Historical discourse analysis 

‘Discourse’[1] may be used to refer collectively to the communicative practices of a particular group of people. Medical discourse is used to refer to the communicative practices of the medical profession. It includes written genres such as 

202 Irma Taavitsainen 

research articles to advance knowledge, textbooks with which new members are introduced to the field and manuals for reference, but medical discourse also includes genres that mediate the subject matter to a larger audience and oral communication about medical matters, e.g. doctor-patient interactions. 

In historical discourse analysis, written texts are, of course, in focus. Genres are important operational tools, as they condition both the writing process and the reception of texts.[2] Genres constitute dynamic systems, which undergo change and variation: new genres are created in response to their users’ sociocultural needs, existing genres may be adapted to new uses and genres that have lost their function in a society fall out of use. In a historical perspective, inventories of genres at various points of time give synchronic descriptions of the range and scope of genres. These descriptions can then be compared along the diachronic axis to achieve an overall picture of the evolution of a genre and to discover the mechanisms of change. The aim is to find answers not only to the questions of what changed and how, but to the ultimate question why. The problem is that theoretical claims and assumptions about genres and their changing conventions often remain at the level of abstract statements. What we need in order to trace the developments and achieve the goals of historical discourse analysis is an adequate methodology and easily retrievable data covering a broad range of genres and representing an unbroken line of textual traditions; detailed empirical evidence can illuminate the issues in a new way.[3] 

## 2.1 Medical discourse: Some examples of writing conventions 

The following examples are taken from the extreme ends of the scale of medical writing during the Late Middle and Early Modern periods and are arranged according to chronological order. The first two extracts belong to the most learned level of writing to promote knowledge among professionals. The first extract is from one of the first academic treatises in English, an anonymous text, translated from Latin. The second is from a Royal Society report on microscopic findings of anatomy in the _Philosophical Transactions_ . The microscope provided a new tool for examining the natural world. Experimental reports formed a new genre of scientific writing for learned circles at the academic level, and scientists interacted with written texts. (Emphasis in all examples mine.) 

- (1) a. _Yt is to wyt as seyþ Galien in Metategni_ þat if it _owth to be done_ by apoferisim, be þe flebotom dippyd in oyle (…) Also sum men of custum when þey are flebotomyd swoneþ; sech, _as seþ Constantyne_ , be þey refreshyd (…) If þis ys hit that old men owth to be fleobotomyd 

Historical discourse analysis 203 

after mete (…), yong men forsoþ affore mete (…) for yong men sith þey abovnd with hote humoris owth to be flubotomid in þe ouyr of hote homors. (c. 1400, _Phlebotomie,_ p. 39) 

- b. _I fixt_ upon; _I found_ , notwithstanding all my care to preserve the Vessels, when _I was freeing_ them, as heedfully as _I could_ , from the supposed Parenchyma, that in every breach, _I made_ , either with my fingers or otherwise, _all my endeavours_ were destructive to my purpose; and, upon examination of those bits, much of which is called Parenchyma, _I met_ in them more Vessels, than _I had preserved_ in the parts whence they came: And though the Portion were never so small, yet _my bare eye could make this discovery_ : much more _could I_ , when assisted _by Microscope_ , _perceive_ , _I had destroyed_ more Vessels, than preserved (1666, King, p. 316) 

The second two texts given below are medical recipes directed to a more heterogeneous audience. Middle English recipes, illustrated in the first extract, are often found in collections containing miscellaneous items. Their readership was wide, ranging from medical professionals to literate women. The second text is from a notebook of a medical doctor “Given mee 18th Novemb: 1664 by Mr Sheepeside”. Recipes circulated among the widening readership of Early Modern English. They were an important part of utilitarian and practical books intended for the general public.[4] 

- (2) a. For to hele þe festere. Tak betis and stampe wiþ barly mele. And tempre hit wiþ white of eyren and make a plastre þer of. and ley hit þer to. and Šif þer be eny ded flesch hit wele d[e]strie hit. (c. 1450, _A Middle English Leechbook_ , pp. 43–44) 

   - b. For a Dropsie. Take y[e] juice of Pellitorie of y[e] wall & boyle it up to a syruppe w[th] honey & so keepe it in a Glasse. Take one spoonefull of this every Morninge. (1664, Palmer, p. 193) 

## 3. The history of science 

In the history of science, external events and sociohistorical facts provide anchorage points for language internal developments. The first printing press was established in England by Caxton in 1476. The practices of manuscript transmission continued, but the new medium was to revolutionize information transmission in the future. The influence of the broadening of the world with the discoveries of new continents (America in 1492) began to be felt little by 

204 Irma Taavitsainen 

little as reports on voyages, with observations of strange plants and animals became known to the public. More precise descriptions of the natural world demonstrated the limits of learning inherited from ancient books, and the old doctrines were not considered as authoritative as before. The middle of the sixteenth century was important as two ground-breaking books came out in 1543: Copernicus, _De revolutionibus orbium caelesticum_ , and Vesalius, _De humani corporis fabrica_ ; discoveries paving the way for Harvey’s _De motu cordis_ (1628) were made in the mid-sixteenth century on the continent (Friedman & Friedland 1998:22–23). 

The change in world views during this period can be summarized by pointing out certain contrasts: at the beginning of the sixteenth century all scholars believed that knowledge was contained in authoritative texts. Between 1550 and 1650 educated Europeans ceased to believe that they could find all important truths in ancient books (Grafton et al. 1992:1–5). A sixteenthcentury witness gives direct evidence of the growing disbelief: 

- (3) The nauigations in these latter yeeres made by the Portingales into the east Indians, and by the Spaniards into the west Indians, hath made manifest to vs, how greatly the old authors, I meane Dioscordes, Galen, Plinie, Auicenna, Serapio, and other writers of the former time were deceived (…) For first, all the ancient writers in their monuments haue deliuered to vs (…) But by the nauigations of the Portingals, and of the Spaniards into those countries, in which these pepper trees do growe, it is euident and well knowen, that (…) (1588, Bailey, f. A5) 

By the early seventeenth century the idea of how knowledge was obtained was changing, and in 1660 the history of science reached a new phase in England. The founding of the Royal Society is one of the landmarks, with its explicit formulation of the principles of doing science and shaping knowledge. Their way of thinking and communicating was new and consequently a new style of reporting on experiments was created, as will be shown later in this article. 

## 3.1 English scientific writing 

English scientific language emerged in Old English. The body of writings in this period includes some astrological and computational treatises of the calculation of time, herbals, and medical texts. The most important collections are _Lacnunga_ (ed. by J.H. Grattan & C. Singer 1952), Bald’s _Leechbook_ (ed. by Cockayne 1864–1866), and _Leechdoms_ (ed. by Cockayne 1864–1866). They can 

Historical discourse analysis 205 

be classified as remedy books, as their main contents are recipes, rules of health, charms and other related materials.[5] Detailed investigation has shown that they combine native elements with classical learning drawn from a wide range of Latin sources. The greater part of _Leechdoms_ consists of fairly close translations of Latin originals, and Latin medical learning is also found in Bald’s _Leechbook_ (Kitson 1989:57). A veterinary tract is also extant from the Old English period, but the repertoire of medical writings does not contain texts at the learned or theoretical level. After late Old English, there is a gap before the emergence of the scientific register in English in the last quarter of the fourteenth century. 

The process of vernacularization, i.e. the introduction of a broader range of genres and registers of writing into English, has been the focus of several recent studies (e.g. Wogan-Browne et al. 1999; Diller & Görlach 2001). It took place on a broad front, including administration, the Wycliffite translations of the Bible and other Lollard texts, fiction, with Chaucer’s work establishing the literary canon, and scientific and utilitarian writing. Vernacularization is connected with the rise of nationalistic feelings, and it seems to have involved a conscious effort to broaden the scope of English writings by creating a new prestige register of scientific writing (Taavitsainen 2001a). At the end of the fourteenth century, medical writings at all levels were translated into English or composed in the vernacular, and the activity increased in the following century. Vernacular scientific texts from the Late Medieval period range from the most learned contemporary encyclopaedia _De proprietatibus rerum_ (1387) and academic texts like the _Phlebotomie_ to recipe collections with miscellaneous materials. They were produced in the multilingual context of Late Medieval England (Voigts 1996). According to publication statistics, Latin retained its position as the _lingua franca_ of science in England until the late seventeenth century and continued to be used and written even after that (Webster 1975:267). Latin was the language of the universities and higher learning, and the style of Latin scientific writing provided the model for English texts. Foreign influence has often been underestimated (Blake 1992:22), though Graeco-Latin scientific writing was the model for the macroforms of discourse, logical argumentation and syntactic structures, as well as for stylistic choices of language features. 

## 3.2 Scientific thought-styles and the Corpus of Early English Medical Writing 

Different periods are traditionally connected to different styles of thinking and making decisions, and the question of what continued and what changed is 

206 Irma Taavitsainen 

central in the historical analysis of scientific thought-styles (Crombie 1994:6). Language is the medium through which scientific conceptions, objects of inquiry, methods and evaluations are mediated to us. These underlying commitments are reflected on the linguistic repertoire of texts. Pilot studies have proved that scholasticism and empiricism show different language features and that the contrast between these two thought-styles can be detected by linguistic analysis. Our project at the University of Helsinki “Scientific Thoughtstyles: The Evolution of Early English Medical Writing”[6] aims precisely at tracing these developments. The aim is to describe the various thought-styles, verify the changes, relate these differences to the sociohistorical background and ultimately find explanations for whatever changes have taken place. 

For our research purposes, we are compiling an electronic database of texts, the Corpus of Early English Medical Writing 1375–1750 (for the plan, see Taavitsainen & Pahta 1997). The first part covers the period from the appearance of vernacular medical writing in the late Middle Ages to the middle of the sixteenth century (1375–1550), and it is tentatively divided into two subperiods by the introduction of printing into England (1375–1475 and 1476–1550). The text selection of the first part is almost finished, but the Early Modern part still needs more work to find a balance between various traditions and genres of writing. In this period (1551–1750), the foundation of the Royal Society in 1660 marks the dividing line between subperiods. The present size of the corpus is over 1.5 million words. Shorter texts are included _in toto_ , and longer treatises are represented by extracts of c. 10,000 words. All kinds of extant materials are covered: surgical and anatomical texts, treatises dealing with special diseases like epilepsy, fields like ophthalmology and gynaecology, different types of writing like encyclopaedias and compendia, remedy books and even medical verse. In the linguistic analysis of thought-styles, the frame is provided by traditions and genres, but the starting point is individual texts; the aim is to find out their characteristics and relate them to the changing conventions. By detailed linguistic assessment, it is possible to gain insights into the changing conventions of scientific writing: what happened, how and why. Factors that complicate the issues are the changing resources of language for expressing the same things and changing communicative needs. We do not consider that any feature alone is sufficient for an overall description, but a matrix of features with changing co-occurrence patterns may reveal the dynamics along a time scale. 

Historical discourse analysis 207 

## 3.3 Evidentiality and scientific thought-styles 

One of the basic questions in the assessment of scientific writing concerns evidentiality, i.e. how the source of knowledge is indicated and how certain this knowledge is. Different types of evidence include hearsay “that someone said so”, sensory evidence by observation, or knowledge deduced from the existing evidence (Palmer 1990:12). Evidential features provide a tool for distinguishing between different underlying thought-styles as scholasticism relies on language as the source of knowledge, and empiricism is based on observation.[7] Conventions for encoding scientific concepts and processes vary. To judge from my earlier studies, thought-styles are connected with different kinds of modality. Scholastic writings favour prescriptive impersonal constructions and the prevailing modality is deontic, whereas experimental essays of the Royal Society are written with subjective epistemic judgements.[8] Scholastic science has been described as logocentric with its reliance on axioms, i.e. statements accepted as being true, though not necessarily so. The source of knowledge was “that someone said so”. This mode of knowing is called the Quotative (Palmer 1986:51). Thus reporting the opinions of various authorities is an evidential feature of scholastic texts and no other evidentiary justification was considered necessary. In contrast to scholasticism, empirical science relies on sensory evidence. In language, this difference can be verified at the lexical level: instead of factual public verbs like say and tell, private verbs of processes which can be only subjectively verified become important. Such verbs can be further divided into semantic classes of intellectual states such as belief and intellectual acts such as discovery (know, think, conclude, etc.; see Quirk et al. 1985:202–203, 1180–1181). Induction is the mode of knowing in empiricism, and there is an overall shift to a more subjective way of writing with first person pronouns and past tense verbs (Taavitsainen 1994). 

## 4. Empirical study: Speech act verbs of reporting 

The empirical part of this article focuses on the quotative mode of knowing (see Section 3.3 above) and on speech act verbs of saying, and traces the developments in various traditions and genres with a focus on the transition period from scholasticism to empiricism. In the quotative mode of knowing, the speech act verbs (SAVs) of saying, telling and asserting, with the authority as the subject, provide the pattern ‘x says/claims that y’. This is the common pattern 

208 Irma Taavitsainen 

in Late Medieval academic tracts, and my hypothesis is that SAVs of reporting provide a useful way of finding passages connected with the logocentric mode of scholastic science. Such passages are likely to be found in the more learned texts. The assessment may also show how such passages evolved in the course of time. To test this hypothesis, the word lists generated by the WordCruncher program of the Corpus of Early English Medical Writing were gleaned, and verbs that could be used in evidentiary function were checked. The present assessment is based on a pilot version of our corpus and I used electronic searches to find illustrative examples for a qualitative study only; statistical assessments remain to be carried out later. 

Texts of the early vernacularization period 1375–1475 had a fairly comprehensive array of verbs (with various spelling forms) in evidential use, but some of them occur only once. The selection becomes more varied in the later periods.[9] In addition to public verbs of saying, some private verbs are found in the same evidentiary function, ascribing opinions to authorities. The distinction between the semantic groups of verbs is not always clear. In assessing these verbs, a further refinement of the analytical toolkit proved necessary. Scientific texts have two levels of discourse: the primary level is that of scientific argumentation; the secondary level is found in metatext, i.e. in comments about the unfolding discourse. The relevant questions are in which function the speech act verb is used and who performs the speech act; is it the author of the text in the metadiscursive parts, or someone else, such as an ancient authority whose opinions are quoted?[10] Both levels are important for the formation of writing conventions and relate to the social practices of doing science. 

Definitions, argumentation and explanation are important elements of scientific writing, and are likely to vary according to different thought-styles. There seems to be a distinct pattern. The verbs call, clepe and name occur in definitions, whereas the other SAVs such as affirm, agree and argue (see note 9 for a complete list) are found in argumentation, either in the body of the text or in metadiscursive passages. In scholastic texts the quotation also provides the explanation: it is true as the authorities say so (see Section 3.3 above). 

## 4.1 Definitions 

Defining scientific terms is one of the prerequisites for accurate dissemination of knowledge. In modern professional languages, definitions have been described as relational identification processes that construe systems of taxonomies of logical relationships (Wignell 1998:299). Two types prevail in 

Historical discourse analysis 209 

modern textbooks: interlocking definitions that consist of two or more mutually defined terms and naming processes that set up technical terms. The latter are further divided into projecting and nonprojecting naming processes according to the voice. The projecting type employs personal phrases like “we call”, whereas nonprojecting naming processes use the passive voice “is called” (Wignell et al. 1993:149). In the present material, the speech act verbs call and clepe mark the early type of definition as in early texts there seems to be a concern with naming. Definitions are frequent. In the following passage, naming is marked in italics. Relational identification is based on an enumerative text strategy, marked in bold. The nonprojecting type is frequent, for example, in Trevisa’s translation of the learned encyclopaedia which sets up a technical vocabulary and presents classifications of things and concepts: 

- (4) Mannes body is made of foure elementis: of erþe, watir, fire, and aier; and eueriche þereof haþ propre qualitees. Foure _þer beþ iclepid þe firste and principal qualite_ [ _s_ ], þat is to wite hete, coolde, drye, and wetenesse; and _ben iclepid þe firste qualites for_ þey sliden first of þe elementis into þe þinges þat ben imaade of elementis. _þey ben also iclepid þe principal qualitees for_ of hem comeþ al þe secundarye effectis. Tweyne of þese qualites _ben iclepid actiue_ ‘able to worche’, hete and cooldnes. þe oþir tweyne, drynesse and wetnesse, _ben iclepid passiue_ ‘[able] to soffre’. And so as þese qualites haue maistrie, _þe elementis ben iclepid_ [ _actiue oþir passiue_ ] ‘able to do or soffre’. þe firste tweyne ben principallich _iclepid actiue_ , noŠt for þey worchin alone, for þe passiue qualites worchiþ also [for] non qualite is in þe body an ydel; but _þerfore þey beþ iclepid actiue_ for be þe worchinge of hem þe oþir beþ ibrouŠt inne and ikept and isaued. (1387, Trevisa, pp. 129–130) 

Another common type of definition gives the names of objects or concepts as equivalents in several languages. 

- (5) The fyrste pannycle apperyth yn the ey as the seyd of a corne _clepyd in Laten_ milys and _yn Ynglych_ myleseed (…) _in sum place_ they _arn clepyd_ guttatici, and _yn oþer places_ pedacelle, and _in Naples_ þei _ar callyd_ creature. (c. 1450, Grassus, p. 62) 

- (6) And _men of Tuskan calle_ it humerus benedictum; _them of Rome call_ it nexionam, and _men of Cicile_ and _grekys cal_ it papulam; but the _citramowntayns and Frenshemen and oþer cal_ it maledictam — and no wonder, for yt growyth with grette soroues and grete peyn. (c. 1450, Grassus, p. 76) 

210 Irma Taavitsainen 

Recipes have a different pattern with frequent past participle forms. They identify the ingredients and the presumption is that the plants or objects are familiar to the users of these texts, e.g. “Take a grass called sentory”. Another frequently found formula is an impersonal phrase “men call”. 

The pattern seems to be somewhat different in later texts, as call begins to have other functions and meanings in the Early Modern period. In the specialized texts of the period, definitions and relational identification rely mostly on the verb be. The following definition represents the modern type (in italics); yet the old authorities are named to guarantee its truthfulness, according to the old pattern (in bold); no other explanation was needed: 

- (7) Then as I sayde before, _Elementu~_ in this signification _is a body moost pure and simple_ , and _the leest parte of the same, wherin it is_ : whiche can not be deuyded into any other kinde, and of it all thynges naturall haue theyr originall begynnynge. This definition is taken both out of Gallen and Aristotle: therfore I truste, no man wyll be so impudent to contrary or impugne it. (1550?, Langton, f. 12) 

## 4.2 Argumentation 

Western science was initiated by the ancient Greek philosophers, mathematicians and medical men, in their search for principles of nature, including human nature, and of argument itself (Crombie 1995:225). Latin scientific writing was modelled accordingly, and argumentation patterns can be seen in the structure of discourse forms. There was a set pattern of logical argumentation in Aristotelian questions, and in scholastic commentaries quotations from authorities served to provide the premises for conclusions (cf. Section 3.3 above). The vernacularization of scientific writings in the Late Medieval period involved transferring the classical models into English. It seems, however, that the macroforms of discourse such as question-answer formulae, the dialogue form and scholastic commentaries occur in the vernacular in a somewhat modified form, with a lapse of time in their full realization (see Taavitsainen 1999, forthcoming). 

## 4.2.1 _Scholastic commentaries_ 

One of the most distinctive characteristics of scholasticism has been said to be its unfailing persistence in examining its own progress; texts built on earlier texts and quotations were added to in the course of time with each subsequent authority. The literary form associated with this method is the commentary. In 

Historical discourse analysis 211 

a typical case, e.g. in biblical and philosophical writings, a passage of the base text is given with explanations and opinions of various authorities, often in an attempt to reconcile different, even contradictory views. Scientific texts were built in the same way. Masters of medicine based their teachings on the reading of authoritative texts divided into points, often with opposing opinions, which could then be subjected to discussion. Authoritative texts of medicine formed a canon of their own, assembled by Salernitan masters and used until the Renaissance (Lawn 1993:18–38). The core of writings consisted of eleventhcentury translations from Greek and Arabic, of texts by Hippocrates, Galen and other authorities. This text format helped to preserve and reinforce the intellectual standing of medicine as a science (cf. above). 

Vernacular writings follow the continental patterns. In the earliest layer of learned, academic texts, quotations of authoritative opinions are realized with SAVs of power and certainty, such as command, though the more neutral say is by far the most common verb: 

- (8) It is to weten þat _auturs tretyn of causon commaundeþ not_ mynuschynge to be don, _as it schewiþ in Viatica & in Liber febris Constantyny_ , & þat be suche reson. (…) _Alexandire forsoþe commandiþ_ a pacient leucoflamcie to be fleubotomyed; eu[e]l humourus forsoþe if þey be in veynes ar competenly brouŠt out be flebotomye. (c. 1400, _Phlebotomie_ , pp. 50–51) 

- (9) And _as þe comentator super librum vegitabilium seiþ_ , þat is for (…) _So seiþ idem comentator_ . (…) among þe Moores, in coolde lond white men, as among þe Sclaues. So _seiþ Aristoteles in libro de celo et mundo and telliþ_ þe resoun why; and _seiþ_ þat in coolde londes þe modres of wommen ben disposid to conseiue suche children. (1387, Trevisa, pp. 135–136) 

- (10) _So seiþ Galien in amphorismys Ypocratis particula tercia, super locum illum_ : Inmutaciones temporum maxime generant morbos, et cetera, þat is ‘chaunginge of [tymes] most brediþ eueles’. _Here Galien seiþ þat Ypocras meneþ_ þat tymes of þe Šere brediþ nouŠt sikenes but chaungeinge of complexioun of tyme; (1387, Trevisa, p. 145) 

- (11) _Porphirius sum considerith_ nat the soule but considerith the lifly spirite racional. And therfor _Porphirius hath compowned and made Ysagogas as bi theym shal be shewed_ that corporat spirite to be to thuse of the soule, nat the same soule. But _Socrates, Plato, Aristotil, Theodorus, Platoniciens, Andronices, Perypaticis, Pophilijs, and many mo other of oon wil affermen_ that neither bodi is, neither bi lawe of place holdith, neither hymsilf seeth, for whi whan al the body with his vertu illumyneth to eueriche progresse 

212 Irma Taavitsainen 

withoutfurth, nat as in his substaunce suffrith any scisscioun, (c. 1450, _De Spermate_ , p. 217) 

The same pattern of quotations from authorities as the main argumentation line is found in surgical treatises. Passages from Chauliac and Arderne illustrate the technique: 

- (12) _GAliene_ þat was lanterne off ffisiciens & of Surgenes _seiþ in þe seuentenþe boke de vtilitate particulari, in þe laste chapiter saue one_ . þat þer ben foure commodites (…) & þerfore _galien seiþ in þe begynnynge of his boke de interioribus_ þat boþe olde ffisiciens & Šunge (…) ffor _as Rasis seiþ in toto continenti, at þe begynnynge,_ þat a ffisicien owe for to be wyse & discrete (c. 1400, Chauliac, p. 3) 

- (13) And _henricus de amonda-villa, in þe firste boke of his surgerie, deduceþ bi resoun_ þis skille and _seiþe_ þat euerye craftye man is holden to knowen þe þinge þat he worchiþ inne, & elles he schal erren ofte-times in his worchinge. (c. 1400, Chauliac, p. 3) 

- (14) _Auicen, forsoþ, seiþ_ , ‘experience ouercomeþ reson’; and _galien in pantegni seiþ_ , ‘No man ow for to trust in reson al-oon but Šit it be proued of experience.’ And _he seith in anoþer place_ , ‘Experience without reson is feble, & so is reson withoute experience fest vnto hym.’ Neþerlesse _I afferme_ noŠt that I miŠt hele al ffistulae in ano. Ffor som ben vncurable as it shal be seid [{more fully{] within when I shal trete of tham. (c. 1400, Arderne, pp. 3–4) 

This style of writing continues in the Early Modern period. In Vicary’s text from 1548 it is to be expected; his anatomy is based on a medieval thirteenth-century treatise (Bennett 1969:108) and is in accordance with the medieval tradition of texts building on earlier ones. 

- (15) But noble _Ipocras sayth_ , that Surgerie is hande working in mans body (…) Nowe the seconde manner of knowing what thing Chirurgerie is, it is _the saying of Auicen_ to be knowen by his beeing, for it is verely a medecinal science: and _as Galen sayth_ , he that wyl knowe the certentie of a thing (…) (1548, Vicary, p. 13) 

In John Banister’s text from 1578 the old pattern is clearly recognizable, but the quoted authorities are nearly contemporary. Andreas Vesalius and Realdo Columbo, the forerunner of anatomy in the sixteenth century, are quoted alongside the old authorities. This proves that the dissemination of new knowledge had started, though the mode of conveying it was old: 

Historical discourse analysis 213 

- (16) Wherfore of the Bones of mans body (…), the good phisition ( _sayth Galene_ ) ought not to be ignoraunt. (…) These therefore are _the wordes of Vesalius_ . (…) Wherfore _it is a shame if we otherwise affirme_ : but _with Gal. Vesal. and Col. conclude_ , that Bones of their own proper Nature are altogether destitute of sence, except (as I sayd before) the teeth onely which are approued sensible, _as experience our Scholemaistres teacheth vs_ . (…) Some agayne are small, but much hollow, as the bones of the fingers _co~trary to Galene_ , as we will proue in their propper place. _Andraeas Vesalius also supposed_ that neither the bones of the nose, and Sesaminae, neither the little Ossicles that constitute the organ of hearyng, should be otherwise then made of massiue Soliditie. Which (notwithstanding) _Collumbus doubteth not to denye_ : for proofe wherof (sayth he) breake one of them (…) (1578, Banister, f. 1) 

A text from 1603 about a very topical disease, the plague, uses the old argumentation pattern: 

- (17) _Behold here the worthy and true opinion of this excellent Philosopher, which before him_ (…) _Aristotle in the ninth of his Phisiques, Chapter 10. had testified. The wise Philosopher Mercury Trimisgistus in his Dialogue intituled Asclepius, saith_ ; that all that which descendeth from heauen is generatiue; if then in respect of vs the influence of heauen be generatiue (as in truth it is): _for as Aristotle saith_ , Sol & homo generant homine~) it cannot any waies be possible that it can corrupt or cause the confusion of mankinde. The _like also is confirmed by Proclus, (who interpreteth vpon Platoes booke de Anima & Demone,)_ The Celestiall bodies (saith he) by a soueraigne harmony containe all thinges (…) If then it appeareth that the Celectiall bodies perfect all things, and both confirme & conserue them, (as in truth they doo, and _this Author witnesseth_ ) how can these engender contagion and infection in vs, which abolishe our perfection and integritie, and destroy vs by rauishing our liues? _To speake truth, as me seemeth_ it were a thing impossible. For it is contrary to the nature of contagion, that it should descend from heauen, because contagion is no other thing but an infection proceeding from one vnto an other by communication of a pestilent and infected vapour, and by this meanes if the Plague and contagion proceeded from the starres, it should necessarily follow by the definition of contagion, that the starres were primarily or formerly infected, if by their influence they should send a pernicious contagion among vs. But this in no sort may be graunted, because the starres by being Celestiall bodies, pure, diuine, and estranged from all corruption, receiuing 

214 Irma Taavitsainen 

and containing no infection in them, being no materiall bodies apt to transmutation or chaunge, ( _as Aristotle and Auerrois in his Booke de Caelo et mundo, doo learnedly alledge_ ) cannot be capable of infection or contagion, neyther communicate it to the inferior bodies. _Let vs therefore cast off this vaine and sottish opinion_ whereby we are induced to beleeue that the Plague proceedeth from the heauens: that is to say, from the influence of the starres, (as by the vanitie of time we haue had inducements.) _But let vs confesse_ that it proceedeth from the secret iudgements of God, who intendeth by this scourge to whip vs for our sinnes, _as it appeareth in Leuiticus, and Deuteronomy._ To conclude, _we say_ that the cause of the Plague is a malignant alteration and corruption of the ayre infecting our bodies, as it hath bene declared in the beginning of this Chapter. (1603, Lodge, ff. C1–C2) 

The examples show that the old style of argumentation with an authoritative tone and reliance on the old doctrines continued well beyond the medieval period in both academic texts and surgical treatises. The influence of the period style is, however, evident in scientific argumentation. The above passage shows features typical of Renaissance rhetoric, such as inclusive first-person plural forms, parallel structures, rhetorical questions and other audience-involving devices. 

> 4.2.2 _Argumentation in the first-person singular_ A new style of first-person narration is prominent in experimental essays (see example (1b) from 1666), though sometimes third-person narration is used. In the Royal Society texts, verbs of reporting are found when describing the setting and the activities of the discourse community (for a discussion of this term see Atkinson 1999:4–6): who was present, what he said, etc. The experimental report itself does not contain SAVs of reporting. Instead, verbs of observation and cognitive processes become prominent. The text strategy is iconic: observations are carefully recorded in chronological order and each step in the process of doing science is explicated. This way of writing is in accordance with the principle of reproducibility, the essence of the empirical method. Facts are given so accurately that it is possible to reproduce the experiment. The text strategy also reflects the ideological tenet of ‘the matter of fact’, one of the key ideas of the new science: even if scientists could not agree on the causes of nature, consensus could be reached on the processes that could be witnessed by anyone (Dear 1991:161). 

Historical discourse analysis 215 

First-person narration is already found in some medical texts of the Late Medieval period. For example, Benvenutus Grassus argues in the first person in direct quotations in his learned ophthalmology: 

- (18) Thys maner of infirmyte the _grete lechys of Salerne clepyd_ obtalmyam, but _I, quod Benuonucius, calle_ yt torturam tenebrosam, ffor so much as it commyth with so greet a torment þat it makyth the eye dymme and derke. And the forseyd medycyne is callyd puluerem benedictum, the blessed powder; ffor with þat powder (…) _I haue holpyn_ many (…) (c. 1450, Grassus, p. 61) 

John Arderne writes in the first-person singular, in an author-centred style, but the tone of these texts is different from the later reports. The scholastic mode of knowing with absolute certainty is dominant: 

- (19) _I helid hym perfitely_ within half a Šere; and aftirward, hole and sounde, he ledde a glad lif by 30 Šere and more, ffor whiche cure _I gatte myche honour and louyng_ þruŠ al ynglond. (…) that tho thingis whiche in wrokyng _trewly I am_ ofte tymes _experte_ (…) (c. 1400, Arderne, pp. 1,3) 

Clowes’s _Struma_ from 1602 is an interesting text from the stylistic point of view. It contains mixed passages. Some are obviously written in the old mode of knowing, with firm reliance on authorities and with SAVs of power: 

- (20) (…) _Guido_ taketh the forenamed powders (…) _Galen approoueth & commendeth_ the use of Aurea (…) _Mercurialis saith_ moreover (…) (1602, Clowes, p.12) 

Some other passages are in accordance with later reporting styles. Mostly the new style occurs in the preface, but more personal passages are found in the body of the text as well. In the following, the new alternates with the old style in an unusual mixture. Hedges are used to express uncertainty, but at the same time phrases like “according to my poor ability” repeat the old medieval modesty formulae, which are frequent stock phrases in medieval literature: 

- (21) _I haue by experience_ (…) _carefully obserued, and faithfully also collected_ out of the famous writinges of sundry worthy Authors (…) Therefore we ought not to thinke any study, paines, or trauaile too great (…) And _heere I must needs say, (according to my poore ability) I haue my self been very industrious for the common benefit_ , and good of others, truly to set downe diuers approoued remedies of mine own collection: _wherin (to my knowledge) I haue not_ in al this whole discourse, published. (1602, Clowes, pp. 17–18) 

216 Irma Taavitsainen 

Likewise, the _Surgeon’s Mate_ from 1617 has passages in the commentary tradition, but without explicit names of authorities. In accordance with the new style, personal remarks are prominent and the author’s own role is made explicit. The author is clearly in charge of the reasoning process: 

- (22) _Some charge_ (…), but _I am not of their opinion_ : _Some say_ inordinate watchings are cause thereof: _Some say_ extreame labour wanting due nourishment: _Some also affirme_ cares and griefe to be some cause thereof, _others affirme_ the very heate of the aire, resoluing the spirits and vapors, and (…); but what shall _I amplifie further_ , for _it is also true_ that (1617, Woodall, p. 180) 

## 4.3 Metatext 

The secondary level of scientific discourse can be found in the communication between the author and the audience about the evolving text. Such metadiscursive comments may be author-centred and deal with the intentions, motivations and goals of the author, or reader-centred, guiding the reception of the text and making the contents more easily understood. In all cases the author intrudes in some way to direct the reader’s reception, adjusting the text according to the addressee or the target group. Sometimes audience response is anticipated in face-saving speech acts. 

Metadiscursive comments have two main functions: textual, e.g. to signal new sections, and interpersonal, making participant roles explicit. In general, metadiscursive passages promote successful communication (Swales 1990:188; Gläser 1995; Hyland 2000:129; Taavitsainen 2000). There are two alternative ways of expressing these comments in early English medical writing. They may be personal with first and second person pronouns, or impersonal with the passive voice (cf. projecting and nonprojecting definitions above). Metatext is frequent in learned discourse, in academic texts, specialized treatises and surgeries, while the anonymous texts of the remedy book tradition do not employ this device. Metadiscursive comments of the impersonal kind prevail in the earliest period, but they are not entirely restricted to it, e.g. sixteenthcentury general guides of health by highly learned doctors contain such passages. On the whole, though, the style of writing becomes more involved and more subjective in the course of time. SAVs of reporting are frequent in metatext of both impersonal and personal types: 

Historical discourse analysis 217 

- (23) _It is to sei_ of acutes, _i.e._ , agues. Laboryng of watsomeuer day acute, mynusche hym to þe 4th day, not ouer þat, for þanne is (c. 1400, _Phlebotomie_ , p. 49) 

- (24) Forsoth, _it is to be saide_ in what maner the visible qualite cometh to the eyen, and wherof (c. 1450, _De humana natura_ , p. 58) 

- (25) The complexions compounde, maye be decerned by the sayde symple qualyties. And here _it is to be noted_ , that the heate of the harte maye vanquyshe the colde in the lyuer. (1541, Elyot, p. 6) 

- (26) To trete of þe propertees of mannes body and of þe parties þerof, _we schul first biginne to trete_ of þe qualitees of þe elementis and of þe humoures of whiche þe body is maad. (1387, Trevisa, p. 129) 

- (27) These matters here nede not to be rehersed; _wherfore I do returne to my purpose, and do say that_ the moderacyon of slepe shulde be mesured (1542, Boorde, p. 245) 

Addressee-oriented passages are also found, and sometimes an explicit dialogue between the text participants ensues. The examples from guides to health by learned doctors intended for the general public reveal an extremely condescending and even rude attitude to the reader: 

- (28) And forsomuche as _I haue not yet forgotten to whome I write, in this declaration I will leaue a part al learned & subtil reaso~s, as here void & vnmiete, & only vse suche as be most euident to whom I write, & easiest to be vnderstanden of the same: and at ones therwith shew also_ why it hau~teth vs English men more the~ other nations. _Therfore I passe ouer_ the vnge~tle sauoure or smell of the sweate, grosenes, colour, and other qualities of the same (…) (1552, Caius, p. 16) 

- (29) First _to the Vulgar_ : Kind souls _I am sorry_ it hath been _your hard mishap_ to have been so long trained in such _Egyptian darkness_ , even darkness which _to your sorrows_ may be felt; _the vulgar road to Physick is not my practice_ , and _I am therefore the more unfit to give you advice_ ; I have now published a little Book (Galen’s _Art of Physick_ ) which will fully instruct you (…) _You must not think_ (curtaeous People) _that I can spend time to give you examples_ of all Diseases, these are _enough to let you see so much light as you_ without Art _are able to receive, If I should set you to look upon the Sun I should dazzle your eyes and make you blind_ . (1652, Culpeper, p. 242) 

A different stance can be found in some other texts that also unfold as imaginary dialogues with the readers in a new, more involved style of writing. 

218 Irma Taavitsainen 

Examples of writing to peers in this new style can be found in learned texts of the mid-seventeenth century, including Royal Society writings. The passage below is from Robert Boyle’s famous and influential text advocating the new way of thinking: 

- (30) And now Sir, _having thus presented you with such thoughts of mine_ upon this Subject, as its Nature did read[i]ly suggest, _I shall take the liberty_ succinctly _to discuss_ their evasions, that are of a contrary sense. And in the first place, _I find some Physitians objecting_ , that having (…) _In this case I must ingeniously confess_ that (…) _In the next place it is objected_ by divers, that their receipts are of mere curiosity, (…) _In which case, though I will not_ precisely (…) (Boyle 1655, pp. 136–140). 

The above examples show that metatext is clearly distinct from scientific argumentation itself, but even metatext can be used for argumentation, as in the last example. The general trend from impersonal to a more subjective and author-centred style of writing can be detected in metacomments. The examples also show how important such passages are for the participant roles in the text, and how the author’s attitude towards the readership is made explicit in them. 

## 5. Changes in thought-styles and changes in the styles of writing 

As the aim of this paper is to chart medical discourse in a diachronic perspective, the points of interest are whether the stylistic repertoires in various genres and layers of writing are different, how they change and how much variation there is. Genres and traditions are important in this connection, and the SAVs of reporting proved helpful in showing the spread of scholastic argumentation patterns. The scholastic style reflecting the logocentric mode of knowing is explicit in the top layers of writing for learned audiences in the Late Medieval period. Surgery was the spearhead field with advances reported in the treatises by Lanfranc, Mondeville, Arderne and Chauliac (McVaugh 1998:284–290). Their texts represent the latest scientific developments of their time, and especially Chauliac’s texts for an academic audience abound in scholastic features. In addition, other academic treatises, like the _Phlebotomie_ , show high frequencies. Radical changes took place at the top level of scientific writing with the introduction of a new way of reporting on experiments by Royal Society members. New tools like the microscope inspired scientists to new discoveries, 

Historical discourse analysis 219 

and the style becomes more subjective, involved and author-centred as the emphasis shifts from ancient authorities to observation as the source of knowledge. A new genre, the experimental essay, was created to meet the needs of the discourse community. In contrast, the old commentary style was retained in surgical treatises and textbooks mediating established learning. The majority of scientific writings do not seem to change in any radical way in the Early Modern period. The old attitude to knowledge with certainty is reflected in these texts. The remedy book tradition with recipes and prognostications in almanac literature and anonymous collections (see Capp 1979) had acquired set conventions early, as the tradition stretched back for centuries even in the early periods. The anonymous remedy book tradition does not seem to undergo change. Early Modern health guides and handbooks, however, acquired a new category as learned doctors began to write for a large and heterogeneous audience in the sixteenth century. The style of writing is in accordance with their own level of education, and the influence of the Renaissance ‘period style’, with devices like parallel structures and rhetorical questions, can be detected in these texts. The stance towards the audience is striking: the doctors are writing as superiors to the unlearned, though in fact the audience was heterogeneous and included their peers as well. 

## 6. Conclusions 

The questions of how and why the new style of writing evolved are essential and remain to be answered. More detailed assessment with some statistical evidence is needed for definite conclusions, but this study suggests some pertinent lines of further research. The general trend from impersonal to a more subjective and author-centred way of writing is intriguing. An analytical approach to the constituent parts of discourse is helpful here, and the developments of metadiscourse, especially in introductions and prefaces, are a potential influence. The involved style of writing seems to emerge first in metadiscursive passages in which the author gives explicit guidance to the reader. Explanations for the changes of style are likely to be found in the sociohistorical developments of the times including education, increasing literacy and changes in book production circumstances, to name a few most important factors. Styles of thinking and making decisions within any society or culture endure only as long as the underlying commitments and dispositions remain stable (Crombie 1995:227). The widening of the world to the new continents and the new technical 

220 Irma Taavitsainen 

instruments that gave new knowledge of the natural environment changed the world-view. Old questions and answers were not satisfactory at the top level any more, and new questions were being posed. A new era had begun. 

## Notes 

* This article was written under the auspices of the Academy of Finland. 

1. The term is also used to denote self-contained, coherent units of language longer than a sentence, but most often it is defined as language in context and language in use, seen as a socially situated dynamic process which both constructs and is constructed by social practices (Carter 1997:111–122). Interaction is an essential component. One of the most recent definitions gives ‘text’ as “the visible evidence of a reasonably self-contained purposeful interaction”, and this interaction is referred to as ‘discourse’ (Hoey 2001:11). 

2. The terminology is complicated as different researchers use the terms in different ways. I use the term ‘genre’ of functional groupings of texts, and text-external features are important. ‘Text types’ rely on text-internal linguistic features as the guiding principle. ‘Register’ is a cover term, e.g. scientific writing includes several genres. Traditions account for the transmission and describe the intertextual quality of writings. Used in this way the terms complement one another. I have dealt with the issues in both literary and non-literary genres and text types in my earlier studies (Taavitsainen 1997, 1998, 2001c, forthcoming). For other definitions and approaches to historical studies, see the special issue of the _European Journal of English Studies_ , ed. by Lilo Moessner (2001). 

3. A new way of looking at genre and register variation was initiated by the multi-dimensional analysis of linguistic variation across speech and writing developed by Biber in 1988 and subsequent studies, e.g. Biber & Finegan (1994). The methodology has been applied to medical writing in the _Edinburgh Medical Journal_ 1735–1985 (Atkinson 1992), and combined with rhetorical analysis to chart the evolution of scientific writing in _The Philosophical Transactions_ 1675–1975 (Atkinson 1999). The earliest phases of scientific writing have received less attention (see Section 3.2 below). Biber identifies five basic dimensions of variation in English, or ‘factors’. Factor 1, ‘Involved versus Informational Production’, is marked by linguistic features like private verbs ( _think, feel_ ) and second and first person pronouns. These features are important in the present assessment, as will become apparent later. 

4. For comparison I quote a modern text at the same academic level as extracts (1a) and (1b): 

   - We examined the records of 1680 patients and found 80 definite and 13 probable cases of ischaemic heart disease. The G3 Read code had a sensitivity of 47% and a positive predictive value of 83% (table). Nitrate prescriptions had, by chance, the same results. A search based on G3 or nitrate prescriptions had a sensitivity of 73% and a positive predictive value of 79%. (Gray et al. 2000) 

The extract is from a research article in _The British Medical Journal_ , and illustrates the statistical approach of modern medicine. The textual pattern of recipe writing continues in 

Historical discourse analysis 221 

modern cookery recipes. Medical industry has taken over the preparation of medicines, and thus medical recipes are not in circulation as before. 

5. _Lacnunga_ is included in _Anglo-Saxon Magic and Medicine_ , ed. by Grattan and Singer (1952), _Leechdoms_ in _Leechdoms, Wortcunning and Starcraft in Early England_ , ed. by Cockayne (1864–1866), and Bald’s _Leechbook_ in _Leechdoms II_ , ed. by Cockayne (1864–1866). 

6. The project was started by Irma Taavitsainen and Päivi Pahta a few years ago, and the team has been joined by Martti Mäkinen and Carla Suhr. The project has received funding from the Academy of Finland 1999–2001, and is an independent part of the Research Centre for Variation and Change at the English Department of the University of Helsinki. So far we have focused on scholastic features, studied references to authorities and prescriptive phrases and outlined the evolution of noun phrase structures (Taavitsainen & Pahta 1997, 1998; Pahta & Taavitsainen forthcoming). In addition, some pilot studies of developments in a longer diachronic perspective have also been carried out. See http://www.eng.helsinki.fi/ scientific_thoughtstyles.htm for the aims and further publications. 

7. The term ‘evidentiality’ is also used to cover a broader area, to include any linguistic expression of attitude towards the mediated knowledge (Chafe 1986:271). In this meaning it overlaps with ‘stance’, but the main difference is that ‘evidentiality’ focuses on knowledge and ‘stance’ on the author’s attitude (Biber et al. 1999:975). Four major components of evidential features have been distinguished: the reliability of knowledge, the mode of knowing, the source of knowledge and the matching of knowledge against verbal resources or expectations (Chafe 1986:261–312; for an adaptation to scientific thought-styles, see Taavitsainen & Pahta 1998:162). 

8. Two kinds of modality are usually distinguished: epistemic and deontic. Epistemic modality is concerned with language as a conveyor of information as well as with various attitudes towards knowledge. Epistemic expressions indicate the speaker’s attitude and degree of commitment towards the proposition in a scale from confidence to doubt (Coates 1983:18), and convictions about particular states of affairs, events or actions. Deontic modality is connected with obligation and necessity. In modern medical writing research articles are connected with epistemic and handbooks with deontic modality (Vihla 1999). Evidentiality is related to epistemic modality in literature: in a narrow sense it indicates the source of knowledge or gives the evidence for what is stated (Palmer 1990:12), but the term is also used to cover a broader area, as noted in footnote 7. For modality in early scientific texts, see Taavitsainen (2001b). 

9. The following verbs of reporting are found in the present version of the Corpus of Early English Medical Writing: accord, account, acknowledge, add, advice, affirm, agree, amplify, answer, argue, ascribe, ask, assert, assure, bid, call, charge, clepe, consider, command, commend, conclude, confirm, conjecture, consider, contradict, counsel, declare, defend, describe, devise, discourse, enforce, express, extol, forbid, glorie, graunt, inculcate, inform, intimate, maintain, make mention, mention, note, praise, prescribe, proclaim, promit, propose, prove, put, question, quod, recite, register, rehearse, report, reprehend, reprove, say, speak, swear, teach, tell, terme, treat and utter. The private verbs used in the same function are deem, deduce, comprehend and understand. 

222 Irma Taavitsainen 

10. Authorities are connected with verbs of reporting. There are clear differences in the layers of writing according to the level of learning: surgical treatises and specialized texts contain names of authorities often with specific references to the source. Galen has the leading role, then come Hippocrates, Avicenna, Rhazes, Haly Abbas and Averroes, philosophers and contemporary or near-contemporary practitioners. Remedy books have more general comments or no references at all (Taavitsainen & Pahta 1998:168–175). The pattern becomes even more interesting when the Early Modern materials are related to the preceding period. The spearhead of science, the experimental article, mentions the names of fellow scientists only.
