## _Key_ issues in English etymology[*] 

## Theo Vennemann 

University of Munich 

## 1. Introduction 

Many English etymologies end with statements such as ‘the root is not found outside Germanic’, ‘ulterior etymology unknown’, or ‘of obscure origin’. 

Two major sources for new or better English etymologies will be discussed and exemplified in this paper.[1] They open themselves through the insight that language contact of English and its ancestral languages did not begin in historical times, e.g. with Latin influence on the Germanic languages, but in prehistoric times, in part affecting many or all Indo-European languages, in part restricted to the West Indo-European languages, or to the Germanic or West Germanic languages, or even to Anglo-Frisian or English; viz. that the giving languages were (a) Vasconic languages, i.e. prehistoric languages related to Basque, and (b) Semitidic languages, i.e. prehistoric languages related to Semitic.[2] 

I have to limit the illustration here to very few examples. The word _key_ is among them, which accounts for one sense of the title of this paper. Another sense is that I have selected the examples in such a way that they can serve as keys to the prehistory of Northwestern Europe, namely of the speakers of English, Germanic, and their non-Indo-European contact languages. 

## 2. Phonological problems in Vasconic and Semitic loan etymology 

## 2.1 Vowel problems 

Many words of unknown origin show an alternation between short and long vowels, namely _i/ı¯_ , _u/u¯_ , and _a/a¯_ – _a/o¯_ , alternations which are reminiscent of Indo-European quantitative ablaut, namely full grade vs. lengthened grade, but 

228 Theo Vennemann 

are, at least partially, unexpected from an Indo-European point of view. This kind of apparent ablaut can in principle have two sources: 

- a. It can belong to the source languages, which presupposes that these languages had quantity and quantitative ablaut and implies that ablauting sets were borrowed from those languages. 

- b. It can have originated in the borrowing process itself, namely by assigning quantity to vowels in words borrowed from languages which did not themselves have quantity. 

The old Semitic languages have a long vs. short vowel contrast and make, furthermore, intensive use of both qualitative and quantitative ablaut. Vowel alternations resembling ablaut in loan-words may thus go back to a giving Semitic language. 

By contrast, Basque is, and has been for as long as we know its history, a language without quantity in vowels. Here only the second explanation is available for presumed Vasconic loans in Indo-European languages. The important point is that quantity assignment is unpredictable in such a situation: one and the same loan-word may be assigned length in one dialect and shortness in another, or length in one derivate and shortness in another. Several examples have already been found of such loaning doublets with both long and short vowels of the same quality.[3] One such case will be shown directly. 

## 2.2 Consonant problems 

Many words of unknown origin also show alternations between plosives with identical place of articulation but different grade, or manner of articulation. Following the terminological model of _ablaut_ and _umlaut_ , I call this type of alternation _beilaut_ . Such beilaut variation is also to be expected in loaning doublets.[4] The reason is that the number of plosive series need not be the same foragivenpairoflanguagesincontact,andeveniftheirnumberdoescorrespond, the phonetic properties of the series need not match, so that speakers may be uncertain about the proper identification of a plosive with regard to its grade. To be more specific for the pairs of languages here assumed, Germanic and Vasconic, and Germanic and Semitic: Germanic had three series of plosives at an early level of development but Basque, and therefore probably Proto-Vasconic, only two; problems of mapping are inevitable in such a situation, see (1). 

_Key_ issues in English etymology 229 

|(1)|Proto-Vasconic|Proto-Vasconic|Proto-Vasconic|Pre-Germanic|Pre-Germanic|||
|---|---|---|---|---|---|---|---|
||P|T|K|ph|th|kh|kwh|
||p|t|k|p’|t’|k’|k’w|
|||||b|d|g|gw|



Proto-Vasconic: capital letters – tense; lower-case letters – lax Pre-Germanic: apostrophe – tenseness.[5] 

And again: Pre-Germanic and Proto-Semitic both had three series of plosives, but these series differed considerably in their phonetic properties: the three preGermanic series included (a) an aspirated series, which changed into the ProtoGermanic fricatives by Grimm’s Law; (b) a tense-voiceless series which in later Germanic changed into aspirate plosives in Low Germanic and into affricates (positionally fricativized) in High Germanic; and (c) a series of lax plosives, positionally voiced and later positionally fricativized in some dialects. By contrast, the three Proto-Semitic series were voiceless, voiced, and ‘emphatic’ (probably pharyngealized or glottalized).[6] The problem for the borrowing speakers of finding proper matches in such a situation is obvious. See (2). 

|(2)|Proto-Semitic|Proto-Semitic||Pre-Germanic|||
|---|---|---|---|---|---|---|
||p|t|k|’<br>ph<br>th|kh|kwh|
||b|d|g|p’<br>t’|k’|k’w|
|||»t|q|b<br>d|g|gw|
||Proto-Semitic:||’|glottal stop|||
||||_»t_|a voiceless emphatic dental plosive|||
||||_q_|(also _»k_): a voiceless emphatic velar (or uvular)|||
|||||plosive|||



Proto-Semitic had, in addition, interdental fricatives, both emphatic and plain, and at least one dental emphatic fricative, which might on occasion sound similar to dental pre-Germanic plosives, namely in the absence of any fricatives but plain _s_ ; see (3). 

- (3) _[t] »_ a voiceless emphatic interdental fricative _[d] »_ a voiced emphatic interdental fricative _t_ a plain-voiceless interdental fricative _d_ a plain-voiced interdental fricative _s»_ a voiceless emphatic dental sibilant fricative 

These dental fricatives were more or less likely to be mapped onto pre-Germanic dental plosives in the borrowing process, while pre-Gmc.[+] _s_ was the most natural mapping target of Semit. _s_ , _z_ and _š_ . 

230 Theo Vennemann 

Proto-Semitic had, furthermore, velar, pharyngeal, and laryngeal fricatives, see (4), whose only phonetic counterparts in pre-Germanic, given attempts at sound substitution, would be the velar plosives and perhaps the glides: 

- (4) _hç_ a voiceless velar fricative 

   - _g˙_ a voiced velar fricative 

   - _h»_ a voiceless pharyngeal fricative 

   - ‘ a voiced pharyngeal fricative 

   - _h_ a voiceless laryngeal fricative 

Thus, we have no right to expect matches in contact etymologies of the same degree of precision, or _Lautgesetzlichkeit_ ,[7] as in cognate etymologies. 

## 2.3 Problems of sound change and relative chronology 

Furthermore, it has to be considered that the kind of correspondence may depend on the time level at which the word-transfer took place; i.e. sound changes both in the giving and in the receiving languages have to be taken into account. We know all this to be important from the study of borrowing in historical languages. E.g. the words of the English triple _candle_ [k-], _chandler_ [v-], and _chandelier_ [w-] show great semantic and phonological similarity. Nevertheless assuming a loan complex would probably cause concern if we did not know that a chain of sound changes [k-] > [v-] (before [a]) > [w-] occurred in the giving language, so that we can associate the borrowings with three different stages of this development, that way establishing a relative chronology of the borrowing events. Of course, in the case of a pair of languages as well documented and intensively studied as French and English we even have a good picture of the absolute chronology of the events: _candle_ is attested in English as a Latin loan-word since about a. 700, _chandler_ since the fourteenth century, and _chandelier_ since the eighteenth (in the military sense the seventeenth) century. Likewise, relating Engl. _cheese_ [v-] to Lat. _ca¯seus_ [k-] would cause concern if we did not consider the development of umlauted vowels and preceding velars in the early history of English. 

In the analysis of possible prehistoric loan-words, we have to proceed with exactly the same care, considering sound changes in both languages of the studied pair. E.g. relating Engl. _gate_ to Bq _at(h)e_ ‘door, gate’ will certainly cause concern as long as it is not considered that word-initial _g-_ has been lost in some other Basque words as well, so that a reconstruction[+] _gathe_ , with a sequence of changes[+] _gathe_ >[+] _hathe_ > _athe_ , _ate_ , is reasonable. If in addition we consider 

_Key_ issues in English etymology 231 

that English _gate_ is borrowed from Scandinavian (ON _gata_ ) and has other relatives in Germanic as well, but that this group of related words presents certain formal problems which are typical of borrowed material, the assumption that the unexplained Proto-Germanic words with a root[+] _gat-_ and meanings such as ‘gate, town street, hole’ represent a Vasconic loan complex becomes normal (cf. Vennemann 1998a:§2.3). 

Conversely, while for _gate_ we have to consider change in the giving language, the following pair requires attention to change in the borrowing language, viz. to Grimm’s Law. I have traced both _plough_ (<[+] _plo¯g-_ ), name of an instrument for dividing the ground, and _folk_ (<[+] _fulk-_ ), originally meaning a division of an army, to the same Semitic root _plg_ ‘to divide.’[8] Even putting aside problems of vocalism, this etymology evidently can only be made to work if _folk_ is assumed to be an old loan-word so that Grimm’s Law (with[+] _p_ > _f_ ,[+] _g_ > _k_ ) may apply, whereas _plough_ must be assumed to be a young loan-word which has only entered the language after the operation of Grimm’s Law. Fortunately this is borne out by known facts: there is no indication that _folk_ is not a ProtoGermanic word, but clear evidence that _plough_ , which only appears in West Germanic and is attested late, is a comparatively recent loan; cf. the quotations in Vennemann (1998d:§1). I will return to these words in 4.1 below. 

## 3. A Vasconic etymology: E _key_ 

## 3.1 Review of the _hook_ set 

Etymologists have identified a group of words of similar, although not _lautgesetzlich_ relatable phonological shape, and all meaning ‘hook’, cf. (5). 

- (5) ON _haki_ , OHG _hac_ , _ha¯co_ , _ha¯ho_ , _ha¯go_ , OE _haca,_[9] _ho¯c_ , OFris. _ho¯k_ , cf. G _Haken_ , E _hook_ , also ON _hœkja_ ‘crutch’, perhaps Russ. _kógot’_ ‘claw, iron hook’ 

Members of the set and derivates live on in various forms. Cf. the examples in (6): 

- (6) a. E _hook_ , E dial. _hake_ (from Old Norse) ‘a hook, esp. a pot-hook; the draught iron of a plough’, G _Haken_ ‘hook’ 

   - b. E _hake_ ( _Merlucius vulgaris_ , extended to _Phycis_ found on the coast of North America and to the New England _Lotella rhacinus_ ), cf. Norw. _hakefisk_ , lit. ‘hook-fish’, applied to fish, as the salmon or trout, with a hooked under-jaw[10] 

232 Theo Vennemann 

- c. G _Hecht_ ‘pike’, OHG _hehhit_ (<[+] _hakida_ ), cf. OE _haced_ (<[+] _hakida_ ), OE _hacod_ (<[+] _hakuda_ )[11] 

- d. E _hatchel_ , earlier _hechele_ , _hetchel_ (an instrument for combing flax or hemp), G _Hechel_ ‘hatchel’ (<[+] _hak-il-_ )[12] 

In the roots of these ‘hook’ words we have apparent nominal ablauts which at first sight look quite strange: 

- + 

- (7) a. _a/e¯_ (> _a/a¯_ ), e.g. in OHG _hac_ , _ha¯co_ b. + _a/o¯_ (> _a/o¯_ ), e.g. in OE _haca_ , _ho¯c_ 

They become understandable if we interpret this strange set as a loan complex based on an etymon with an[+] _a_ unspecified for length, cf. (8). 

**==> picture [235 x 46] intentionally omitted <==**

If such an[+] _a_ is assigned shortness in pre-Germanic, we receive the short[+] _a_ reflected in OE _haca_ , OHG _haco_ , etc., cf. (8a). If however the[+] _a_ of the etymon is assigned length, the problem arises that prehistoric Germanic did not have a long[+] _a¯_ . Therefore, a phonetically similar long vowel had to be substituted, and the phonetically most similar long vowel phonemes were _e¯_ and _o¯_ , cf. (8b). These are exactly the long vowels we find in the set of ‘hook’ words. 

We also note some beilaut variation: if we consider the _hak-_ variant of the root as basic, which is imperative if the comparison with Russian _kógot’_ is accepted, then Old High German shows both possible beilaut variants, _ha¯ho_ with _h_ for _k_ , and _ha¯go_ with _g_ for _k_ ; cf. (9): 

(9) OHG _ha¯co_ /ha¯ko/; /k/ fi /h/: _ha¯ho_ /ha¯ho/; /k/ fi /g/: _ha¯go_ /ha¯go/ Note also (10), Goth. _hoha_ ‘plough’, with both ablaut and beilaut: 

(10) OHG _hac_ /hak/ ‘hook’; /a/ fi /o¯/, /k/ fi /h/: Goth. _hoha_ /ho¯ha/ ‘plough’ The word shows the[+] _a_ fi[+] _o¯_ counterpart of the earlier[+] _a_ fi[+] _e¯_ example, OHG + _he¯ho_ (> _ha¯ho_ ), cf. (7.a and b).13 

In short, we are dealing with precisely the kind of problematical word set characterized earlier in this paper. And we are undoubtedly dealing with a loan complex. This is clear not only from the internal phonological variability of the set, which is not found in Indo-European cognate sets under _Lautgesetzlichkeit_ , 

_Key_ issues in English etymology 233 

but also from the limited distribution: the ‘hook’ set is strictly Germanic, with only a possible relative in a neighboring language.[14] 

I have made a proposal for the source of this set, namely that the ‘hook’ set is a Vasconic loan complex (Vennemann 1995:§6.9; 1998e:227). See the Basque words in (11). 

- (11) Bq _kako_ , _kakho_ ‘hook’ (de Azkue 1984)[15] Bq _khako_ , _kako_ (also: _gako_ ) ‘hook, cramp, clasp [etc.]’ (Löpelmann 1968) Bq _khakodun_ ‘hook-shaped’ (Löpelmann 1968) 

   - Bq _kakola_ ‘walking-stick, crutch, stilt’ (Löpelmann 1968) Bq _gakulu_ ‘sting, spur’ (regional, de Azkue 1984) 

Within the context of this proposal, I would now like to suggest my etymology for _key_ . 

## 3.2 The English and Frisian _key_ word 

The origin of the word _key_ ‘instrument for locking and unlocking’ is unknown.[16] It certainly has no Indo-European relatives. Even within Germanic it is limited to English and Frisian, cf. (12): 

- (12) E _key_ , OFris. _kei_ , _kay_ ‘key’ 

The oldest English attestations of the word (beginning c. 1000 A.D.) show the forms in (13):[17] 

- (13) OE _c ¯æg_ , _c ¯æge_ f., _c ¯æga_ m. ‘key’ 

Projecting this word back to an early pre-Anglo-Frisian stage, the forms would appear as in (14): 

- (14) OE _c ¯æg_ f. <[+] _ka¯go¯-_ f. ( _o¯_ -stem) OE _c ¯æge_ f. <[+] _ka¯go¯(n-)_ f. ( _o¯n_ -stem) OE _c ¯æga_ m. <[+] _ka¯go¯(n-)_ m. ( _o¯n_ -stem) 

These reconstructed forms are reminiscent of the Old High German ‘hook’ set _ha¯co_ , _ha¯ho_ , _ha¯go_ , with additional beilaut variation of the anlaut. If they were Proto-Germanic we could reconstruct pre-Gmc.[+] _ge¯'k[h] o¯-_ which would yield, with Verner’s and Grimm’s Laws,[+] _ke¯'go¯-_ and further, with accent shift, PGmc. + _'ke¯go¯-_ and thus (with West-Germanic + _e¯_ > + _a¯_ ) + _'ka¯go¯-_ , i.e. the shape we need for the Anglo-Frisian word. This pre-Gmc.[+] _ge¯'k[h] o¯-_ gives the impression that a PVasc.[+] _gako_ was fitted into pre-Germanic by supplying length to both vowels 

234 Theo Vennemann 

and then assigning gender[18] and declension classes on the evidence of the phonological shape alone. However, since the _key_ word is restricted to AngloFrisian, whereas the ‘hook’ set is found more widely in Germanic, it is more likely that the _key_ word was borrowed later, when speakers were distributed over a larger territory than the speakers of Proto-Germanic. The fact that the words of the ‘hook’ set all show an initial _h-_ , which in comparison with the plosive anlaut of the Basque words can only have arisen in the Proto-Germanic Consonant Shift (Grimm’s Law), whereas the _key_ word preserves the plosive quality of the Vasconic anlaut, suggests that _key_ is a later loan-word, borrowed directly into pre-Anglo-Frisian.[19] 

Two questions arise at this point. The first concerns the semantics of the word. Is it reasonable to associate a word for ‘key’ with a set of words for ‘hook’? I think it is. Before the advent of the modern pin-tumbler lock, the cylinder lock, the magnetic keycard lock, and sensor-computer and voice recognition systems, the typical key was hook-shaped, and many ordinary household keys still are, as is also true of the symbolic keys of public buildings or cities. The oldest keys found by archaeologists likewise had the shape of a hook, cf. the following quotation, especially the passage emphasized in the translation: 

So wußte man bereits in der Latènezeit Mitteleuropas (bes. in den keltischen Oppida) eiserne, an Holzkästen befestigte Schlösser zu fertigen, die auch in german. Gräbern der jüngeren Kaiserzeit (Pritzier, Mecklenburg, 4. Jh. u. Z.) belegt sind. Als Schließvorrichtung dienten überwiegend haken- und T-förmige Schlüssel, wie sie in röm. Siedlungen und germ. Gräbern ausgegraben wurden. (…) Seit dem 4.-5. Jh. hatten die Schlüssel schon kompliziert gestaltete Bärte (Herrmann et al. 1987:II.237). 

As early as the Latène period of Central Europe (esp. in Celtic oppida), iron locks were attached to wooden chests; such locks were found in Germanic graves of younger imperial times (Pritzier, Mecklenburg, 4th century CE). The locking devices consisted mostly of _hook- and T-shaped keys_ of the kind excavated in Roman settlements and Germanic graves. (…) As early as the fourth or fifth century, keys were made with beards of complicated shapes. [my translation] 

To the extent that keys were used in prehistoric times, they were undoubtedly likewise hook-shaped. There is then no semantic reason that requires us etymologically to dissociate a ‘key’ word from a ‘hook’ word, in the case on hand: the _key_ word from the _hook_ words. 

The second question concerns the level of semantic development at which the borrowing of the _key_ word took place: was it borrowed with the original 

_Key_ issues in English etymology 235 

meaning ‘hook’, only developing the special meaning of ‘key’ within AngloFrisian, or was the word directly borrowed from Vasconic with the special meaning ‘key’? Either way the answer may be hard to prove. But I consider a direct borrowing of the _key_ word with the ‘key’ meaning to be more likely, and for three reasons. First, since all the Vasconic _hook_ words share the initial _h-_ , it would be hard to understand why the semantically derivative _key_ word does not have the same anlaut. Second, the specialization of meaning from ‘hook’ to ‘key’ is ascertained for Basque; cf. (15). 

(15) Bq _gako_ , _gakho_ ‘key’ (different dialect forms)[20] 

This word is, according to Agud & Tovar (1991:s.v. _gako_ ), the same in origin as Bq _kako_ ‘hook’. This adds further plausibility to the etymology and suggests, once again, that the semantic transition from the _hook_ group to the _key_ word was not made within Germanic but in Vasconic and that the _key_ word thus represents a separate Vasconic borrowing. Third, the Basques were known for their advanced metallurgy in the Middle Ages. They may have given us _knife_ , another word for a metal instrument, of a less limited but nonetheless peculiar distribution.[21] Thus it is at least conceivable that earlier on they also gave us the _key_ word, and for the same reasons. 

Let us finally see how the etymology of _gako_ ‘key’ is seen in a Basque perspective, cf. Agud & Tovar (1991:s.v. _gako_ ; 1993:s.v. _kako_ ). While the main reference under Bq _gako_ ‘key’ is to Bq _kako_ ‘hook’, as already said, a number of similar Asian words are cited from the literature, to which Agud & Tovar do not assign any significance. From the present point of view, the following short remark is interesting: 

Lewy, _RIEV_ 25, 237 ( _Kl.Schr._ 574) compara ingl. _key_ , de ags. _caeg_ , (cf. an. _kagi_ ‘piloto’, pero esto es una comparación muy personal de Lewy, que no parece probable); se trata de una palabra oscura, según se ve en los Diccionarios de Skeat y Onions (Agud & Tovar 1991:s.v. _gako_ ). 

Lewy, _RIEV_ 25, 237 ( _Kl.Schr._ 574) compares Engl. _key_ , from OE _caeg_ (cf. ON _kagi_ [used as a nickname, T.V.],[22] but this is a very personal comparison of Lewy’s which does not appear probable); according to the dictionaries of Skeat and Onions, _key_ is an obscure word. [my translation] 

I too will keep ON _kagi_ apart. But otherwise I welcome the fact that the comparison of OE _c ¯æg_ and Bq _gako_ has been made before, even if outside of a theory which makes such a comparison plausible. 

236 Theo Vennemann 

Lewy himself (1961:574) presents his observation as “ein wunderliches Beispiel” (‘a curious example’) for a recurring pair of meanings: both Bq _gako/ gakho_ and E _key_ mean ‘key’ as well as ‘the middle stone of an arch’. He is also struck by the fact that E _key_ (as OE _c ¯æg_ ) “wunderlich an das bask. _gakho_ anklingt” (‘curiously chimes in with Bq _gakho’_ ). And he asks, “Ob auch noch bask. _kako_ , _khako_ … ‘Haken’ heranzuziehen ist” (‘whether Bq _kako_ , _khako_ … ‘hook’ may have to be called on’). Thus it remains unclear whether Lewy’s comparison is intended as an etymology for E _key_ or merely a collection of observations. In any event, the proposal was not accepted, and for an obvious reason: there was at that time no theory in which to interpret the connection. Within a theory according to which almost all of Europe was Vasconic before the advent of the Indo-Europeans,[23] various and repeated kinds of contact between speakers of Germanic and Vasconic languages are to be expected.[24] 

## 4. A Semitic etymology: E _fallow_ , G _Falge/Felge_ 

## 4.1 Review of the _plough_ and _folk_ words 

As in the case of the Vasconic _key_ etymology where I could fall back on the etymology of the _hook_ set, I will in this Semitic section present a new etymology which naturally connects to a previously etymologized set, that of E _plough_ , a West Germanic word, and E _folk_ , a common Germanic word.[25] These as well as some further related words have been treated in detail in Vennemann (1998d). They have been interpreted as loan-words based on Semitic words containing the following family of roots of the shape _plC_ , all of them possessing the same basic meaning ‘to divide.’ The list of root forms in (16) is collected from Hebrew, but similar instances occur in other Semitic languages too (cf. Gesenius 1915 for the root forms as well as for exemplification): 

(16) _pl’_ , _plg_ , _plgh_ , _plh_ , _plh »_ , _plk_ , _pll_[26] 

The most interesting members of the set may be those in (17) and (18): 

- (17) _plg_ which in several Semitic languages[27] means ‘to split, to divide’ and which in Assyr. _puluggu_ , _pulungu_ has taken on the specialized meaning of ‘district’[28] and, as a derivate _plgh_ , in Hebrew has taken on the specialized meanings ‘district (as a division of a tribe)’,[29] vocalized _p[e] laga¯h_ , and ‘division, class (of priests)’, vocalized _p[e] luga¯h_ 

_Key_ issues in English etymology 237 

- (18) _pl »h_ with its specialized meaning of ‘dividing the ground’, i.e. ‘to furrow, to plough’, as in Aramaic, Modern Hebrew, and Arabic 

A semantic specialization similar to those in (17) can be seen in PGmc.[+] _fulka-_ ‘division of an army’ (later ‘aggregate of people, tribe, people, nation’, cf. E _folk_ , _flock_ , G _Volk_ ) and especially in ON _fylki_ (<[+] _ga-fulk-j-a_ ) ‘kriegerschar, haufen; distrikt’ (‘band of warriors, troop; district’; cf. de Vries 1977:s.v.), which Zoëga (1910:s.v. _fylki_ ) glosses as ‘(1) district, county, shire; (2) battalion, host (in battle)’.[30] 

A semantic specialization similar to that in (18) can be seen in the West Germanic _plough_ word ([+] _plo¯g-_ , E _plough_ , G _Pflug_ ) and also in the West Germanic strong verb[+] _plegan_ (G _pflegen_ ‘to cultivate, to do customarily’ and, a _-jan_ -derivate, E _play_ ): a plough is the modern instrument for dividing the ground, for furrowing; and the original meaning of[+] _plegan_ ‘to cultivate’ is likely to have been the same as the original meaning of _to cultivate_ , late Lat. _cultivare_ itself, namely ‘to till’, cf. the basic verb Lat. _colere_ ‘to till, tend, care for, cultivate’ and also Lat. _cultu¯ra_ ‘cultivation, agriculture’, _cultor_ ‘cultivator, tiller’.[31] Even in the _OED_ the first set of meanings given for E _cultivate_ is ‘to bestow labour and attention upon (land) in order to the raising of crops; to till; to improve and render fertile by husbandry’. 

## 4.2 A new etymology for E _fallow_ , G _Falge/Felge_ 

Within the scope of the latter etymology, that of West Gmc.[+] _plo¯g-_ ‘plough’ and West Gmc.[+] _plegan_ ‘to cultivate’, I would now like to turn to a word for which so far no convincing etymology has been found. The word is E _fallow_ , attested since about a. 1300, for which the OED gives the following definitions: 

For the noun, ‘1. A piece of ploughed land; also _collect_ [ _ively_ ] ploughed land in general, arable land. _Obs_ [ _olete_ ]. (…) 2. Ground that is well ploughed and harrowed, but left uncropped for a whole year or more’. (…) For the adjective, ‘Of land: frequent in phrases, _to lie_ , _to lay fallow_ . a. That is uncropped for the current year. (…) b. Uncultivated’. For the verb, ‘1. _trans._ To plough or break up (land); to prepare for sowing. Also rarely, _to fallow up_ . 2. To plough and break up (land) without sowing (it), for the purpose of destroying weeds, etc., and for mellowing the soil; to lay fallow. Also _absol_ [ _utely_ ].’ 

There is also, for the noun, adjective, and verb, a chiefly Scottish variant form _fauch_ or _faugh_ , in northern dialects _fauf_ (cf. _OED_ :s.v. _fauch_ ). The following remarks are given in the _OED_ concerning the etymology of _fallow_ : 

238 Theo Vennemann 

The relation between this and the cognate fallow _a._[32] and v. is not quite clear. The OE. _fælŠing_ , glossed _novalia_ (‘fallows’) and _occas._ (‘harrows’) in _Corpus Glossary_ , seems to imply a vb. * _fealŠian_ (= fallow _v._ ), f. * _fealh_ , recorded in pl. _fealŠa_ harrows, implements for breaking clods ( _occas._ Epinal Gl.). The n. and adj., which have not been found in OE, were either f. _fealh_ or f. the vb. Cf. OHG. _felga_ harrow, mod.Ger. (…) and East Fris. _falge_ fallow (n.), _falgen_ to break up ground, plough (OED:s.v. _fallow_ n.). 

The root form of E _fallow_ and _fauch/faugh/fauf_ is[+] _falg-_ . In _fallow_ the _-g-_ , spirantized _-Š_ -, was vocalized. In _fauch/faugh/fauf_ the _-Š_ was devoiced, _-χ_ , and the _l_ was vocalized,[33] and in _fauf_ the _-χ_ was replaced by _-f_ (as in _enough_ [−f] etc.). 

Except for the Frisian and German connections, nothing is offered toward an etymology of the word, either in the _OED_ or in Onions (1966). The word is apparently so obscure that not even a remark such as the usual “of unknown origin” seems necessary. The picture improves only slightly when we compare what the German dictionaries have to say. First, Kluge/Seebold (1995:s.v. _Felge_ 2): 

Felge2 f., arch., auch _Falge_ ‘Brachland nach dem Umpflügen’ (< 13. Jh.). Ahd. _felga_ ‘umgepflügtes Feld’, vgl. mndt. _valge_ , mndl. _valge_ ; dann erst wieder in der Neuzeit mundartlich (bairisch) bezeugt. Mhd. nur _valgen_ , _velgen_ ‘umackern, umgraben’, aber ae. _fealh_ , _fe(a)lg_ ‘gefelgte Brache’. Außergermanisch vergleicht sich gall. _olka_ ‘Ackerland’, russ. _polosá_ ‘(Land)-Strich, Streifen, Ackerfurche.’[34] 

_Felge_ 2 f., archaic, also _Falge_ ‘fallow-ground after ploughing’ (< 13th century). OHG _felga_ ‘ploughed-up field’, cp. MLG _valge_ , MDu. _valge_ ; afterwards only attested in modern dialects (Bavarian). MHG only _valgen_ , _velgen_ ‘to plough up, to break up (the soil)’, but OE _fealh_ , _fe(a)lg_ ‘ploughed-up fallow-ground’. Outside Germanic, Gaul. _olka_ ‘plough-land’, Russ. _polosá_ ‘tract (of land), strip(e), furrow of a field’ may be compared. [my translation] 

Pfeifer et al. (1997) treat Kluge/Seebold’s Felge2 in the same article as _Felge_ 1 ‘rim of a wheel’. They write on _Felge_ 2: 

Zieht man ahd. _felgen_ ‘umpflügen’ (Hs. 12. Jh.), mhd. mnd. _valgen_ , _velgen_ ‘umpflügen, umgraben’, ae. _fealgian_ , engl. _to fallow_ ‘pflügen’ mit ahd. _felga_ ‘umgepflügtes Feld’ (Hs. 13. Jh.), mnd. mnl. _valge_ , aengl. _fealh_ , engl. _fallow_ und gall.-spätlat. _olca_ ‘zum Pflügen taugliches Land’ (woraus afrz. _ouche_ ‘pflügbares Land’, frz. ‘guter Ackerboden, Obstgarten’), aruss. _polosa_ , russ. _polosá_ (����%�) ‘Streifen, Landstrich, Ackerfurche’ heran, so kann von ie. * _pelk-[´]_ , * _polk-[´]_ ‘wenden, drehen’ ausgegangen (…) werden. (Pfeifer et al. 1997:s.v. _Felge_ ) If one connects OHG _felgen_ ‘to plough up’ (manuscript, 12th century), MHG MLG _valgen_ , _velgen_ ‘to plough up, to break up (the soil)’, OE _fealgian_ , E _to fallow_ ‘to plough’ with OHG _felga_ ‘ploughed-up field’ (manuscript, 13th 

_Key_ issues in English etymology 239 

century), MLG MDu. _valge_ , OE _fealh_ , E _fallow_ and Gaul.-Late Lat. _olca_ ‘land good for ploughing’ (whence OFrench _ouche_ ‘arable land’, Fr. ‘good arable soil, orchard’), ORuss. _polosa_ , Russ. _polosá_ (����%�) ‘strip(e), tract (of land), furrow of a field’, PIE * _pelk-[´]_ , * _polk-[´]_ ‘to turn, twist’ may be reconstructed. [my translation] 

Even if we accept the connections with Gaulish and Russian offered here, we are not entitled to interpret the _fallow_ word as Indo-European, because a word shared only by a few contiguous Indo-European languages is likely to be a loanword from a non-Indo-European language. This is, indeed, the proposal I would like to make. 

Looking at the root structure of the _fallow_ word,[+] _falg-_ , one recognizes the sequence of a voiceless labial, the liquid _l_ , and a velar plosive already known to be that of a Germanic word having to do with the meaning of ploughing, namely _plough_ , West Gmc.[+] _plo¯g-_ , itself. The initial _f-_ of _fallow_ tells us, furthermore, that the word was borrowed before the operation of the Proto-Germanic Consonant shift, the[+] _p[h] t[h] k[h] k[wh]_ >[+] _f þ x x[w]_ part of Grimm’s Law. The rootfinal _-g-_ may reflect whatever the so-called aspirated media[+] _g[h]_ of Proto-IndoEuropean was in Germanic at the time of borrowing. Alternatively, it may reflect the pre-Germanic velar tenuis[+] _k[h]_ in a word whose accent fell behind the root, because it would then be subject to Verner’s Law which would likewise yield the root-final _-g-_ of[+] _falg-_ . In any event, the source was a word[+] _palC-_ with a root-final consonant C which would be mapped onto the appropriate preGermanic velar. The pattern C1VC2C3, including C1aC2C3, is the most basic Semitic nominal pattern for triconsonantal roots (Lipin´ski 1997:§§28.12, 29.5). Specifically, “it constitutes the stem of the Assyro-Babylonian stative ( _pars-_ ), with the exception of the 3rd pers. masc. sing. ( _paris_ , ‘is separated’)”.[35] Since the originalmeaningofWestGmc.[+] _falg-_ appearstobe‘(thatwhichis)inaploughedup state’ or ‘(field) in a ploughed-up state’, which would be the meaning of a verbal adjective, the following quotation may be most appropriate: 

The aspectual category of the verbal system is based on the adjectival _C_ 1 _aC_ 2 _C_ 3 pattern, represented e.g. by the Assyro-Babylonian verbal adjective _pars(um)_ , and developed to the stative/permansive forms, well-known in Old Akkadian and in Assyro-Babylonian: _CaCiC_ (e.g. _damiq_ , ‘he is good’, _CaCaC_ (e.g. _rapaš_ , ‘he is wide’), _CaCuC_ (e.g. _qarub_ , ‘he is near’). The anaptyctic vowel _a/i/u_ should appear only when there is a two-consonant cluster (…), i.e. in the 3rd pers. masc. sing.[36] (Lipin´ski 1997:§38.3) 

240 Theo Vennemann 

Assuming that the language which gave[+] _falg-_ ‘ploughed-up ground’ to West Germanic was a Semitic language with a root _plC_ ‘to divide the ground, to plough’, e.g. _pl »h_ as in Aramaic and Arabic, we may also assume that it had a verbal adjective[+] _palC-_ , e.g. _palh- »_ ‘ploughed up’. Specifically, the development may have been as in (19). The reconstruction makes use of the observation that penult and ultimate word accentuation systems were wide-spread in Semitic (Lipin´ski 1997:§§25.1–8) and that in particular Phoenician, which “appears to have had a strong stress accent, usually accentuates the final syllable of the word” (§25.6). 

- (19) Semit. _palh-'- »_[37] ‘ploughed up (sc. ground)’ Æ pre-Gmc. (West)[38][+] _p[h] alk[h] -'-_ ‘ploughed up ground’ > Palaeo-Gmc. (West)[+] _falg-'-_ (idem) (with Verner’s and Grimm’s Laws) > Proto-Gmc. (West)[+] _'falg-_ (idem) (with change to first-syllable accent) 

If this reconstruction is correct, it suggests that the pre-Germanic people, even though they are likely to have brought a knowledge of agriculture to their North Europeanhomeland,learnedspecificfarmingtechniquesfromspeakersofSemitic languages. This would support the general theory that Semitic colonizers operated along the Atlantic littoral all the way to Scandinavia and, in particular, that prehistoric Germanic developed under a Semitic superstratum.[39] 

## 5. “ _Volk_ , _Sippe_ , _Adel_ , (…)” 

_“Volk_ ; _Sippe_ ; _Adel_ , (…)”, i.e. ‘people, family, nobility, (…)’: those are, according to Pohl (2000:6), the first three of several key concepts of research into the Germanic world. All three words are of Proto-Germanic origin but have no generally accepted etymology: G _Volk_ , E _folk_ (< PGmc.[+] _fulk-a-_ ), originally a military term meaning ‘division (of an army)’; G _Sippe_ , E _sib_ (< PGmc.[+] _sibjo¯_ ) ‘(extended) family’; G _Adel_ , OE _æðel-_ (< PGmc.[+] _aþal-_ ) ‘nobility’. 

It is here where the expression “ _key_ issues” of the title of the present paper takes on its second, figurative meaning most clearly: military, legal, and constitutional terms are central concepts of a society and as such reflect the language of the ruling class. If such terms are of foreign origin the language of the ruling class is likely to have been a foreign language. Since PGmc.[+] _fulk-a-_ , + _sibjo¯_ , and + _aþal-_ have no plausible Indo-European etymology, they ought to reflect the language of a foreign ruling class. 

_Key_ issues in English etymology 241 

I have shown in recent papers (Vennemann 1998d, 2001, forthc.) that all three key concepts are in Germanic named with Semitic loan-words. They thereby tell us something about the political prehistory of Germania, namely that it was once a Semitic colony, or dominion. 

Since these three words name key concepts of an even more forcible character than E _key_ itself, I summarize all three etymologies in this paper. For the _folk_ word this has already been done in §4.1 above. I can therefore turn directly to[+] _sibjo¯ ‘_ family’ and[+] _aþal-_ ‘nobility’. 

The English words _family_ and _nobility_ are well understood: they are French loan-words that came into the language after the Norman Conquest of a. 1066. The reason is clear: in the centuries after the Conquest Anglo-Saxon society was redefined, so to speak, by the French-speaking new aristocracy, and the subjugated Anglo-Saxons in time learned the new terminology, together with the corresponding institutions; cf. (20). 

(20) French Superstratum English _famille_ < _familia sibb_ fi _familie_ , _famyle_ > _family nobilité æðel-_ fi _nobilite_ > _nobility_ 

This is the proto-typical effect of a foreign superstratum. It is interesting to note in this connection that the origin of the Latin word _familia_ is itself a matter of debate. One etymological suggestion is that it is an Etruscan loan-word. This makes sense: Rome was at an early date ruled by Etruscan kings, which means that Latin developed under an Etruscan superstratum. 

The question suggested by this model is thus: what is the origin of OE _sibb_ , which survives in E _sib_ , _sibling_ , and _gossip,_[40] and of _æðel-_ , which is now only remembered from the names of important personages of early English history, e.g. _Æðelsta¯n_ , _Æðelweard_ , _Æðelwold_ , _Æðelbald_ , _Æðelred_ , _Æðelflæd_ ? Both words are without doubt of Proto-Germanic origin, see (21) and (22): 

- (21) PGmc.[+] _sibjo¯_ : OE _sibb_ , OFris. _sibbe_ , G _Sippe_ , OHG _sippa_ , OS _sibbia_ , ON _sifjar_ plur. ‘relatives’; also adjective: OE _sibb_ , OFris. _sib_ , MDu. _sibbe_ , _zibbe_ , OHG _sippi_ , Goth. _(un)sibjis_ 

- (22) PGmc.[+] _aþal-_ : OE _æðel-_ , OHG _adal_ , OSwed. _aþal_ , Goth. _aþal-_ (in the name _Athalaricus_ ); also adjective: OE _æðel-_ , OFris. _ethele_ , OS _eðili_ , OHG _edili_ (G _edel_ ) 

But neither has a plausible Indo-European etymology. For[+] _aþal-_ this is not even to be expected, because the society that spoke Proto-Indo-European did not yet have a nobility. Mallory & Adams (1997) have no entry _nobility_ , and the 

242 Theo Vennemann 

great number of different terms for the nobleman in the Indo-European languages (see Buck 1949:§19.36) show that there was no such legal institution in Proto-Indo-European society. Speakers probably simply used circumlocutions when differences began being made, as could be done even later, see e.g. Goth. _(manna) godakunds_ meaning ‘ _ε�γεν�ς_ , of good descent’. For the notion of family, both in its narrow and its wide definition, there are again several different terms, so that it is unknown which term was used with the sense of Gmc.[+] _sibjo¯_ . It is possible that pre-existing rival expressions such as[+] _kunja-_ ‘family’ (cf. E _kin_ ) and[+] _hı¯wiskja-_ ‘family’ (OE _hı¯wisc_ ) had to give up some of their semantic ranges in order to accommodate[+] _sibjo¯_ . 

While PGmc.[+] _sibjo¯_ ‘family’ and[+] _aþal-_ ‘nobility’ have no etymological matches in Indo-European, they both have very good matches in Semitic (Vennemann 2001, forthc.). Semitic expressions for ‘family’ are given in (23). 

- (23) Pun. _šp »h_ ‘family’, Ugarit. _šp »h_ ‘posterity’, Hebr. _mišpaha¯ »_ (with prefixal _mi-_ ) ‘family’,[41] Hebr. _šip »ha¯_ ‘slave-girl’ (“originally a house-born girl who was not a legal daughter of the _paterfamilias_ ”, cf. Lipin´ski 1997:§62.4), Arab. _siƒa¯ »h_ ‘concubine by capture, cohabitation by force’ (Mod Arab. _siƒa¯ »h_ ‘whoring, fornication’) 

A number of important legal parallels between Old Semitic and Old Germanic family structures can be cited (cf. Vennemann forthc.:§§7.1–3). This should indeed be so if the Germanic word is a superstratal Semitic loan-word. 

Turning finally to[+] _aþal-_ ‘nobility’, we find the expression well represented in Semitic; see (24). 

- (24) Arabic (with _þ_ for the now common transcription _t_ , following Möller 1911:19): _’aþala_ ‘it had root, or a foundation, it was (became) firmly rooted’, _’aþula_ ‘it (nobility) was of ancient origin’, Part. _mu’aþþulu[n]_ ‘having root, old, of ancient origin’, _’aþlatu[n]_ ‘root, foundation, origin (of a thing and of a man), honor, dignity, glory’, _’aþa¯lu[n]_ ‘glory, honour, dignity, nobility’ (Lane 1863) 

Arabic has another, probably related root with a similar range of meanings as _’tl_ ( _’atl_ ), viz. _’sl»_ with its most basic word _’asl»_ ‘root’. This root or root form is the more common one in Modern Arabic; cf. the selection in (25), taken from Wehr (1985). 

- (25) a. _’asula»_ and _’asa¯la»_ ‘to be or become firmly rooted; stand fast; be of noble origin’ 

   - b. _’asl»_ (plur. _’usu¯l»_ ) ‘root; stem [of a tree]; foundation, origin, descent’ 

_Key_ issues in English etymology 243 

- c. _’aslı¯»_ ‘original, Proto-; genuine, pure; true’ 

- d. _’ası¯l»_ (plur. _’asa¯’il»_ , _’usala¯»_ ) ‘of pure, noble descent; thorough-bred; genuine, firmly rooted, innate, native inhabitant’ 

- e. _’asa¯la»_ ‘originality, original or traditional kind [e.g. of Arabhood]; genuineness; noble kind of descent, purity of origin’ 

- f. _ta’sı¯l»_ ‘anchorage; rootedness; determination of the origin, tracing back to the foundation’ 

- g. _ta’sı¯la»_ ‘pedigree, genealogy’ 

- h. _ta’as» sul»_ ‘rootedness’ 

This alternative root form _’sl»_ also occurs in Hebrew, viz. in the Old Testament (Exodus 24,11), as _’syly»_ , vocalized _’[a] sîlê»_ , plur. constr. ‘the nobles’. It is also well reflected in Modern Hebrew (Ivrı¯t), cf. the examples in (26), taken from Baltsan (1992). 

- (26) a. _’swlh»_ [atsulah] ´‘aristocracy’, _’sylwt»_ [atsilut] ‘nobility’ 

   - b. _’syl»_ [atsil] ‘nobleman’ 

   - c. _’syly»_ [atsili] ‘noble (adj.)’ 

   - d. _n’sl»_ [ne’etsal] ‘ennobled’ 

The degree of phonological similarity between these forms and the Germanic words of the same semantic domain is astounding. Note in particular the nearidentity of Hebr. _’syly»_ [atsili] ‘noble (adj.)’ and G _edel_ < OHG _edili_ < pre-OHG + _aþili_ ‘noble (adj.)’. 

The root may also exist in Berber, and again with the most basic meaning ‘root’, cf. the words for root in various dialects in (27), taken from Bennett (1998:228, Table 116 ‘root’). But I could not find etymological confirmation for this connection. 

- (27) a. _aza» r»_ (Kabyle) 

   - b. _azur»_ (Ghadamsi, Wargla) 

   - c. _azu» r»_ (Ayt Seghrouchen, Shil »ha) 

   - d. _azγr_ (Shil »ha) 

Summarizing this section, we have a situation in Germanic with regard to Semitic that is parallel to that in English with regard to French, see (28): 

(28) Semitic Superstratum pre-Germanic + _plg_ (other terms) fi[+] _fulk-a-_ + _šp »h_ (other terms) fi[+] _sibj-o¯_ +’ _aT(V)l-_ 42 (no term) fi[+] _at[h] al-_ >[+] _aþal-_ 

244 Theo Vennemann 

To make the correspondence perfect, we find a derivate of the[+’] _aTl-_ root with the meaning ‘real estate’ not only in Germanic but also in Semitic, see (29): 

- (29) a. Nabataean _’sl’»_ ‘landed property’ (Gesenius 1915:s.v. _’sl»_ I) 

   - b. PGmc.[+] _o¯þal-/_[+] _o¯þil-_ ‘ancestral home, native land, country’: OE _o¯ðel_ , _e¯ðel_ , OS _o¯ðil_ , OHG _uodil_ , ON _o¯þal_ 

Whether the _a/o¯_ alternation in PGmc.[+] _aþal-/_[+] _o¯þal-_ reflects an application of inherited lengthened-grade ablaut ([+] _o¯þal-_ as that which belongs to the[+] _aþal-_ ), or was borrowed together with the Semitic words, cannot be decided because the vocalization of Nabataean _’sl’»_ ‘landed property’ is unknown. 

## 6. Conclusion and prospect 

I have tried to show in this paper that comparing unetymologized English words to Basque and Semitic can lead to fruitful new etymologies, etymologies which do not only explain individual words but throw light on the stratal, cultural relationships between English, or Germanic, on one hand and Basque and Semitic on the other. The etymologies proposed above, together with other evidence discussed elsewhere (cf. Vennemann 2000b for a recent survey), suggest a tree of genealogical descent for English as in (30). 

- (30) Genealogical tree for English[43] 

Atlantic Italic (Semitic) (Latin, Romance) fl fl 

Proto-Indo-European Æ West-Indo-European Æ Germanic Æ English (Pre-Germanic) (Anglo-Saxon) › › Old European Celtic Æ Insular Celtic (Vasconic) › Atlantic (Semitic) 

Many English words have undisputed Indo-European etymologies. But quite a number of English words do not. The _OED_ is a conservative dictionary whose authors were often content with Indo-European etymologies that some modern etymologists would consider of questionable value. Nevertheless it has quite a number of entries that are judged with the following predicates, where the noun 

_Key_ issues in English etymology 245 

may be qualified by various attributes, such as _exact/ulterior/ultimate_ (e.g. “ _key_ (…) ulterior etym. unknown”): 

- a. _origin/etymology/derivation/formation/source unknown/obscure/uncertain/doubtful_ : 1,471 entries 

- b. _of unknown/obscure/uncertain/doubtful origin/etymology/derivation/formation/source_ : 2,766 entries 

There are other characterizations too, such as the following, where the parenthesis may contain qualifications such as _the/its precise/immediate/proximate/ ulterior/ultimate_ : 

- c. (…) _origin/etymology/derivation/formation/source is unknown/obscure/uncertain/doubtful_ : 149 entries 

- d. The same characterizations as in (a) to (c) but in partially abbreviated form: 310 entries.[44] 

Thus it seems that at least 4,696 English words[45] are in need of a better etymology, or any etymology at all.[46] Even if some of these words have been given better etymologies since the _OED_ was composed, this may be more than compensated by the number of words whose recorded etymologies are no longer generally accepted. Needless to say, I am not suggesting that most or all of the 4,696 or more words singled out in the _OED_ are of Vasconic or Semitic origin. But it should also be evident that there is work to do,[47] and that it will be a long way until the goal of a complete etymology of the English language is reached. In this paper I hope to have pointed out new etymological paths leading to that goal, and to have taken a few steps in that direction.

1. A third source, non- _lautgesetzlich_ etymologies, i.e. etymologies not gained through the strict abidance by sound-laws in the Neogrammarian sense but through the application of phonological preference laws, is discussed and illustrated in Vennemann (2000a). 

2. Since both Basque and the Semitic languages have been very conservative over the last several thousand years, I could simply speak of Basque and Semitic influences, and in the case of Semitidic I often do.

246 Theo Vennemann 

4. Cf. Vennemann (1993:§4; 1994:§8.3; 1995:§7.24). Boutkan (forthc.) too has identified probable loan-words characterized by both, an irregular nominal ablaut (mostly _a/o¯_ ), cf. the preceding section, and non-matching plosive grades. He has not identified a source, or giving language. 

5. The question of what this parameter was phonetically, e.g. a kind of glottalicness, is unimportant for this paper.

8. The assumption of an identical root _plg_ for both loan-words,[+] _fulk-_ and[+] _plo¯g-_ , makes the argument simple. In actual fact, as is true of many Semitic roots, one has to reckon with an entire family of roots, or root forms, of the shape _plC_ (with various consonants _C_ ) and all meaning ‘to divide.’ Cf. §4.1 below. 

9. Only as a gloss of Lat. _pessulus_ ‘bolt’ (Epinal Gl. 803); cf. _OED_ :s.v. _hake_ . 

10. The _OED_ says about _hake_ : “Origin uncertain.” 

11. See now Boutkan (1999) on these fish-names. 

12. Whether E _hack_ , G _hacken_ etc. are related words is a matter of debate. 

13. Goth. _hoha_ is usually connected to an Indo-European word-family meaning ‘branch (of a tree)’. But if we consider that the original reference of the word is likely to have been to the primitive hook-plow (e.g. Jacob Grimm bases the Old High German diminutive _huohili_ on the same etymon as Goth. _hoha_ which he describes as a wooden hook-plow made from a curved branch, cf. Lehmann 1986:s.v. _hoha_ ), then connecting Goth. _hoha_ (and OHG _huohili_ ) to the ‘hook’ set is possible strictly within Germanic and therefore preferable. Such extensions of the fundamental ‘hook’ meaning to derived concepts are to be expected. As a matter of fact, we already saw an undisputed example of this process, ON _hœkja_ ‘crutch’. The reason why Goth. _hoha_ has been kept separate from the ‘hook’ set appears to be the implicit breach of _Lautgesetzlichkeit_ . But since the subset OHG _hac_ , _ha¯co_ , _ha¯ho_ , _ha¯go_ is not questioned, this offense has already been committed anyway — and tolerated. Therefore I propose that it be tolerated in the case of Goth. _hoha_ as well. 

14. This is not the end of the story, for there are other words similar in form and meaning to the above, and equally difficult for Indo-European etymological analysis. Southern G _kuechen_ , Standard G _Kufe_ (with dissimilatory /x/ > /f/), OHG _kuohho_ ‘skid, sledge-runner’, with MLG _koke_ ‘sledge-prow’, of unclear origin (Kluge/Seebold 1995:s.v. _Kufe_ ), appears to belong here. Schrader (1896:6,11) connects G _Kuchen_ , ON _kaka_ ( _k˛oku-_ ) ‘cake’ (Æ ME _cake_ , Finn. _kakko_ etc.), which are usually considered to have no etymology, to the preceding group (and thus, if my etymology is correct, indirectly to the _hook_ set). This is not implausible in view of the fact that cakes (and cookies!) come in various shapes (note in particular G _Hörnchen_ ‘crescent, croissant’, literally ‘little horn’) and that in Old High German the regular Latin gloss for _kuohho_ ‘cake’ is ‘torta’ (cf. E _tart_ ), which derives from Lat. _torque¯re_ which can mean both ‘to turn’ and ‘to twist,’ so that Schrader thought that cake was originally named for its curved shape. If the etymological connection between the _cake_ set and the _hook_ set is 

_Key_ issues in English etymology 247 

correct, or, more generally: if the _cake_ set is a Vasconic loan complex, then the _a/o¯_ root-vowel alternation between ON _kaka_ etc. and G _Kuchen_ (< West Gmc.[+] _ko¯ko¯n-_ , cf. also the Old English diminutive _ce¯cil_ , <[+] _ko¯k-il-_ ) need no longer be interpreted as Indo-European lengthened-grade ablaut (the root structure * _gag-_ would be in violation of Proto-IndoEuropean root-structure constraints anyway), but can be subsumed under the loan-word adaptation strategy in (8) above. A further example is the _key_ word treated in §3.2 below. It should be obvious that the _hook_ set would be a worthy subject for a monographic study. 

15. The form _kako_ occurs in High Navarrese (Baztan community), in Bizkaian and Gipuzkoan generally, and in Roncalese. The aspirated variant _khako_ belongs to Zuberoan.

Lipin´ski (1997:§28.2) writes about such Semitic root families C1C2C3 with variable C3: “Their number [that of biconsonantal roots] increases significantly if one accepts that only two of the three radicals of the triconsonantal roots are the main bearers of the meaning and that the third one had at one stage the task of a determinant or modifier in very much the same way as occurs with the vowels in the fully developed triconsonantal system. This is illustrated by the well-known example of the Hebrew verbs _prd_ , _prm_ , _prs_ , _prs»_ , _prq_ , _prr_ , _prš_ , etc., that have the radical _pr_ in common and express the basic notion of ‘dividing’”. This root family _prC_ is, for phonological and semantic reasons, further related to the root family _plC_ called upon above.

_Key_ issues in English etymology 249 

reflecting a genetic relationship between Semitic and Indo-European. He fails to explain in this way the limited distribution of _fallow/Falge_ . Since Möller’s aim was _lautgesetzlich_ to connect Semitic and Indo-European roots, he could not see a connection between the _plough_ word and the same set of Semitic roots (and thus between _plough_ and _fallow/Falge_ ). I have found and counted the following abbreviations: _origin unkn._ , _of unkn./uncert. origin_ , _orig. unknown_ , _of obsc. orig._ ; _etym./etymol. unkn./unknown/obscure/uncertain/doubtful_ , _of unknown/obscure/uncertain/doubtful etym./etymol._ , _etym. is unknown/obscure/uncertain/ doubtful_ ; _deriv. unknown/obscure/uncertain/doubtful_ , _of uncertain/doubtful deriv_ . There may be others, which would increase the above number. There are also other adjectives combining with the same nouns to express etymological uncertainty, e.g. 164 qualifications formed with _unascertained_ , which definitely increases the above figures. 

45. The qualification “at least” is not only needed for the reasons given in the preceding note but also because for many words their equally unascertained provenience is not characterized in such terms at all but has to be inferred from the fact that no satisfactory etymological connections are made. This is true, e.g., for _hook_ , _fallow_ , _folk_ , _sib_ , and _æðel-_ . The number of such words cannot be determined by simple search routines; it may be very large. Yet other words escape such a search because non-standardized characterizations are used; e.g. “ _plough_ (…) the origin of _plóŠ_ , _plóh_ , is involved in obscurity”. 

46. It may be doubted that the various characterizations noted above are a guide to the nature of the etymological problems posed by the words so characterized. My impression is that the combinations of _unknown/obscure/uncertain/doubtful_ and _origin/etymology/ derivation/formation/source_ are used more or less promiscuously in the _OED_ . Nevertheless, a clear preference can be observed in favor of the following phrases, in descending order of frequency: _of obscure origin_ 1,519, _origin unknown_ 476, _of uncertain origin_ 455, _of unknown origin_ 329, _origin obscure_ 324, _origin uncertain_ 274 and _of doubtful origin_ 169. All other phrases are each used less than a hundred times, beginning with _origin unascertained_ (98 times). 

47. Working along different lines, Anatoly Liberman too has accepted the challenge to identify and clarify what he calls the “dregs” of English etymology (cf. Liberman 1992). He approaches the common goal by producing a new etymological English dictionary (cf. Liberman 1994).

# The dialectology of ‘English’ north of the Humber, c. 1380–1500[*] 

## Keith Williamson 

University of Edinburgh 

## 1. Introduction 

This paper concerns the ‘English’ written during the late fourteenth and fifteenth centuries, in Scotland and the six Northern counties of England. By convention, the English of Northern England is termed _Northern Middle English_ , that of Scotland _Older Scots_ . However, the received view is that up to the fifteenth century these two language labels are used to distinguish geopolitically what is perceived to have been a common speech area, or _Sprachgebiet_ . James Murray (1873:29) considered the Germanic tongue used in Scotland in the late fourteenth and early fifteenth centuries as “simply the northern English, which was spoken from the Trent and Humber to the Moray Firth (…)”. Ritchie Girvan, discussing the language of John Barbour (c. 1325–1395), described it as “one common, as far as we can judge, to the whole of the North from Humber to Moray Firth” (Girvan 1939:xliii). Certainly, the variety of English in this area contrasted in major respects with the varieties used in the Midlands and the South of England. The fifteenth century, however, was a period of linguistic divergence within the Northumbrian _Sprachgebiet_ , both between Lowland Scotland and Northern England and within these respective areas. 

In England, the emerging ‘Standard’ variety was beginning to exert influence on the written language of the Northern counties, although locally produced texts still exhibit distinctive linguistic traits (see Benskin 1989). The advance of the Standard variety led to the general erosion and ultimate disuse of local varieties as a written medium. By contrast, the Anglian-derived vernacular in Scotland, as well as evolving distinctively in speech, was developing as the written medium for literature, law and administration within a separate state. The view that the Lowland tongue of Scotland was “an artificial, created 

254 Keith Williamson 

‘literarylanguage’(…)withanastonishingmeasureofuniformity”(HarveyWood 1979 [1933]:xxxi) has been shown to be mistaken (see especially Aitken 1971). 

TheextralinguistichistoryoftheNorthumbrian _Sprachgebiet_ mustbearupon the distribution of the linguistic systems that arose within it. But the focus in the present paper is on a set of systemic linguistic elements and their observed distribution in space and time as defined by their arrangement in two data-sets. 

The strong linguistic coherence of this area is not at issue, but I will propose that the story is more complex — and so more interesting — than the sketchy accounts of it given in the handbooks. To investigate more deeply it is necessary to move beyond the small amount of data that tends to be reworked, generally taken fromliterarytexts,withlittleornoregardtothelinguisticissuesintextualcopying. Below I suggest, with the aid of some illustrations, how the dialectology of the _Sprachgebiet_ between Humber and Moray can be further explored. 

## 2. Data sets 

The evidence I use here is drawn from two data-sets: (1) _A Linguistic Atlas of Late Mediaeval English_ ( _LALME_ ), or rather the computer data-base from which the published atlas was drawn; and (2) the Edinburgh Corpus of Older Scots (ECOS). The latter is being compiled with the aim of making a linguistic atlas of Older Scots. There are differences in content between the two data-sets. ECOS presently comprises entirely local documents — record books and charters of sundry kinds — while _LALME_ also encompasses a considerable number of literary texts. The _LALME_ data derive from an intentionally restricted set of items — the questionnaire that was used to collect linguistic forms from texts — while the ECOS comprises complete texts, diplomatically edited, with all words and morphemes lexico-grammatically tagged. The datasets have different ranges of linguistic material and their respective data have been gathered and treated under different circumstances, using different techniques. The diachronic and diatopic coverage of both sources is variable (see Section 6, below). Nevertheless, the ECOS project is founded on the methodological principles of _LALME_ , even if it has adapted and developed their application, and there are sufficient similarities between the two data-sets to permit some comparisons to be made. 

Since the aim is to make diatopic and diachronic comparison of the material in the two data-sets, I have used from _LALME_ only the _prima facie_ datable documentary material. Of course, this adds a further restriction to the 

The dialectology of ‘English’ north of the Humber, c. 1380–1500 

255 

investigation in that such texts are generally short and tend to draw on a more limited range of vocabulary than do literary texts. This must be kept in mind when assessing the incidence of features. 

## 3. Character and value of the sources 

When James Murray in his _Dialect of the Southern Counties of Scotland_ considered the linguistic characteristics of Scots and Northern English texts of the fourteenth and fifteenth centuries he used mainly literary materials (Murray 1873:29–76). The investigation here is founded on the local documents from the two data-sets partly because they are comparable in terms of style and register.[1] But, further, they offer an extensive body of evidence with respect to their diatopic and diachronic representation. Such material was simply not accessible to Murray in the way that it is to the investigator now. 

The dichotomy between local documents and literary texts is over-simple: the characteristics of different kinds of texts within these broad categories needs to be properly investigated. Nor is one class more important or more valuable than the other overall as linguistic evidence. Each type of text can inform us of some things that the other cannot. For us, the evidence of documents as _linguistic_ witnesses is important because they are usually datable and often can be localized. They provide the spatio-temporal framework for the corpus. 

## 4. The linguistic features to be examined 

The following features will be discussed.[2] 

1. Third person plural pronouns: they, their, them: ·-a(C)eÒ compared with ·-ai(C)(e, ay(C)(eÒ.[3] 

2. Infinitive markers: (a) at; (b) to; (c) til. 

3. if/gif conjunctions, introducing a conditional clause: (a) if compared with gif; (b) gif: ‘gif’ compared with ‘gef’. 

4. thir, this/these plural demonstrative adjectives and pronouns: (a) thir compared with this/these; (b) the distributions of ‘this’, ‘thes’, ‘these’ and ‘theise’ types. 

The items and their features will be familiar, but the perspective of analysis may be less so — that of historical dialectology. 

256 Keith Williamson 

We tend to view Middle English or Early Middle Scots as phases in the historical developments of these varieties. Of course, we recognize that there were processes of change within these phases — processes that can be summarized in such formulae as /e:/ > /i:/. We cannot, of course, observe such long past linguistic processes. However, a corpus containing dated and localized texts can allow us to take snapshots of diatopically arranged states in an ordered temporal sequence. By comparison of the states portrayed in the sequence of snapshots we may be able to infer information about the processes of change. Here snapshots are presented as maps and graphs, albeit the snapshots are rather coarse-grained and have been taken with long exposures. Of course, we must take great care not to over-interpret such representations of the linguistic phenomena. They may seem dangerously precise.[4] 

I am looking at the spelling evidence, which is the primary evidence of the texts. Interpretation of spelling in medieval vernacular texts is fraught with difficulties and the relationship of spelling to sound has to be assayed cautiously. The evidence of the spelling must be considered along with other evidence — from modern dialects, from older stages of the language and from reconstructive techniques. 

## 5. Diatopic perspective 

I have defined Northern Middle English as any local variety of English used in texts originating within the six northern counties of Northumberland, Cumberland, Durham, Westmorland, Lancashire and Yorkshire and recorded in the _LALME_ linguistic profiles (LPs).[5] 

For further comparison, I have included LPs derived from dated documents localized in the counties immediately to the south of the North as just defined, viz. Cheshire, Shropshire, Derbyshire, Nottinghamshire, Leicestershire and Lincolnshire. These I characterize as a North Midland set. 

## 6. Diachronic perspective 

I have divided the Northern Middle English and the Older Scots data-sets into two chronological subsets: (1) data from texts from 1380 to 1439 (period I); (2) data from texts from 1440 to 1499 (period II). The work on the first phase of the Older Scots atlas project covers the time-span 1380–1500 and 1440 is simply 

The dialectology of ‘English’ north of the Humber, c. 1380–1500 

257 

the mid-point.[6] Neither of the two data-sets has an even spread diachronically or diatopically. In particular, the Northern Middle English set is not so well represented in the earlier part of the period nor in the later part, especially after 1470. Diachronic comparison with the Northern Middle English material is attempted only within two broad time bands: before 1440 and the period from 1440 to 1499. Within the Older Scots data set a further diachronic division into four 30-year time-bands is used. 

Table 1. Number of data-sources (ECOS tagged texts and _LALME_ linguistic profiles) in each time-band 

|Time-bands|Older Scots|Time-bands|ECOS texts|Northern|North Mid-|

|1470–1499|117|||||



The diatopic study of the language of vernacular texts in the late Middle Ages is inevitably founded on an accidental sample — what has happened to survive. It should be remembered that when comparing two places over two periods, we cannot always expect to find witnesses from the same location (as a town or village or religious house) in each of the periods. Each locality on a map should be considered as the focal representative of an area of larger radius. 

## 7. Diatopic and diachronic description and analysis of the features 

- 7.1 Third person plural pronouns: they, their, them. ·-a(C)(eÒ compared with ·-ai(C)(eÒ / ·-ay(C)(eÒ spellings (i.e. ‘a’ type compared with ‘ai’ type) 

I consider here the spelling of the vowel in third person plural pronouns. These items (forms of which ultimately come into the Standard variety) are presumed to have had diphthongs in the Early Middle English period (all are derived from Old Norse, i.e. _þeir_ nom pl masc, _þeir(r)a_ gen masc/fem/neut, _þeim_ dat masc/fem/neut). 

258 Keith Williamson 

In Scots and Northern Middle English a merger of the phonemes /ai/ and /a:/ has been hypothesized. Luick (1921:§515) has merger taking place in the course of the fourteenth century. Murray (1873:53) too proposed merger (though not for South Scots) and linked it to the later use of ·iÒ in vowel spellings as a marker of length (i.e. ·ai,ayÒ, ·ei,eyÒ, ·oi,oyÒ). Later scholars have challenged this view, notably Kohler (1967). The development of ·aiÒ as a spelling for ESc /a:/, he attributes “primarily” to orthographic reasons — a matter of _figurae_ , not _potestate_ s (Kohler 1967:56). For recent detailed discussion of the history of ESc /ai/ and /a:/ see Johnston (1997:76–77). We may note here that they, them and their in the modern Scots dialects generally show monophthongized outcomes and merger with other items whose vowels derive from ESc /a:/.[7] 

I do not intend here to enter the wider issue of the origins of the ·i/yÒ digraph spellings for long vowels. I want to make the simple point that closer scrutiny of the evidence drawn from a large corpus can reveal patterns in the orthography which will need to be accounted for in the construction and evaluation of any future hypotheses. 

We find in the spelling of the vowel in the third person plural forms of the personal pronouns a trend through the fifteenth century where ·-ai(C)(eÒ spellings are changing to ·-a(C)(eÒ spellings. 

Period I (Map 1):[8] ‘thai’ type spellings predominate. ‘tha’ type is attested only in Scotland, with a few instances in the Forth–Clyde zone. In N England, the only variation is with ·eÒ forms which occur on the margins, in N Lancs and Cumbld and in the East Riding of Yorks (one location). 

Period II (Map 2): ‘tha’ in Scotland shows a very limited extension, in Angus and in the area around Ayr. In the South, in the Central zone and in the North-east (apart from instances in Aberdeen), ‘thai’ remains the dominant type. In N England, only the area around the Humber estuary (East Riding of Yorks and Lincs) shows ‘tha’, except for one locality in Westmld. 

To the South, in the N Midland area, ·eÒ spellings prevail, though ·eÒ forms occur in two northern locations, one in Westmld. 

Period I (Map 3): ‘thaim(e’ type predominates, but ‘tham(e’ type is apparent in N England (East Riding of Yorks, North Riding of Yorks, Northumbld and Cumbld). The Scottish texts show much less evidence of ·-am(eÒ with occurrences in only four locations — in Stirlingshire, Fife, Midlothian and Roxburghshire. 

Period II (Map 4): In N England, East Riding of Yorks and West Riding of Yorks have exclusively ·-am(eÒ spellings; North Riding of Yorks, Durham, Lancs

Accompanying the change from ·-air(eÒ to ·-ar(eÒ in their is an increasing tendency to write their with ´‘-Vr(-’ abbreviated. In Latin texts the literal strings ´‘ar’ and ´‘er’ were commonly abbreviated. The Latin conventions were adopted in the writing of the vernacular. Most professional writers probably had some knowledge of Latin. It would not, in either a Latin or a vernacular text, be usual to abbreviate the sequence ·airÒ. However, once it becomes possible to write their with ·arÒ, then applying the abbreviatory convention is not a problem.

What is clear from the graphs and the maps is that the rate of dissemination and the geographical spread of the change in the vowel spellings from ·aiÒ to ·aÒ is different for each word in the class of the third person plural pronouns. But is this evidence of a phonological change? Reflection in orthography of a phonological change can, I would argue, be plausibly inferred, i.e. a process of monophthongization in these words was being progressively reflected in the spellings through the fifteenth century, though not necessarily a merger into the /a:/ set.[11] 

It is worth comparing the orthographic pattern for there(-) where the vowel is ESc /a:/ < OE /æ:/. Table 3 shows the distribution of spellings for there(-) in ECOS texts over the four time-bands.

|th·_abbr_Ò|0.074|0.329|0.348|0.634|



seen in this period as a significant option for the spelling of the monophthongal vowel in there (< ESc /a:/). 

Even allowing that the change of spellings observed in this set of words in some measure reflects a phonological change, purely orthographic considerations may also be in play. For example, to what extent is the retention of the ‘ai’ type spelling of they a preference for retaining it in an open monosyllable? 

Observation of the spelling of other words with /ai/ and /a:/ etyma may throw more light on the extent of the shift in spelling in the lexis and on its diatopic spread and rate of change.

## 7.2 Infinitive markers: (a) at; (b) (for-)to; (c) til 

The Northern dialect area is characterized by three items which may serve to mark an infinitive: at < ON _at_ infinitive marker; to < OE _to¯_ ; til < ON _til_ (preposition + gen). Further, both to and til could combine with preceding for, to give for-to and for-til. These originally expressed purpose, but eventually came to be an alternative form simply marking the infinitive (Fischer 1992:317, 324). All are originally prepositions. In Old English _to¯_ preceded the inflected infinitive, which was historically nominal. 

In the ECOS and _LALME_ documents, both to and til are recorded. for-to/-til were only sporadically collected for _LALME_ and so their distributions are not available for that material. These variants are attested in the ECOS texts and have been separately tagged there. 

at as an infinitive marker is not a feature of Older Scots.[12] It does turn up in one ECOS document of 1442 relating to the disputed priorship of Coldingham priory, which, although in Scotland (Berwickshire), was a daughter house of Durham.[13] The text contains a copy of a document presented by one of the competitors — an Englishman — and the language of the copied portion is distinctively Middle English in character. 

268 Keith Williamson 

When at infinitive marker is considered in the corpus of datable Northern English documents, only three _LALME_ LPs attest it. Yet at is very well attested in the literary LPs which have been localized in the Northern counties (Map 7). Before considering possible reasons for this, to and til will be considered. 

**==> picture [104 x 53] intentionally omitted <==**

**----- Start of picture text -----**<br>
AT inWnitive marker<br>in all linguistic proWles<br>in “local documents”<br>**----- End of picture text -----**<br>


Map 7. at infinitive marker. Distributions in (1) all _LALME_ LPs, (2) LPs derived from northern “local documents” only. 

Period I (Map 8): In Scotland both to and til are used, with a number of texts making use of both forms. to not uncommonly occurs as the sole form, 

The dialectology of ‘English’ north of the Humber, c. 1380–1500 269 

**==> picture [65 x 59] intentionally omitted <==**

**----- Start of picture text -----**<br>
1380–1439<br>InWnitive markers<br>. TO<br>TIL<br>**----- End of picture text -----**<br>


Map 8. to and til infinitive markers. Distributions in Period I. 

til rarely so. In N England, til occurs only sporadically in the documents, in York, North Riding of Yorks, Durham and Cumbld.[14] 

Period II (Map 9): Scotland shows a very similar pattern to that of period I. til does not occur in the North-east outside Aberdeen, except in one place just north. It is found in the North in one text localized in Dingwall, Ross-shire. In N England, til is not recorded at all in the local documents. 

The evidence of the Northern English documents — or rather the lack of it — suggests at least that at and til were disfavoured in certain kinds of text. There would seem, in the local documents, to have been a rationalization of the 

270 Keith Williamson 

**==> picture [123 x 59] intentionally omitted <==**

**----- Start of picture text -----**<br>
1440–1499<br>+<br>o InWnitive markers<br>TO<br>°<br>e<br>TIL<br>**----- End of picture text -----**<br>


Map 9. to and til infinitive markers. Distributions in Period II. 

earlier multi-form system of infinitive marking. As a preposition, til is still found in some texts from Yorks, Durham and Cumbld. The currency of this is diminished in the later period, especially in Yorks. Again, the main form is to. 

The difference of occurrence between the literary LPs and the local document LPs in _LALME_ requires explanation. Michael Benskin in his introduction to Vol. 4, “County Dictionary”, observes: 

even though the language of the literary corpus is sufficiently differentiated to enable various _schematic_ reconstructions of the northern dialect pattern, none of these has so far been convincingly related to the testimony of the documents 

The dialectology of ‘English’ north of the Humber, c. 1380–1500 271 

and the few literary manuscripts of known local origins. (McIntosh, Samuels & Benskin 1986:4, x) 

There may be a combination of factors for the low incidence of at and til in the N English documents compared with the literary texts. This may have to do with the nature of the literary texts from which the _LALME_ linguistic profiles were drawn: a number are verse; they have an earlier date of authorial origin with older forms preserved in copies; there may be a relationship, in some texts, with oral tradition.[15] The use of English in legal writings — certainly in the North of England and in Scotland — was a fairly new development and the language of their manuscripts may be more contemporary with their date of composition than that of the literary manuscripts. 

The failure of at and til to show in the local documents may simply be because, as minority variants, they would have been much less likely to occur in a short text, such as a legal document, than in a longer literary text. That said, in the Scottish documents — texts of like character and length — til survives widely both as a preposition and an infinitive marker, albeit as a minority form in both cases.[16] 

A factor here may be in the training of lawyers. In fifteenth-century England, London was becoming the focus of legal education. Benskin (1989) summarizes the position: 

it was not until the fifteenth century that the Inns began to offer systematic legal education (…) They attracted students from all over the country, and increasingly gave young men from the provinces an experience of London language at a formative stage of their lives. By the late fifteenth century, over half of those trained in the Inns of Court made their careers in the provinces, and for the Inns of Chancery the proportion was higher still; and many of the Westminster lawyers themselves maintained large provincial practices in their home counties or elsewhere. (Benskin 1989:20–21) 

Linguistic influence on lawyers in the North of England must therefore be considered in a comparison of the documentary texts produced in the two realms. Scotland in the fifteenth century had no single centre for legal education and Scottish lawmen, in common with their compatriots, were beginning to find their own linguistic paths.[17] This might be one explanation for the apparent avoidance of the Northern infinitive markers at and til in legal writing, while til remained in use in Scotland. 

272 Keith Williamson 

## 7.3 if/gif conjunctions, introducing a conditional clause: (a) if compared with gif; (b) gif: ‘gif’ compared with ‘gef’ 

The _LALME_ maps show two principal types in England, if and Šif (< OE _g˙if_ ). The origin of if is not certain; Old Norse had _ef_ . _LALME_ shows Šif occurring over the most part of Midland and Southern England, in particular in the West Midlands, the East Midlands and across the South. 

In Scotland and in Northern England gif occurs. This is presumed to be from < OE _g˙if_ , with ·gÒ /g/ substituted for the original palatal fricative.

Map 10. if and gif conjunctions. Distributions in Period I. 

Period I (Map 10): if is general across the North, with only sporadic gif, one location in East Riding of Yorks, three locations in Lancs; gif is dominant in Scotland, with only occasional if in the south-east. 

Period II (Map 11): In N England, gif is a little more widely attested, though the instances are rather scattered. In Scotland, if is attested in only one location (in Berwickshire). 

In the Scots texts, however, a variant form of gif develops in the second half of the fifteenth century, ·gefÒ. This spelling seems to imply a lowering of the vowel.

## 7.4.1 _The distributions of ‘this’, ‘thes’, ‘these’ and ‘theise’ types_ 

Period I (Map 16): ‘this’ type appears in Yorks (East Riding and North) and once in Durham and in S Lancs. ‘thes’ type is found in Lancs mainly and in a small cluster in North Riding of Yorks. Forms with ·ei,eyÒ, which imply a long realization, occur in locations in Lancs and West Riding of Yorks, in one location nearby in North Riding and in one location in Cumbld. 

Period II (Map 17): ‘thes’ has a much wider currency across the North. ‘this’ type is now found only sporadically in N Lancs, Cumbld and Durham, with two further instances, one in S Yorks and one in N Lincs. 

Forms whose spellings imply a long vowel (·-ies(eÒ, ·-eseÒ and ·-ees(eÒ) occur predominantly in the West: Lancs and Westmld. This seems to be an extension of a NW Midland distribution: the forms are strongly attested in the documents of Chesh, Staffs and Shrops.

## 8. Conclusion 

The above comparative analysis of the data attempted to take account of variation both across space and through time. Albeit very limited in scope, it has revealed a more chromatic picture of the language of the Scots and Northern English texts considered here. The methodology adopted has served as a lens to refract the ‘white light’ of the text-books into colour. Discernible from the analysis of the data-sets are: 

The dialectology of ‘English’ north of the Humber, c. 1380–1500 279 

**==> picture [101 x 68] intentionally omitted <==**

**----- Start of picture text -----**<br>
1440–1499<br>THIR ‘thir’,‘ther’<br>THIS ‘this’,‘thes’<br>THESE ‘these’,‘theis(e’<br>**----- End of picture text -----**<br>


Map 15. thir, this and these (= ModE “these”) in Period II. 

- a. a shift in the spelling of the third person plural pronouns, to varying extents, which may have phonological import (this was observable more clearly in the Scots data); 

- b. marked contrast between the Northern English literary texts and documents in the use of at infinitive marker, where the writers of the Northern English vernacular documents seem to have favoured to, rejecting at and til from the outset as forms for the infinitive marker; in the Scottish documents (in which at does not feature), til maintains a significant minority presence through both periods;

- c. almost uniform use of gif in Scotland, where Northern England has mostly if (also in contrast with the Southern and Midlands Šif); 

- d. development in southern Scotland of a variant ‘gef’ in the latter part of the fifteenth century; 

- e. thir “these” still current in the border counties of England in the defined earlier period I, but recessive in the later period II; 

- f. in this, a shift from ‘this’ type as more widespread in period I to ‘thes’ in period II across the northern counties of England, with these (‘these’ and ‘theis(e’ types) confined largely to west of the Penines and to the NW Midlands in period II.

**==> picture [85 x 99] intentionally omitted <==**

**----- Start of picture text -----**<br>
