Map 17. this, thes and these (= ModE “these”) in Period II. 

Overall, the dialectal character of the North of England and Scotland in the later Middle Ages remains problematic. We need more data to explore further the occurrence of features and their diatopic and diachronic distributions. A _desideratum_ is that the more detailed methods of taxonomy and analysis being used for the Older Scots and Early Middle English atlas projects should be extended to the Northern English material. For Lowland Scotland, further detailed investigation using more texts, fully analysed linguistically, will enhance our knowledge and must lead to a revision of our ideas about the nature of Older Scots. 

282 Keith Williamson 

## Notes 

* I am very grateful to Derek Britton, Sandy Fenton and Anneli Meurman-Solin for taking trouble to read this paper and offer useful criticisms and, of course, corrections. My colleague Meg Laing has, as ever, tholed my prose well beyond the call of duty and I thank her especially for her careful readership. I also wish to thank the anonymous reviewer for proposing useful emendations and the editors for agreeing to publish this paper and for their efficiency and courtesy. I gratefully acknowledge financial support for the research project to which this paper contributes of The Leverhulme Trust (1994–99), the Carnegie Trust for the Universities of Scotland (1999–2000) and the Arts and Humanities Research Board (2000–). 

1. For further discussion of the nature and use of this material see _LALME_ vol. 1 (Introduction to “Index of Sources”). The use of local documents for the Older Scots atlas is outlined in Williamson (2001). 

2. For the purposes of description and comparison, three basic concepts are used in taxonomizing linguistic data drawn from texts — item, form and feature. An _item_ is a unit for linguistic comparison. It is a hyponym for a group of elements — _forms_ — which are functionally equivalent, as (1) ‘the same word’, e.g. _heo_ , _scho_ , _xe_ = ‘she’, or (2) as ‘the same morpheme’, e.g. _+eþ_ , _+ith_ , _+es_ , ‘3rd person singular indicative inflexion’, or (3) as ‘having the same basic meaning’, e.g. _til_ , _fort_ , _unto_ , _þat_ ‘until’. In (1) and (2), form is more or less equivalent to ‘the spelling adopted for the word or morpheme’; in (3) the forms may be different lexical items, each with distinctive forms. A linguistic _feature_ comprises a set of strings of one or more segments comprising all or part of the form(s) which realize one or more items: e.g. initial _x-_ in forms of ´‘she’; _-þ_ in third person singular indicative verb inflexions; _but_ -forms expressing the meaning ‘without’. 

3. In this paper, items are given in upper case; · Ò contain specific spellings; ‘ ’ signify sets of spellings or spelling types defined from the forms for an item. 

4. Observation of linguistic phenomena in this way can be likened to tracking an object travelling in distant space. We pick up signals from it which give a series of positions for it. We determine its path by linking the positions, on the assumption that it is the same object we are viewing on receipt of each signal. Likewise, we may have evidence of a linguistic feature occurring in a series of temporally distributed texts which we can, on the face of it, date and localize. However, even given information about the date and place of origin of a text, these co-ordinates really fall within a wider indeterminate area of space-time. This is especially so with time. In most cases we have no information about the age of the writer and so of the period in which his or her written linguistic habits were formed. We cannot usually tell if a text was written by someone at the beginning of his writing career, as I conjecture the maker of the Scone Gloss to have been (Williamson in preparation, _A Linguistic Study of the Scone Gloss_ ), or near the end, as (probably) Dan Michel of Northgate, the Canterbury author of the _AŠenbite of Inwyt_ . The writing habits may have been formed a generation before the text we are looking at; or the writing habits may be innovative, possibly even idiosyncratic. When the products of a number of writers are bundled, the degree of currency of a feature may be better observed. But we still have to allow that any locus in time and/or space established for a text has to be considered fuzzy. 

The dialectology of ‘English’ north of the Humber, c. 1380–1500 283 

5. Within England a major linguistic divide runs roughly southeastwards from N Lancs to South of the Humber estuary into N Lincs. This divide is defined by the northern and southern limits of distribution of a number of linguistic features. It marks an area of transition between Northern and Midland Middle English. And, on the evidence of a number of maps in _LALME_ , the transition seems fairly sharp. Traditional dialectology would view this transition in terms of a ‘bundle of isoglosses’. One must, however, distinguish between the southern limits (however defined) of northern features and the northern limits of features predominantly attested in the southern and midland regions: we are still dealing with a dialect continuum. 

6. 1499 becomes the end-point purely to serve arithmetic. 

7. The Linguistic Survey of Scotland phonological survey collected information about the personal and demonstrative pronouns, but this material was not published in vol. 3 of the _Linguistic Atlas of Scotland_ (Mather & Speitel 1986). their occurs in Section 3 of the questionnaire, as does there (ESc /a:/). Except in the following locations both these words are recorded under the same phoneme: Caithness 3.1 Stroma, 3.4a Thurso, Fisher-Biggings, 3.4b Thurso, Up-Town, 3.5 Watten, 3.8 Pultneytown; Sutherland 4.3 Embo; Ross-shire 5.3 Culbokie; Nairn 6.1b Nairn, Up-Town; Banff 8.6 Upper Cabrach; Angus 12.1 Migvie, 12.3 Brechin, 12.5 Kirriemuir; Perthshire 13.3 Fowlis Wester; Clackmannanshire 15.1 Tillicoultry; Midlothian Stow; Roxburghshire 24.9 Newcastleton; Dumfriesshire 25.5 Westerkirk, 25.6 Hightae, 25.7 Canonbie, 25.8 Gretna Green, 25.9 Creca. Only 3.8 Pultneytown gives a diphthong as the reflex. 

8. In all the maps, survey points for which no occurrence of an item is attested in either of the periods are represented by small unfilled circles. 

9. When the _LALME_ data were entered on disk the frequencies of occurrence of forms in the LPs were not recorded; they are formatted using the same system of marking relative proportions by bracketing as appears in the published atlas (McIntosh, Samuels & Benskin 1986:3, xiv). In the corpora for both the ECOS and the Linguistic Atlas of Early Middle English projects frequencies of occurrence of any data element are readily calculated and so may be used for numerical, including statistical, analysis. 

10. The scale on the y-axis of the graphs in Figures 1 through 3 measures the mean relative proportion of occurrence of the features in each time-band. 

11. The details of the histories in Scotland of ESc /ai/ and /a:/ in terms of the putative merger and the dating of it remains problematic. For details see Luick (1921:§434), Jordan (1925:§132, 123–124), Girvan (1939:lvii–lx), Kohler (1967), Aitken (1977) and Johnston (1997). Aitken (1977:8) dates the merger much later than Luick or Girvan, to the early sixteenth century. He observes that the fifteenth-century poets Robert Henryson and Blind Hary “carefully avoid” rhyming words whose vowels derive from ESc /ai/ and ESc /a:/ “except before /r/” while William Dunbar and Gavin Douglas (fl. late 15th, early 16th century) “do occasionally rhyme the two items”. Johnston (1997:76), while accepting Aitken’s rhyme evidence, also points to fifteenth-century ·aCeÒ spellings for words whose vowels derive from ESc /ai/ suggesting “an earlier date, at least in certain positions”; which are: final, before /r/ and before /n/. 

284 Keith Williamson 

The issue is complex and it is beyond the scope of this paper to tackle it. Nevertheless, the small amount of evidence presented here, based on the spellings of they, them and their, lends support to Johnston’s view. Rather than confusion of spelling in these words, a diffusion through time and across space in the spellings of each of these words is indicated, implying, _post hoc_ , a phonological change. The change reflected in the orthography would be monophthongization, if not merger. There is also the issue of the raising of ESc /a:/ to consider. 

12. The form _at_ occurs in Scots texts as a relative pronoun and a conjunction as well as a preposition. 

13. Durham, Dean & Chapter Muniments, Misc. Charter 1439, dated 10 January 1442. 

14. See _LALME_ dotmap 622 for the full distribution of til in all mapped linguistic profiles (McIntosh, Samuels & Benskin 1986:1, 462). Samuels (1989:110) considers the distribution of at to be characteristic of the area of “primary” Scandinavian settlement, while til in its extended function of infinitive marker is distributionally peripheral to the “core” area. 

15. at infinitive marker survived in speech well into the modern period (see Wright 1898, s.v. _at_ prep. I, “Used instead of _to_ as the sign of the infinitive”; though he marks it as “ _Obsol_ [escent]”). It is recorded in the _Survey of English Dialects_ , under a response to question IX.5.9 at La[ncashire] 2 (in N Lancs), as part of incidental material (Orton & Halliday 1963:I.iii, 1045). 

16. The following sets of figures compare the incidence in ECOS texts of to and til as infinitive marker and preposition — the figures give the mean average occurrence in each text within the time-bands of to and til: 

- a. to infinitive marker/til infinitive marker — _1380–1409_ , 0.917/0.083; _1410–1439_ , 0.871/ 0.129; _1440–1469_ , 0.921/0.079; _1470–1499_ , 0.889/0.110

17. A legal education in fifteenth-century Scotland might be found at one of the two Scottish universities (St Andrews, founded 1411 or Glasgow, 1452), in a religious house or at one of the Continental law schools such as Paris or Orleans. See Macfarlane (1995) on the education of Scottish lawyers in the fifteenth century. 

18. Dobson (1947/48), Macafee (1992/93) and King (1997:168) all observe that the origins of _thir_ are “obscure”. Various hypotheses have been advanced. Dobson (1947/48) and Bazell (1962) both ascribe its origins to analogy. Dobson proposed _this_ ~ _thir_ was modelled on _his_ ~ _hire_ . Bazell found his analogy in the _s_ ~ _r_ alternations in _es_ ~ _ere_ and _was_ ~ _were_ , though a requirement to make this “convincing” was that “the vowel quantities in _es ere_ : _this there_ must correspond” (Bazell 1962:59). Bazell too argues from geography: “It is really too striking a coincidence that one should find _this thire_ in only those regions in which there was a verbal alternation _s_ – _r_ in forms otherwise identical except perhaps for vowel quantity” ( _ibid._ ). However, as Bazell admits, there are no parallels to support either his or Dobson’s theory. Macafee (1992/93) offers no explanation. King proposes that “a very likely source of the plural demonstrative _thir_ in the north would seem to be Old Norse _þeir_ or _þeir(r)a_ (the forms which provided the English third person personal pronouns _they_ and _their”_ (King 1997:169). 

The dialectology of ‘English’ north of the Humber, c. 1380–1500 285 

She notes that in Old Norse the third person plural pronouns functioned also as plural demonstratives = ‘those’, so nom masc pl _þeir_ , gen masc/fem/neut pl _þeir(r)a_ . There are two major problems with this analysis: (1) the deictic reference of ON _þeir_ is ‘distance from the speaker’ while that of thir is ‘proximity to the speaker’; (2) ON /ei/ usually corresponds to ESc and NME /ai/. 

Old English dialects did have variants with /r/ in both the that and the this paradigms (gen pl of that, _þa¯ra/þ ¯æra_ ). Of possible significance is that in Old Northumbrian “analogical forms with _r_ appear in g.d.s.f (…) g.p. _ðisra_ ” (Campbell 1959:292). The frequency of these OE _r_ forms and the phonological plausibility of them as a possible source remain to be explored. 

For this and thes “these”: cf. OE gen pl _þissa_ , dat pl _þissum_ “these”; ON plural with stem _þess-_ (also in sg except for acc masc and nom and acc neut).

## **CURRENT ISSUES IN LINGUISTIC THEORY** 

E. F. K. Koerner, Editor Department of Linguistics, University of Ottawa OTTAWA, Canada K1N 6N5 koerner@uottawa.ca 

The _Current Issues in Linguistic Theory_ (CILT) series is a theory-oriented series which welcomes contributions from scholars who have significant proposals to make towards the advancement of our understanding of language, its structure, functioning and development. CILT has been established in order to provide a forum for the presentation and discussion of linguistic opinions of scholars who do not necessarily accept the prevailing mode of thought in linguistic science. It offers an alternative outlet for meaningful contributions to the current linguistic debate, and furnishes the diversity of opinion which a healthy discipline must have. In this series the following volumes have been published thus far or are scheduled for publication: 

1. KOERNER, Konrad (ed.): _The Transformational-Generative Paradigm and Modern Linguistic Theory._ 1975. 

2. WEIDERT, Alfons: _Componential Analysis of Lushai Phonology._ 1975. 

3. MAHER, J. Peter: _Papers on Language Theory and History I: Creation and Tradition in Language. Foreword by Raimo Anttila._ 1979. 

4. HOPPER, Paul J. (ed.): _Studies in Descriptive and Historical Linguistics. Festschrift for Winfred P. Lehmann._ 1977. 

5. ITKONEN, Esa: _Grammatical Theory and Metascience: A critical investigation into the methodological and philosophical foundations of ‘autonomous’ linguistics._ 1978. 

6. ANTTILA, Raimo: _Historical and Comparative Linguistics._ 1989. 

7. MEISEL, Jürgen M. & Martin D. PAM (eds): _Linear Order and Generative Theory._ 1979. 

8. WILBUR, Terence H.: _Prolegomena to a Grammar of Basque._ 1979. 

9. HOLLIEN, Harry & Patricia (eds): _Current Issues in the Phonetic Sciences. Proceedings of the IPS-77 Congress, Miami Beach, Florida, 17-19 December 1977._ 1979. 

10. PRIDEAUX, Gary D. (ed.): _Perspectives in Experimental Linguistics. Papers from the University of Alberta Conference on Experimental Linguistics, Edmonton, 13-14 Oct. 1978._ 1979.
